Heaven is a Brane
                                      Illustration 13. Various Spheres of Reality




       Various Christian writers use multiverse to explain such things as the UFO phenomena. Putnam and Horn
use the braneworld variation. In brane cosmology, brane stands for membrane. Putnam and Horn cite scientific
opinion that the assumed dark matter and energy within our universe is the gravitational force of adjacent banes, so
there is no dark matter or dark energy in our universe.65 I am not necessarily inclined to accept this argument as
proof of a multiverse because Hartnett’s 5D cosmological model accounts for all so-called dark matter and energy.
One may not need to venture outside of our brane to explain the phenomena attributed to dark matter. Nevertheless,
for theological reasons, the multiverse hypothesis is adopted herein. As Horn and Putnam correctly deduce: “If one
can believe in heaven and hell, then one should be able to believe in other universes.”66 Various spheres of reality
have been lined up in the illustration. Obviously, they are not drawn to scale. Since Paradise was removed from
Hades to Heaven when Jesus rose from the dead, Paradise has been removed from Hades in the illustration. Our
universe is in the center of the lineup of 3D universes. Our universe is labeled cosmos since the Greeks used the
word cosmos to refer to our observable universe.67
                                      Illustration 14. 3D Spheres in Braneworld




       One way commonly used to describe braneworld cosmology is to think of parallel universes as slices of
bread. In that case, as I picture it, Heaven would be the membrane composing the plastic bread wrapper containing
the bread within the loaf. This heavenly wrapper is made up of a different material than the 3D bread inside of the
loaf. In M-theory, membranes can run anywhere from 1 to 11 dimensions.68 Some might add another time
dimension. In that case, Heaven is a 12-brane (i.e., 12D) universe, while our universe is only a 3-brane (i.e., 3D)
space. The 3D universes inside the loaf need not be thought of as being identical. You might have slices of whole
grain, multigrain, enriched, etc, so to speak. But all the slices are bread, not plastic. Another way to picture

space, thus a multiverse.

                           Illustration 16. Stacks of Metal Coins and Brane Universes




       Most people have probably seen flattened pennies. Imagine something similar, where spherical metal balls
have been flattened into coinage disks that can be stacked on top of one another. In multiverse theory, the same
thing is done with universes: 3D universes are flattened out into 2D disks that can be stacked on top of one another.
Sometimes these disks are represented with space in between them to represent hyperspace. These universes need
not all be the same size.

Moving Branes Out of the Kingdom of Heaven
       The biblical evidence leads to the deduction that branes can be moved from one location to another.
Following through on the observations inherent from such a deduction might assist with answering the thorny
question as to how unbelievers can be removed from the kingdom of Heaven. If the kingdom of Heaven entails the
spatial domain encompassed by the heavenly sphere, then it would include the whole multiverse—the heavenly
bread wrapper and all the bread inside of it, so to speak.
                                           Illustration 17. 3D to 2D Hades




       One advantage of the 2D representation is that it allows us to picture what the Bible tells us is going to happen
to Hades in spatial terms. According to Rev 20:14, Hades will be thrown into the Lake of Fire. But how will this
take place spatially? How will Hades be removed from Earth, taken through our galaxy, moved through Heaven,
and finally deposited into the Lake of Fire?69 How will Hades ascend through the three heavens in order to reach
the Lake of Fire? The simplest answer is that it does not make this trip through these spaces, as such, but through
hyperspace, as is consistent with Illustration 2. 4D Teleportation of Galaxies. This answer is easy to pose regarding
Earth and our universe, as these are only 3D realities in the illustration. A 3D space could move easily through a
fourth dimension. Hades is a parallel dimension contained within these other spatial realities. Yet posing that Hades
can pass through the heavenly sphere in the same way as it basically bypasses passing through these 3D spheres is
difficult because both Hades and Heaven are 12D. A 12D prison would be needed, for example, to confine beings
capable of moving in 12D.




69
   Both Earth and our universe (which contains Earth) have been included in the illustrations for the sake of picturing

it pictures a parallel dimension to Hades. Earth is the specific part of the parallel dimension of our universe that
houses Hades.

Heaven by some other means than jumping through the hyperspace that encapsulates Heaven. An opening would

portal (pictured as a slit) has been opened through the heavenly membrane to allow the passage of Hades from the
hyperdimensional space inside the box to the hyperdimensional space outside the box where the Lake of Fire is
located.

reasonable, then surely the projection offered herein that galaxies can be moved from one universe to another is
within reason.
                                Illustration 21. Moving a Galaxy to Another Brane




       This brane illustration is just another way of picturing the teleportation depicted earlier in Illustration 2. 4D

scale; otherwise, they would be far too small to be seen. All these universes are within the spherical realm of Heaven.
Spatially, they are inside the kingdom of Heaven. Our rulership of the heavenly realm will entail the rulership of
these universes. Moving an entire galaxy seems to be a feasible way of moving a highly populated area to an
unpopulated area within the kingdom of Heaven.
Chapter 1. New Jerusalem                                                                                     Page 31
Heaven and Earth will Pass Away
       The Bible informs us of both the creation and destruction of the heavens and the Earth: “In the beginning
God created the heavens and the earth” (Gen 1:1). The “lights in the expanse of the heavens” were “to give light on
the earth” (Gen 1:15). These heavens would correspond to the visible universe, thus the first and second heavens.
The third heaven does not give light to the Earth. Thus, the third heaven is Heaven proper. The goal for the tower
of Babel was to reach into Heaven: “Come, let us build for ourselves a city, and a tower whose top will reach into
heaven” (Gen 11:4). Most likely, with Nephilim-enhanced eyesight, Nimrod was seeking to build a tower that would
reach an interdimensional portal for the purpose of reaching the second and/or third heaven.70 With God-enhanced
vision, Jacob saw angels ascending and descending an interdimensional ramp between Earth and the third heaven,
described by many translations as a ladder in Gen 28:12, though stairway would be a better translation (GWN,
NAB, NET, NLT, TNK).
       Lucifer fell from Heaven to Earth (Is 14:12). Referring to Luciferian forces, Isaiah says that one day “the
Lord will punish the host of heaven” (Is 24:21). He will “create a new heavens and a new earth” (Is 65:17), which
will endure (Is 66:22). During the Day of the Lord, the first and second heavens will be darkened (Jer 4:23,28). The
Lord will shake the “heavens and the earth” (Hag 2:6,21). The writer of Hebrews explains that the purpose of this
shaking is to remove those things from them that will not be eternally within them (Heb 12:26-27). Lucifer and his
forces will be forcefully removed from Heaven to Earth (Rev 12:4).
                                Illustration 22. Rolled up like a Scroll and Burned




        In Mt 5:18, Jesus alluded to the passing away of Heaven and Earth. In Mt 24:35, He explicitly says, “Heaven
and earth will pass away.” Matthew records both statements. Like the other apostles, he is very much aware that
this planet and this universe will pass away. Peter is emphatic: “The present heavens and earth by His word are
being reserved for fire” (2Pet 3:7). “The day of the Lord will come like a thief, in which the heavens will pass away
with a roar and the elements will be destroyed with intense heat, and the earth and its works will be burned up”
(2Pet 3:10). “The heavens will be destroyed by burning, and the elements will melt with intense heat!” (2Pet 3:12)
This burning makes way for a “new heavens and a new earth, in which righteousness dwells” (2Pet 3:13; cp. Rev
21:4). According to Isaiah, “the heavens shall be rolled up like a scroll” (Is 34:4; cp. Rev 6:14). One way to put all
this data together into a working hypothesis is to pose the 3D universe we presently live in will be rolled up like a
scroll in 4D space and burned. Some mistakenly equate this with what scientists call “heat death.”71

70
   Horn, Zenith, 205-209. Putnam and Horn, Exo-Vaticana, 30-34. As Nimrod became more and more of a jackass,
both literally and morally, he stubbornly built a tower to the stargate he could now see with his opened eyes.
71
   When scientists talk about heat death, they are talking about the second law of thermodynamics, the law of
entropy, in which less and less energy is available for useful work.

                                       Illustration 23. One Brane Destroyed




       The third heaven is not to be destroyed by fire. Rather, it is merely to be shaken. Those things within it that
are not to remain there permanently are to be removed, such as the Luciferian forces. Burning Heaven is apparently
unnecessary. There is no death in Heaven, no gravestones are found there. The death, destruction, and entropy
introduced into our universe, however, will require that our brane be thoroughly renewed, born out of the flames
like a phoenix. Because the other universes are a part of our reward, because our reward has been prepared already,
because our rewards are eternal, and because the death introduced into our universe has not necessarily marred these
other universes, the working hypothesis taken herein is that fire does not destroy these other 3D branes. Only our
universe is dealt with in this way. No graves exist in these other universes, though I will suppose that death in the
form of the food chain exists in these other universes. For example, there are no Rabbit Planets, planets to where
the ever-expanding rabbit population is teleported. Instead, rabbits are part of the natural food chain on the planets
where they are found. Yet there are no rabbit graves. The form of death intended as part of the natural food chain
does not constitute a form of death necessitating a renewal of that brane. The death of man, on the other hand, was
never intended to be a part of the food chain. Therefore, his death runs counter to the divine plan, as ideally
conceived, and thus must be eliminated to restore the divine order. Animals do not make gravestones, only man
does. Only his death runs counter to the divine plan, requiring a reboot of the system wherein his death has marred
the beauty of God’s creation.
       For animal lovers, though, a word of consultation appears to be in order. Pets are an exception to the above
rule. Humans have made graves for their pets. Some animals have been accorded a burial that is out of accord with
their being erased from memory. Conditional immortality might be extended to such animals. If an overcomer wants
to have his or her pet back in Heaven, why would such a request not be granted? Their graves will no longer be in
the new state, even if they were given graves on Earth by their owners originally. Nevertheless, as Jesus called forth
His friend Lazarus from his grave by name, possibly overcomers will be able to call forth their pets by name, if they
have named them, from the old creation to the new one, resulting in them being recreated.72
                                 Moving People Out of the Kingdom of Heaven
      The movement of Hades, inversely, pictures the removal of unbelievers from the kingdom of Heaven to
outside the kingdom of Heaven, to the outside of the heavenly bread wrapper, so to speak. Hades is in the kingdom
of Heaven in the spatial sense of being a dungeon basement, but it will be cast into the Lake of Fire outside of this
heavenly realm. This realization may shed light on a related theme found in several passages of Scripture where

unbelievers are taken out of the kingdom since this is exactly what happens in the migration of Hades. Jesus’
statement, for example, in Mt 13:41 about the fate of the tares is very enigmatic: “The Son of Man will send forth
His angels, and they will gather out of His kingdom all stumbling blocks, and those who commit lawlessness.”

in the kingdom soteriologically.
Tares
       Chitwood argues that the tares are believers.73 Such a position would be in accord with Arminian theology.
After all, these stumbling blocks would have to be in the kingdom in order to be taken out of it, and the only way
to enter the kingdom is to be born again. One can easily see how some would conclude that these are born-again
stumbling blocks. But what is the nature of the burning to which they are submitted when they are cast into the
furnace of fire in Mt 13:42? The impression that Chitwood gives in Mysteries is that the furnace of fire in
Mt 13:42,50 pictures the Lake of Fire. Unfaithful believers would be cast there for 1000 years, which is normally a
kingdom exclusion position, except that Chitwood says that they are only metaphorically cast into the furnace; in
actuality, they stay in the kingdom.74
       Dillow and I, in contrast, do not hold such kingdom exclusion positions.75 We both would take the passage
as describing a soteriological fate for unregenerate tares. Yet Dillow rejects the proposal that the tares are currently
in the kingdom, asserting that the future millennial kingdom is exclusively in view. However, his rationale is weak
in that the passage appears to be describing a present sowing of the tares. Plus, his explanation still fails to explain
how they are taken out of the kingdom. How are they taken out of something they are never allowed to enter? Even
Dillow seems to retreat from saying that they do not enter the kingdom, in any sense, to saying that they do not

   Chitwood, Mysteries, 53-55.

a picture of being cast into the garbage dump outside the city, which in other passages is termed the outer darkness.
The Lake of Fire, though literal for unbelievers, only has metaphorical applicability to unfaithful believers. As
explained in Darkness, I would prefer to call this a correlative rather than a metaphorical applicability. In any event,
neither my soteriology nor my misthology would prevent me from adopting some such exclusively misthological
interpretation of the tares. I simply do not find such an exclusively misthological position hermeneutically
persuasive. In Darkness ch. 18, an exclusively soteriological approach was preferred, but earlier in Darkness, the
possibility of a composite antithetical interpretation was entertained. In the present discussion, this preference is
reversed. The antithetical interpretation is regarded as more probable than a soteriological adaptation. In short,
Chitwood sees the parable of the tares as yellow; Darkness preferred blue but allowed green; herein the preference
is for green (as a combination of blue and yellow). Thus, my neoclassical approach to the tares is a mediation
between Chitwood’s approach and my former preference.

   Though not pursued in the present book, a kingdom exclusion model worthy of investigation has been posed by
Hollandsworth (Keys, 104-105). He properly commends Charles Stanley for placing the outer darkness inside the
kingdom of God but outside the sphere of rulership. Hollandsworth wants to take this a step further and take the
kingdom of God as a comprehensive term, including both the heavenly and earthly realms of the kingdom.
Unfaithful believers are not excluded from the earthly domain of the kingdom of God; rather, they are excluded
from the heavenly realm of the kingdom of God, namely, the kingdom of Heaven, which is the focus of the Gospel
of Matthew. Therefore, Hollandsworth would pose that unfaithful believers are excluded from the kingdom of
Heaven, not from the kingdom of God. Specifically, he believes “faithful saints will be in the heavenly New
Jerusalem which likely hovers over Earth, whereas unfaithful saints will be in the darkness outside, which I
[Hollandsworth] understand to be the earthly realm.” Personal correspondence, 11/16/2017. Like Hollandsworth, I
placed the outer darkness on Earth in TOD, at least initially. However, as a competing model, in the present series
I am now entertaining the possibility of an inverse model in which the outer darkness is in the third heaven and
unfaithful believers are excluded from Earth and the first and second heavens. Either way, a case can be made for
spatial kingdom exclusion from the kingdom of Heaven if it refers to the misthological part of the kingdom of God.
This requires making the kingdom of Heaven a misthological subset of the more comprehensive term kingdom of
God. See James S. Hollandsworth, Keys for Inheriting the Kingdom: Unlocking the Parables of Jesus (USA:
HollyPublishing, 2017).

enter the kingdom in a soteriological sense (Destiny, 264-265). If that is the case, then the tares are (or will be)
within the kingdom in some sense other than a soteriological sense. In that case, why not allow that they are in the
kingdom in a mystery sense?
       Like Chitwood (and to some extent Dillow), Crawford rejects the mystery-form-of-the-kingdom
hypothesis.76 At least like Dillow, in relation to the parable of the wheat and the tare, Crawford holds to a future
76
   After denying at length that there is a present mystery form of the kingdom, Chitwood seems to balk, though, and
admits that there are “two forms of the same kingdom—both present and future” (Mysteries, 4; emphasis his). His
inconsistency surfaces again when regarding Mt 22:10-13 he says, “Those improperly dressed will be denied
entrance into these festivities and be left in the darkness outside, with nothing to look forward to during the Son’s
coming reign, for they will occupy no place in His kingdom” (p. 154; italics his). Yet he clarifies later, “All
Christians, faithful and unfaithful alike, will be in the kingdom” (p. 170). Evidently, we are to understand Chitwood
as saying that they will be in the kingdom but have no place (i.e., no regal position, no misthological portion) in the
kingdom, not that they will be nowhere in the realm of the kingdom. They will have a spatial place but not a regal
place. They will be in the kingdom but not in the kingship. If so, he is using the word kingdom in at least two
different senses. That explanation would reconcile his somewhat confusing statement. He misrepresents the text,
nevertheless, when he tries to make the text merely refer to exclusion. Such believers are not merely left outside;
they are cast outside—after first being inside. Just as the tares are gathered out (ek) of the field after first being in
it (Mt 13:41), such believers are first in the wedding feast before being cast out (ek) of it (Mt 22:13; ekballo). The
field is not the feast. But they have to be in the field/feast before they can be extracted from it. Extraction, not mere
exclusion, is envisioned. Exclusion from the future field/feast is preceded by extraction from the present-future
field/feast.
       In Destiny Dillow seems to be mildly inconsistent when, in reference to himself, he says, “The present writer,
a premillennialist, would identify these stages as a present mystery form of the kingdom, followed by the
millennial kingdom (the kingdom of Christ), and the future kingdom of God (the eternal state)” (115, n. 382).
“Because of Israel’s rejection, the final form of that kingdom, the millennium, was postponed until the second
advent but was inaugurated in a mystery form in the present age” (158, n. 533). Thus, like myself, he affirms
that the present mystery form of the kingdom has been inaugurated. And, like myself, he rejects the notion that
it has been inaugurated in the progressive sense: “We do not believe that the kingdom of heaven has been
inaugurated as progressive dispensationalists teach. Jesus is not on David’s throne today. But there is no reason to
deny that certain aspects of this future kingdom are being experienced today” (p. 249). The kingdom is inaugurated
in one sense but not another:

             Dispensationalists have correctly noted that the fact that certain aspects of the future millennial
      kingdom are being experienced today in no way justifies the teaching that the kingdom reign of David’s
      Greater Son has been inaugurated and we are in it now. When contemporary evangelical scholars use
      phrases such as “already” and “not yet,” it is confusing and incorrect. It is true that some of the blessings
      of the future millennium are already available. But that is not all that these scholars mean. They also
      believe that the future reign of the Messiah has already begun as well, making the preposterous claim
      that the kingdom has been inaugurated. How can one accept this in view of the Nazi holocaust?
      (Destiny, 250).

When already is used in that progressive sense, I would agree with Dillow that it is not present in that sense.
Yet he holds that both the kingdom and New Covenant are experienced now:

            While the present writer is a classical dispensationalist in the Pentecost-Ryrie-Walvoord-
      McClain mold, he would prefer to believe that even though the kingdom has not been inaugurated (there
      is no “already but not yet”), some aspects of that future kingdom are being experienced now, for
      example, the New Covenant. (Destiny, 954, n. 3082).

He repeatedly affirms that the New Covenant has been inaugurated (pp. 355, 370), while denying that the
kingdom has been inaugurated in any already-not-yet sense. But if the kingdom is inaugurated in the mystery
sense, then is it not already in that sense? Therefore, I would suggest that the clarification be added that the
mystery form of the kingdom is already present in some sense, but not the progressive sense, given that
Chapter 1. New Jerusalem                                                                                      Page 35
view of the kingdom and the belief that the tares are unregenerate. When I inquired as to how the tares are taken
out of the kingdom if they cannot enter it, Crawford posed that they are tares within the millennial kingdom and
thus taken out of it at the conclusion of the millennial kingdom. This position has the advantage of affirming that
the tares are literally in the kingdom and literally taken out of it. The two-fold problem with this proposal, however,
is (1) a present sowing seems to be entailed, and (2) Satan will not be sowing tares during the millennial kingdom,
as he will be bound during that time. I suggested that Crawford amend his position by allowing a present sowing.
He was amenable to that recommendation. A present sowing in the Earth will be followed by a final soteriological
extraction at the end of the millennial period of all unregenerate tares from the millennial kingdom to the Lake of
Fire. Still, a weakness with this modified position is that the millennial kingdom would seem to be free of tares and
leaven during the millennium. The present situation, as described in these parables, does not seem to befit the
millennial kingdom. On the other hand, postponing the extraction until the end of the millennial kingdom, as
Crawford does, is certainly an extremely attractive attribute of his model. In further refinement of that model, I
would propose that the Crawfordian model be further revised so that the extraction from the kingdom is not from
the millennial kingdom per se but from the spatial sphere of the kingdom of Heaven. See Illustration 20. Hades
Taken Out of the Kingdom of Heaven. For those who find it necessary to reject the mystery form of the kingdom
hypothesis, this model may be preferred. Personally, I am inclined to adopt both perspectives.

                                              Sons of the Wicked One
       More recently, Hollandsworth has written an intriguing book (Keys) advocating an exclusively misthological
model for the parables that is not crippled by some of the extremism taken by Chitwood. Indeed, Hollandsworth
believes that the Synoptic Gospels are exclusively misthological, while the Gospel of John is soteriologically
oriented. His allowing the soteriological nature of the latter makes his defense for the former more palatable than
that of those who, like Chitwood, try to take an exclusively misthological orientation for all four gospels. In any
event, Hollandsworth makes an attractive argument that the tares are mature believers (huios) who oppose the
kingdom teaching of rewards. Certainly, mature believers opposing the kingdom could be described functionally as
sons of the devil. As I observed in TOD, in relation to 1Jn 3:8, to be of the devil does not mean to be born of the
devil (of the devil ≠ born of the devil). Anyone, lost or saved, who sins is of the devil in nature and action. And
regarding 1Jn 3:10, anyone, lost or saved, who produces sin is of Satan (i.e., Satan is the source of the sin and the
nature that produced that sin). The expression children of the devil could apply to those who are saved as well as
those who are lost. When believers sin, they are demonstrating an action that is of the devil. They are children of
the devil in terms of their behavior. Your behavior shows which parent you are acting like—your old parent or your
new One. A believer can legally and ontologically be a child of God yet experientially be a child of the devil. In
other words, a child of God can live like a child of the devil. And in DAP, regarding Jn 8:30-32, I state that Jesus
likely intends a secondary application to the silent believers in the audience as well. The Lord challenged them not
to live according to their old nature and to realize that their silence stemmed from that old nature and thus from
Satan. A Semantic idiom of “paternity by example,” meaning, “like father, like son” is employed.

                                                  Spatial Removal
       Nevertheless, in contrast to Chitwood, Dillow, Crawford, and Hollandsworth, I defended the thesis in TOD
(pp. 433-440) that the tares currently enter the mystery form of the kingdom but not the soteriological form of the
kingdom. In the present book, this perspective is still retained but a soteric-mistholic duality is now posed. The
misthological applicability made by Hollandsworth to believers is very attractive as a misthological vantage point.
Spatially, he poses that misthological tares will be cast from the Heavenly City to Earth. Semantically, these
regenerate tares are removed from the kingdom of Heaven but not from the kingdom of God.


clarification, or at least Dillow’s admission that the present mystery form of the kingdom has been
inaugurated, his not entertaining the possibility that the tares are presently in a mystery form of the kingdom
is puzzling. The tares are placed inside this kingdom by Satan; therefore, their entry into this kingdom cannot
be equated with the new birth. Moreover, this kingdom cannot be the future millennial kingdom. For one
thing, Satan is bound during the millennial kingdom. He is not planting tares during the millennial kingdom.
And it cannot be the tribulational kingdom because the Davidic kingdom does not start until the millennium.
Once again, extraction, not mere exclusion, is envisioned. The tares are in some form of the kingdom before
being taken out of it. The mystery form of the kingdom is the most reasonable candidate.
Page 36                                                                                 Misthological Models: Part 2
       Nevertheless, the soteriological perspective made in TOD is still retained as a soteriological vantage point in
the present work, with the observation added that those tares are in the mystery form of the kingdom spatially, but
not soteriologically. Physically, they are within the inner part of the super sphere of the kingdom. Those tares that
are currently living are in the field—that is on Earth. Those tares that have died are in the ground—Hades. See
Illustration 20. Hades Taken Out of the Kingdom of Heaven. God will judge the living and dead (Acts 10:42;
1Pet 4:5). Regardless as to whether these soteriological tares are living or dead, they are within the heavenly sphere
since both Earth and Hades are within this sphere. Apparently, though, when the tares stand before God at the Great
White Throne, they will be in a dead state, having been brought forth from Hades in this state of death (Rev 20:11-
15). Thus, no living tares will be left on Earth at that time. They will have been killed by then. Still, even though
dead, they are within the heavenly sphere up until that point in time, as Hades is still within the Earth until then. At
the time of the Great White Throne judgment, however, they will be taken out of the spatial super sphere of the
kingdom of Heaven when they are cast into the Lake of Fire. Therefore, the working model herein poses a two-
phased dualistic extraction for soteriological tares. At the end of the tribulation, the tares are removed by death
from the mystery form of the kingdom. At the end of the millennium, they are removed from the spatial form
of the kingdom when Hades is cast into the Lake of Fire. Thus, a dual-soteric vantage point is being suggested
in the present work. Additionally, Hollandsworth’s mistholic vantage point is being entertained. This composite
soteric-mistholic vantage point augments the simple soteriological position taken in Darkness. And this is not the
only passage posed for reconsideration.
Antithetical Polarizations
       Although TOD defended an exclusively soteriological understanding of both (1) the parable of the wheat and
tares and (2) the parable of the dragnet, allowance nevertheless was made for an alternate antithetical possibility in
its chart of polar opposites (TOD, 106). Yet that perspective was not pursued at that time in relation to these
particular parables. In the present treatment, however, that perspective will be considered as it might apply to these
parables. Further expansion of this perspective will await additional discussion in this series.

                                          Multidimensional Perspectives
       That God who created a multiverse should speak to us in multidimensional perspectives should occasion no
great surprise. Rather, it would be surprising if He did not. These multidimensional vantage points vary in how
many dimensions are involved and the type of dimensionality employed.

      Does Repentance Imply a Mistake?
    Even a basic concept like repentance must be seen through a dualistic perspective: one as applied to humans,
the other as applied to God. Both God and humans repent. Yet the fact that God repents does not mean that He sins.
Some Christians would say that God’s repentance does not even imply that He makes mistakes.77 Other Christians,
taking a more open view of God, would argue that God does not sin but can make mistakes. This open view is
certainly messier theologically. As a synthesis, one might speculate whether God makes mistakes when He puts
Himself into a position where He can make a mistake. When God became a man, did He make any mistakes while
He was growing up as a child? If so, could He learn from His mistakes? Did Jesus, as a child learning His ABC’s
(in Hebrew, Aramaic, Latin, and Greek), always spell His words right and get His grammar correct? Did He perform
every mathematical calculation correctly the first time? When Jesus was playing as a child, did He do so flawlessly?
To put this in a modern from we can relate to, if the incarnation had happened in our time period and if Jesus had
played on a high school basketball team, would He have made every shot—in every game and every practice?

      Does God Make Mistakes?
    A reasonable conjecture is that the Lord knowingly put Himself, via His incarnation, into a position where He
could experience what it is like to be fully human in learning from His mistakes. In other words, before His
incarnation the Lord knew what mistakes He would make, for instance, in His spelling and mathematics during the
early years of His childhood; nevertheless, He chose to allow Himself to become incarnate and to make such

77
  See Morgen Kriedemann, Genesis 6: Does God Make Mistakes? Available at http://lifehopeandtruth.com/bible-
questions/why/genesis-6. Accessed 4-24-2014. S. Michael Houdmann, Does God make mistakes? Available at
http://www.gotquestions.org/does-God-make-mistakes.html. Accessed 4-24-2014.
Chapter 1. New Jerusalem                                                                                      Page 37
mistakes so that He might have the human experience of learning from His mistakes. After all, He grew in wisdom
(Lk 2:52), and making a mistake is not necessarily sin. Every sin is a mistake, but not every mistake is a sin.78 If
God were playing basketball, would He purposefully make every shot to show that He is perfect, or would He allow
Himself the freedom of missing some shots to demonstrate that He can win the game even without making every
shot and even without getting every rebound. Would He really get into the game or only go through the motions?
    How about after Jesus grew up, did He make a mistake in choosing Judas? “No,” one might say, “Jesus knew
from the beginning when He chose Judas that Judas was not a believer and that Judas would betray Him (Jn 6:64).
So He chose Judas in order to fulfill Scripture (Jn 13:18; Acts 1:16).” But this response only begs the question,
“Why did Scripture predict that Judas would betray Jesus?” This prediction did not cause Judas to betray Jesus.
Libertarians correctly affirm that Judas betrayed Jesus of his own free will. The prophecy concerning Judas was not
causative, in the sense of causing Judas to betray Jesus. Rather, knowing before His incarnation that if He chose
Judas that Judas would betray Him, God decided to choose Judas anyway and prophetically called His shot in
advance. Why choose someone who would betray Him rather than not betray Him, though? Perhaps one reason
God chose someone who would betray Him was to learn experientially what it felt like to be betrayed by a close
friend (Ps 41:9). This was a part of the suffering Jesus chose in advance to experience.
    If so, was the choosing of Judas an example of God making His shot, or was it an example of His choosing to
miss His shot to show that He knows how to win the game despite the setbacks and, as a result, giving Himself the
opportunity to perform the greatest rebound in the history of the universe? Ironically, if so, then the Lord made no
mistake in choosing to make a mistake in choosing Judas. Certainly, the pre-incarnate Jesus made no mistake in
choosing to make mistakes in learning His ABC’s and math. Our mistake is to think that God makes mistakes when
He chooses to make mistakes. God makes no mistake when He allows Himself the freedom of making mistakes. If
we can trust Him to recover the rebounds from His own mistakes, and win the game nevertheless, can we not trust
Him to recover the rebound from our mistakes as well and win the game even when the rebounds do not seem to
turn out the way we would have expected? A promise like that found in Rom 8:28 applies not only to our mistakes
but to His. God causes “all things,” our mistakes and His, “to work together for good to those who love God and
are called according to His purpose.”
    Imagine having a shooter and rebounder like that on your team! The only shots and rebounds He misses are
those He chooses to miss! And even when He does miss or fail to get a rebound, we can be absolutely assured that
He will work it for the good in terms of the final outcome of the game. He has chosen to win the game, despite
having chosen to allow Himself the freedom to miss some shots and rebounds along the way, and nothing will
prevent Him from winning the contest. Similarly, God makes no mistakes when He allows us the freedom of making
mistakes. He has complete confidence in His own ability to win the game, despite whatever setbacks we may throw
His way. Even when you miss a shot, have a pass intercepted, pass the ball to the wrong team, or even score in the
wrong basket, nothing is going to prevent Him from ultimate victory (Rom 8:35-39). After all, He even knows when
and where the ball is going to come off the rim every time, and He knows when and where the opposing team is
going to make a shot or pass, and when and where you are going to blow it. We can have complete confidence in
His ability to win and in the process to make us winners. He has proven Himself as the Supreme Winner (Heb 12:2).
Or think of God as the Judo champion par excellence:

     There are a lot of things that you can do on purpose to good effective [sic.] that are disastrous if done
     accidentally. It’s that mindfulness thing. Almost any rule (whether a law or common sense) can be broken
     advantageously....when a beginner stiff arms and blocks a throw, it is a bad habit. Poor judo. When a skilled
     practitioner blocks the same throw in the same way it is “good structure.”79
78
   One can be sincere, but be sincerely wrong, yet being sincerely wrong is not necessarily sin. Even in Heaven one
still can make accidental mistakes and miss the mark. (Frank D. Carmical, The Omega Reunion, 72.) Trying to
define sin simplistically in terms of classical Greek as missing a mark is imprudent. We will not all be able to hit a
hole-in-one on every shot while playing golf in Heaven. Missing the mark will not mean that we have sinned.
Sinless students in Heaven trying to solve complex math problems will make mistakes. This does not mean that
they will have sinned. All sin is falling short, but not all falling short is sin. By the NT time sin no longer simply
meant “‘missing the mark’ or ‘failing when trying’”; rather, sin was considered to be “deliberate disobedience....the
word always had an ethical sense of not conforming to a standard.” R.S. Beal and Earl D. Radmacher, Ephesians:
Life and Love in Christ (Chino Valley: One World Press, 2012), 72.
79
    See Purposeful Mistakes. Available at http://chirontraining.blogspot.com/2010/07/purposeful-mistakes.html.
Page 38                                                                                 Misthological Models: Part 2
      Purposeful Mistakes
        Purposeful mistakes can be very beneficial in the hands of a skilled practitioner. God and Satan are locked
in a judo match in which Satan is learning, the hard way, that when God makes a mistake, it is no mistake. Such
dualism is not self-contradictory; it simply involves learning how to distinguish purposeful mistakes from accidental
mistakes. What is a mistake from one vantage point is not a mistake from another equally valid, noncontradictory,
alternate point of view. Even with the word repent, God is teaching us to look at Scripture multidimensionally.
        An immediate example of a purposeful mistake was just supplied in the last sentence. My spell checker
flagged down my use of the word multidimensionally as a misspelling. Nevertheless, I purposefully choose to accept
that spelling and even added it to one of my custom dictionaries, something I do with some frequency, especially
with my coined terms. Such misspelling (mistakes in spelling) are purposeful mistakes. Not all my mistakes are
purposeful, of course; some are accidental. But God makes no accidental mistakes. God has a purpose in everything
He does. Even the accidental misspellings that Jesus made as a child were the purposeful misspellings He chose to
make before He became a child. From the vantage point of His pre-incarnation, those accidental mistakes were
actually purposeful.
        In the Garden of Gethsemane Jesus prayed, “My Father, if it is possible, let this cup pass from Me; yet not
as I will, but as Thou wilt” (Mt 26:39). Was Jesus mistaken in thinking that it might be possible? Apparently.
Otherwise, if it were possible for the Father to let the cup pass by Jesus, thereby accomplishing the mission some
other way, surely the Father would have granted Jesus’ request. But the Father did not let the cup pass by Jesus. So
the most straightforward deduction is that Jesus was mistaken in thinking that it might be possible. In hope against
hope, Jesus prayed, just in case there was another way. He did not know for sure. As it turns out, taking the Scripture
at face value, Jesus was mistaken in thinking that there might be another way. Yet He did not sin in making such a
request. Perhaps, even making such a prayer was a mistake in that it revolved around a mistaken impression. Even
in that case, though, He knew before His incarnation that, when the time came, He would make that mistake because
of the self-imposed limitation that He put on the exercise of His knowledge during the incarnation. He purposefully
put Himself in the position where He would make that heart-rending mistake! And we love Him even more for it.
He really humbled Himself, even to the point of making mistakes, in coming down to our level to redeem us. To
err is human, and He became fully human. So He erred. Yet He did not sin, being fully God. The God-Man made a
mistake, but He did not sin in doing so. His sinless mistake endears us to Him. Accordingly, His deciding to make
that mistake shows His genius.
        On the battlefield, a general might make a strategic withdrawal to lure the opposing force into an ambush.
William the Conqueror used such a retreat during the Battle of Hastings to lure Harold’s infantry from their high
ground defense to a place where they could be destroyed by William’s cavalry. In the Bible, Joshua used this tactic
to lure the defenders of Ai away from their city, leaving the city unguarded, so that part of Joshua’s forces might
ambush the city from behind. Joshua’s victory was a complete success. God is the one who gave Joshua this battle
strategy (Josh 8:1-35). In this passage, God teaches us that He implements such strategy on occasion. Granted,
Gethsemane is not an example of a strategic retreat, but it is an example of something similar—a strategic mistake.
God allowed Jesus (our Joshua) to make this mistake in order to win our hearts—a brilliant battlefield maneuver.
Making that mistake was no mistake in terms of the overall objective of winning our hearts from Satan.
Nevertheless, it was an honest mistake. At the time Jesus prayed that prayer, He honestly did not know if it might
somehow be possible for the Father to find another way to win the battle for our hearts, souls, and spirits. So earnest
was Jesus in His prayer that His sweat turned to blood, via hematidrosis, as the blood vessels around His sweat
glands burst from the extreme stress (Lk 22:44). Jesus was not just going through the motions in this prayer. It was
not a game; it was a battle. And He has won many hearts from Satan by making this purposeful mistake.
        Recognizing duality is extremely important. Anyone who cannot perceive multidimensional repentance is
going to make a lot of accidental mistakes interpreting Scripture. Likewise, those who cannot perceive many of the
other multidimensional concepts found in Scripture are susceptible to numerous errors. Sometimes those errors are
deadly, such as in the case of those who cannot properly distinguish the gift dimension of eternal life from its reward
dimension. False teachers merge the two into one dimension.

                                                       Mt 18:8-9
       Briefly, to summarize some of these passages, it was argued in Darkness that Mt 18:8-9 could be interpreted
soteriologically as being applicable to the unregenerate. Some unbelievers will need to cut off (i.e., deal with)
anything in their lives that is preventing them from coming to saving faith. Repentance is a precondition for some
of the lost in coming to saving faith. Otherwise, they will burn eternally in the Lake of Fire. Those who do repent
and follow in saving faith will have qualified for kingdom rulership. Therefore, it and a verse like Mk 1:15 might
be regarded as soteric in calling for saving faith or mistholic in that it lays out both soteriological and misthological
requirements, albeit in reverse order because sometimes it is necessary to deal with sin in regard to one’s
performance before one is ready to come to saving faith. Nevertheless, since performance issues are misthological
in nature, a person who repents and then comes to saving faith is already on the path leading to kingdom rulership.
They not only have entered the kingdom gate soteriologically, they are on the kingdom path misthologically, the
way of righteousness that leads to the crown of righteousness. Because blue and yellow make green, the antithetical
composite of a soteriological and misthological contrast is denoted in green on the chart. The following color key
will be key to following the discussion of such passages.

      •   yellow (mistholic)
      •   green (both—an antithetical composite)

                                                    Mk 1:15
      The juxtaposition can sometimes be pictured more clearly as the overlap with other passages, such as the
case of the parallelism between Mk 1:15 and Mt 3:2. Although both passages probably should be understood as
addressing the same group of people and offering the same thing, the Markan offer includes a summons to believe
whereas the Matthean appeal does not. Matthew focuses attention upon the green face of the prism.81
                                           Illustration 27. Venn Overlap




81
   The word prism is being used herein in a nontechnical manner to denote a three-sided pyramid used to split light
into a spectrum so that we may see a passage in any one of four lights. The reader is encouraged to print out the
triangle on the left on a transparency and then cut, fold, and paste it to make a triangular prism. Then, while looking
through the various faces, imagine which light the Scripture intents for certain passages.
Chapter 1. New Jerusalem                                                                                       Page 41
      The call to repent in Mt 3:2, although mistholic, is addressed to unbelievers in its historical context, as is
proven by a comparison with Mk 1:15 and Jn 1:7. Each writer focuses on different aspects of the summons:

        •   Matthew (3:2) is focused on the yellow face of the prism with a call restricted to repent.
        •   Mark (1:15) is dualistically green with a broader call to repent and believe.
        •   John (1:7) is focused on the blue with a narrow call to believe.

       Note carefully that both yellow and green invitations and warnings can be issued to the lost. Invitations to
the soteriological lost are not limited to blue (i.e., soteriological) invitations. Matthew drives this point home most
forcefully in the parable of the wedding feast (Mt 22:1-14). Everyone, lost and saved, is given the same (yellow)
invitation to come to the wedding feast, a misthological event. The lost do not come and, as a result, are killed and
burned. The passage is replete with the full soteriological-misthological spectrum of (1a) indifferent versus
(1b) murderous unbelievers as opposed to (2a) worthy versus (2b) unworthy believers. Therefore, it is a red all-
inclusive passage par excellence. But the invitation issued within it to come is misthological, even though it is
issued to unbelievers and believers alike (Mt 22:4). Being worthy to attend that wedding feast requires not only that
one be a believer but that one be a misthologically worthy believer. Just because a misthological invitation is used
within the Gospel of Matthew, one cannot automatically assume that it cannot be addressed to unbelievers.
Inversely, warnings that seemingly are addressed to unbelievers, such as Mt 18:8-9, might have applicability to
believers. In other words, you might have a blue exhortation issued with yellow applicability to the saved or,
conversely, a yellow exhortation with yellow applicability to the lost that presupposes the blue prerequisite.

                                                      Mt 7:13-27
       For example, Jesus’ exhortation to take up one’s cross in Mt 16:24-27, although issued to the saved, is
nevertheless made applicable to anyone, whoever, and every man. The yellow warning applies to both: those who
are unregenerate (and thus in the blue column), and to those who are regenerate (and thus aspiring for the yellow
column). Consequently, one cannot automatically assume that a misthological text like Mt 7:24-27has no
applicability to the lost. They too are invited to build their lives upon Christ as their own bedrock. If they do not
take advantage of this yellow invitation, they will suffer destruction, which for them is soteriological as their house
collapses. Failing to avail themselves of the yellow invitation will result in blue destruction. For believers,
contrastively, it could be marked as red destruction, signifying that the fate of carnal believers is addressed as well.
But if this concluding pericope from the Sermon on the Mount has soteriological applicability to the lost, then could
not other portions of the sermon also have applicability to the lost, particularly the pericope immediately preceding
in Mt 7:21-23, which is connected with this one in Mt 7:24-27 with the word therefore (oun)? Should we not look
back at the preceding context to see what the therefore is there for?
                             Illustration 28. Two Cut and Dry Options for Mt 7:20-23
                        Soteric                                                    Mistholic
Page 42                                                                                     Misthological Models: Part 2

        The blue applicability of 7:24-27 to the lost would suggest a corresponding blue applicability of Mt 7:21-23
to the lost.82 Darkness presents a strong defense for a blue understanding of Mt 7:21-23. Destiny advocates a yellow
perspective. These Darkness-Destiny arguments for each respective position are straight forward. Both approaches
have their advantages. Such cut-and-dry approaches have the advantage of simplicity. However, the current study
suggests that such either-or approaches fail to see the full spectrum. In Darkness, though, the additional possibility
was postulated that a dual position might be allowable, perhaps even preferable. Now that Dillow has presented a
mature work advocating a yellow perspective, I am consequently more comfortable with the yellow dimensionality
for the 7:21-23 pericope as a counterbalance, and thus a complement, to my earlier blue perspective so that now I
might favor a green perspective, especially since I stated in Darkness that I was anticipating migrating more to such
a neoclassical viewpoint advocating duality once a mature work such as Destiny was available.
        Briefly, and to restate Destiny’s position in color-coded terms, yellow entrance into the regal kingship
requires that believers do the will of God ethically in terms of their performance. Basileia is understood as kingship
when used in such a capacity (e.g., Destiny, 274-278, Darkness, 768, 947ff.). In harmony with the neoclassical
anticipation in Darkness and in contradistinction to Destiny’s thoroughgoing misthological perspective regarding
Mt 7:21-23, adopting a yellow exclusivity is regarded as ill-advised herein. Combining the blue and yellow
perspectives to derive a green result would be far superior to an exclusively yellow perspective. Indeed, the warning
about false teachers in the preceding pericope can be included since Dillow and I are agreed regarding the
unregenerate state of the wolves in sheep’s clothing. Thus, a dual perspective would mark Mt 7:15-23 in its entirety
in green if the passage comprises both blue and yellow elements in juxtaposition and combination.

         Half Way
       These false teachers teach false doctrine, believe a false gospel, produce a false faith, and consequently
produce false converts. Because of their defective soteriology, they sow tares in the church that trust in their
performance for soteriological entrance. They and their false converts will be rejected from the kingdom. They
never entered it soteriologically in the first place; they will not enter it millennially in the future; they will be ejected
from it spatially when they depart into the Lake of Fire at the end of the millennial age. Jesus denies knowing them
salvifically. On the other hand, the antithetical contrast encountered elsewhere seems to be present in this passage
as well in that Jesus does not explicitly condemn them for not believing but for not performing. As already
demonstrated in other such antithetical contrasts, this is by no means a conclusive argument that the passage simply
should be treated in a misthological sense. The poor performance of the goats and tares was also pointed out by the
Lord in those passages: The goats were guilty of sins of omission; the tares were guilty of sins of commission.
Likewise, those who did badly and those who were bad in Jn 5:29 and Mt 13:48 have been noted respectively. They
likewise were rejected soteriologically, with a finger being pointed at their behavior, just as we find here in Mt 7:23,
where they are told to depart from the Lord in view of their lawlessness.




82
     Erchomai (come) is being used in these illustrations as an abbreviation for enter (i.e., eiserchomai, come into).
Chapter 1. New Jerusalem                                                                                      Page 43
                                         Illustration 29. Neoclassical View




        Yet stopping at a blue interpretation seems to fall short of the red application of Mt 7:24-27, seemingly
applied to Mt 7:21-23 with the connector therefore. Additionally, in the other antithetical juxtapositions just
mentioned, blue is contrasted with yellow explicitly so that a soteriological outcome is juxtaposed unabashedly with
a misthological outcome. The good sheep inherit the kingdom. Those who do good (works) experience a
resurrection to life, evidently at the misthological level. The fruitful wheat is gathered into the barn, a beautiful
picture of the Misthological City. The good fish are gathered into the container, again a reasonable misthological
reference to the Reward City. Given the nature of such juxtapositions being surveyed presently, one would be
inclined to think that a misthological outcome is certainly within the scope of the present pericope as well. After
all, this is a judgment scene, and judgment is always according to works. For such reasons, as well as for some of
the additional reasons supplied by Dillow, allowing a misthological dimension is permissible and probably even
advisable.
        Even if Mt 7:21-23 is exclusively blue in terms of its explicit statements, as seems reasonable, it creates the
implicit anticipation (and thereby an implicit contrast) in that those whom the Lord does know are those who not
only do His will in terms of believing in Him for the free gift of eternal life (cf. Jn 6:38-40) but who also do it
ethically in terms of their behavior, in according with the stress in the Sermon on the Mount on kingdom behavior
and the other juxtapositions being surveyed. Those whom the Lord acknowledges as knowing in the fullest sense
are those who not only believe but behave. The misthological juxtaposition that is made explicit in those other
passages is made implicit here. Given my paradigm, it is hard for me not to see Mt 7:21-23 in an explicit blue when
I read it, at least in terms of its primary color. Nevertheless, I can see an implicit yellow dome cast over it by the
context, and therefore a composite green as being a reasonable inference. In this manner, I can meet Dillow half
way in posing a soteric-mistholic understanding of what the Lord intents. Notwithstanding, I am not suggesting an
explicit neoclassical view. The passage evidences greater finesse.
Page 44                                                                                 Misthological Models: Part 2
      Only Half Way
      Even regardless of whether an explicit or implicit approach is taken, meeting Dillow more than half way
regarding Mt 7:21-23 is impossible on Dillowian premises.83 The blue spectrum cannot be dismissed either from
the context or from this particular part of that context. Dillow acknowledges that the false prophets in Mt 7:15-20
are unregenerate. Using this as his exegetical foil, Wilkin drives home his counter point:

              The connection between Matt 7:15-20 and Matt 7:21-23 shows that unbelievers are in view. The
      former speaks of false prophets (v 15) who are known by their fruits, i.e., their words, their false
      teachings (cf. Matt 12:33-36 where fruit is identified as words). The latter also speaks of people who
      claim to prophesy in Jesus’ name (v 22). It is reasonable to equate the false prophets of vv 15-20 with
      those claiming to prophesy in Jesus’ name in v 22. I see no evidence that the Lord is saying that the
      false prophets of vv 15-20 are born again. Indeed, there is no mention of faith in Christ anywhere in
      Matt 7:15-23. To suggest that these are unfaithful believers being excluded from kingdom reward is
      not suggested by the context....
              While he [Dillow] agrees the false prophets of Matt 7:15-20 are unregenerate, he says, “Starting
      in v 21 the scene changes” (p. 302). Several pages later, Dillow does go on to say that the prophets in
      Matt 7:22 are false prophets too, but he calls them “false Christian prophets” (pp. 307-308; see also p.
      300). He cites a passage from the Didache, an early Christian writing, which warned about false
      Christian prophets (pp. 307-308)....
              ... Dillow argues that doing the will of the Father “is the opposite of ‘those who practice
      lawlessness’ (7:23); it is about obedience, not initial faith for salvation” (p. 306).
              This is not the point. The verse is not set up as antithetically parallel to v 21. Here is an
      example of antithetical parallelism: “The young lions lack and suffer hunger; but those who seek the
      Lord shall not lack any good thing” (Ps 34:10). What is compared is lacking versus not lacking.
              “I never knew you; depart from Me, you who practice lawlessness” is clearly related to v 21.
      The reason they are departing from Jesus and being sent to the lake of fire (Rev 20:15) is because they
      did not do the will of the Father by believing in the Son and hence they were not found in the book of
      life.84 (Italics his, bold mine)

       These false prophets in Mt 7:15-20 are false Christians. They have failed to bear the singular good fruit of
saving faith. Their discerning listeners can detect the lack of saving persuasion on the part of the false teachers by
the plural bad fruits of false doctrine taught by such teachers. These false prophets and their false converts are the
subject matter of Mt 7:21-23. Unfortunately, their false converts are not discerning listeners. These undiscerning
listeners have been led astray from coming to a saving understanding of the gospel of grace, which lies at the heart
of the gospel of the kingdom. These two passages, therefore, are not antithetical. Both passages are blue, dealing
with false teachers giving rise to false Christians, both of which fail to enter the kingdom soterically.
       At the conclusion of this passage, Jesus warns His followers not to be led astray by such false teaching.
Otherwise, just as the unregenerate dupes of these false teachers will experience a blue destruction, so His regenerate
followers, if duped, are susceptible to an inclusive red destruction, pictured in Mt 7:24-27, connected with the word
therefore, and thereby made applicable to everyone. Such a warning from Jesus to His regenerate followers is
certainly appropriate. As Matthew records for his readers, Jesus warned them, “Watch out and beware of the leaven
of the Pharisees....the teaching of the Pharisees” (Mt 16:6-12). The writer of Hebrews likewise urges his genuine
Christian readership, “Let us hold fast our confession” (Heb 4:14; cp. 3:6,14; 10:23), rather than fall back into a
Judaic mindset. Paul warns the Galatian believers (who likewise were tempted by this Judaic leaven) that, although
the Galatians were genuine believers, they had fallen from Christ as a result of falling into a Judaic mindset (Gal

83
   Granted, Hollandsworth’s approach might allow an exclusively mistholic coding since he takes the whole passage
as misthological. But critical interaction with his model must be deferred until possibly later in this series. Even so,
his books are recommended as well as standard FG literature taking a soteriological viewpoint. The combination of
both would yield a composite roughly similar to what is being entertained herein.

5:4). The wind had blown, and their house had fallen, to use Christ’s words. To be Christ’s house, we must hold

        Wilkin’s argument is powerful. Both pericopes in Mt 7:15-23 are blue, that is, soteriological. Moreover, as
just demonstrated, this exclusively blue approach can provide a convincing explanation for the misthological link
with Mt 7:24-27. The two passages are not antithetical to one another. For the sake of completeness, however, like
Wilkin, I will not stop at Mt 7:15-27 but also bring Mt 7:13-14 into consideration, as it complements and completes
the present passage. Wilkin objects to Dillow’s interpretation of the narrow way: “If the narrow way that few find
in Matt 7:13-14 is the way to spending eternity with the Lord in His kingdom, then this means the majority of people
will miss the kingdom. That certainly gives us a sense of urgency in evangelism.”85 I concur with Wilkin’s concern
and have stressed the soteriological interpretation of Christ, rather than the Christian life, as the narrow way in
Darkness. This part of the passage must not be robbed of its impetus for evangelistic fervor.
                                       Illustration 30. Classic Narrow Way




       Using pictures to save a few thousand words, my classic perception of Mt 7:13-27 is that punctiliar faith in
Christ results in the immediate soteriological entrance into the heavenly realm, which unconditionally assures
believers of going to Heaven.86 Those believers who subsequently fall from this grace perspective will suffer
misthological loss in the outer darkness as their house comes crashing down misthologically. Unbelievers who take
the broad gate of works righteousness, contrastively, will suffer their destruction in the Lake of Fire. Therefore,
7:13-23 is blue, dealing only with the fate of unbelievers with no intended application to believers. The application
to believers is limited strictly to 7:24-27, which is applicable to unbelievers and believers alike and thus red. No
juxtaposition is intended: (1) between 7:13-14 and 7:16-23, or (2) between 7:15-20 and 7:21-23, or (3) between

85
  Ibid.
86
   Some people look down on using pictures to illustrate a text, thinking that a visual aid is not suitable to an
intellectual discussion. Counter wise, Jesus encoded His material in word pictures; therefore, using pictures to
decode it is highly appropriate. Plus, some people are better able to wrap their brains around a concept if they can
visualize it. Visualized learning is nothing foreign to Scripture, Jesus uses it effectively in much of His teaching.
Of course, He does not do the coloring for us. He is encoding not decoding. He expects us to bring our own crayons
to the table. When you create a coloring book for children, you do not fill in the colors for them. Like any good
parent, the Lord has given His children the coloring book, but He expects them to do their own coloring. If we do
a particularly good job, perhaps He will hang up our picture on the door of the kitchen frig at the Bema to show it
off to the rest of the family.
Page 46                                                                                 Misthological Models: Part 2
7:16-23 and 7:24-27. Rather, 7:24-27 simply makes 7:13-23 applicable to both unbelievers and believers by
implicitly warning believers not to adopt the theological perspective of the false teachers and their adherents.
       If it were not for the duality and antithetical juxtapositions elsewhere in Scripture, Occam’s Razor would
justify being satisfied with this classical position. Notwithstanding the attractiveness and efficiency of the classic
position, a synthesis between Wilkin and Dillow’s positions is permissible and probably even advisable. The
juxtaposition, if present, is not between Mt 7:15-20 and 7:21-23, as Wilkin correctly argues, but between the context
as properly understood soterically and the implicit anticipation created by Mt 7:21-23 and arguably by 7:15-20 that
believers can qualify for kingdom rulership mistholically by doing what the unbelievers in this passage fail to do.
Namely, by living the Christian life the way it is supposed to be lived and by teaching sound Christian doctrine,
respectively, believers can qualify for the kingship.
                                   Illustration 31. Acceptable Neoclassical View




       The primary exegetical strengths of the synthesis being proposed herein are that it does not limit the warning
to the doctrine of false teachers but also encompasses their lawless behavior as well. Believers are appropriately
warned not to follow either the doctrine or behavior of unbelievers. Surely, the warning in Mt 7:24-27 should not
be interpreted as overlooking the lawless behavior that is explicitly condemned. Additionally, the destruction
pictured by the fall of the house in Mt 7:24-27 definitely appears to link back to the destruction in 7:13. The imagery
of the collapsing house in 7:27 illustrates the destruction introduced in 7:13. These verses are bookends for one
another. Correspondingly, if the destruction of 7:27 is universal, the destruction of 7:13 should be as well. In that
case, both the narrow gate and the broad gate have dual applicability.
       As to the narrow gate, blue soteric entrance into the narrow gate is anticipated for a walk on the mistholic
yellow-brick road of the narrow path for a yellow entrance into the green kingship. In other words, soteric entrance
into the narrow gate at the point of regeneration for the believer is intended to be a precursor to mistholic entrance
into the narrow gate at the Judgment Seat of Christ. Not all believers will enter the narrow gate in the secondary
manner, however.
       The blue gate entered by the lost is the broad gate rather than narrow gate. The destruction of Mt 7:13 will
be soteric for the unregenerate because they never entered the blue narrow gate.87 The lingering lure of the broad

87
  The false prophets coming into the church are poisonous teachers (Mt 7:15-20), just like the tares in the church

infiltrators into the mystery form of the kingdom who will be destroyed soteriologically, burned in the blue fire in
both contexts (Mt 7:19; 13:40,42,50). Hollandsworth’s latest book, Keys, has argued for an exclusively
Chapter 1. New Jerusalem                                                                                          Page 47
gate, nevertheless, poses a danger to believers. As to believers, the narrow gate is blue at the initial point of entry,
as is the narrow way. Believing in Christ for eternal life as a free gift is the singular requirement for this leg of their
journey. Punctiliar entrance into the blue narrow gate puts believers immediately and irreversibly into the blue
soteric sphere of Heaven. Yet if believers do not persist in this narrow way as a way of life, they will only have
traversed the narrow way sufficiently so as to end up in the outer darkness, within Heaven to be sure. Persisting on
the narrow way, by abiding in Christ experientially, puts believers on the yellow brick road leading to the abundant
entrance into kingdom rulership and residency in the Heavenly City.88

       Summation
       In summary, a green result for Mt 7:21-23 may be derived in one of two basic ways: one explicit, the other
implicit. The explicit option is the most direct route but is also the most questionable. One may argue simplistically
that the Lord is making an explicit reference to entering the kingdom in two different capacities. The first capacity
is soteric. The second is mistholic. Soteriological entrance into the kingdom is denied to unbelievers, but what
unbelievers fail to achieve in accord with their falling short in their performance, believers are expected to achieve
in terms of entering the kingship. Even though this option is correct to an extent, it is unlikely that the hypothetical
objectors being addressed explicitly by the Lord in this pericope are believers. Rather, the implications, as they
apply to kingdom rulership, are to be derived by the believing listeners as they listen to His rebuke of the
unregenerate responder crying out, “Lord, Lord.” Thus, basileia means kingdom, not kingship, when seen through
the eyes of those unregenerate listeners and applied to themselves.
                                            Illustration 32. Eyes and Ears




       Posing this distinction between the objector and the regenerate listeners leads intuitively to an implicit
juxtaposition. The Lord seems to be setting up an implicit apposition in which believers are to perceive a
misthological application to themselves. Application is not to be equated with interpretation, however. Even back
then, the Lord expected His disciples to be able to discern the sound hermeneutical difference between application
and interpretation. The Lord intended for His believing audience to anticipate that if they do what the lost are failing
to do, that is, believe and perform, then they (i.e., His believing audience) may obtain kingdom rulership. Proper
application encourages us to learn from the mistakes of others, such as the lost, not to confuse ourselves with the
lost interpretatively. Having already believed, the Lord’s regenerate followers are now supposed to move on to
behave in a kingdom manner and thereby qualify for the proffered kingdom rewards. When seen through the eyes
of the objectors, basileia is intended to be interpreted soteriologically as kingdom. This is the correct interpretation
of basileia. When heard through the ears of the regenerate listeners, basileia is intended to be understood as having
a secondary misthological ramification in regard to the kingship.

discussion as a valuable vantage point in terms of inclusivity. The parable of the tares can be seen as a green
antithetical composite intending both soteric and mistholic applicability. Nevertheless, my argument for multiple
vantage points is made stronger by strong arguments for the singular vantage points making up the composite.
Therefore, I welcome both a strong soteric argument by Wilkin and a strong mistholic argument by Hollandsworth.
