        Painting over this passage with a broad brush would yield a blue result. But this approach runs roughshod
over what Matthew is doing in the passage. He is painting a Mona Lisa, and one does not paint a Mona Lisa with a
roller. Illustration 31 attempts to picture some of the exegetical details. Nevertheless, some people may get lost in
the details. Thus, a pictorial summary from a bird’s eye perspective may help.
                                           Illustration 34. Bird’s Eye View




        The bookends in Mt 7:13-14 and 7:24-27 have an underlying soteric interpretation with an intended overlying
yellow applicability. The blue of Mt 7:13-14 stretches all the way across the chart from left to right, while the yellow
of Mt 7:24-27 stretches all the way across the chart from right to left. But the yellow is applied differently to the
bookends than it is to the two intermediate columns. The bookends generate a yellow dome over the intermediate
columns. This yellow dome is generated by the text in the bookends, in those bookends the yellow, like the blue, is
even a part of that text. To picture this relationship a little better, the next illustration will lay the blue text down
flat so that the dome rising above it may be more readily perceived.
Chapter 1. New Jerusalem                                                                                          Page 49
                                            Illustration 35. Mistholic Dome




        Whereas the yellow and red overlays are a part of the blue text in the first and last columns, the intermediate
columns are only blue. The misthology dome, which acts like a lens through which believers are expected to apply
the intermediate columns to themselves, could be thought of as a hermeneutical forcefield generated by the power
cells in the outlying columns. Naturally, in hermeneutical fields one must be able to discern interpretation from
application. That distinction, while present, is not the force generating this mistholic perception in the present case,
however. A much stronger force is generating this field in the present context in that the context expects the reader
to make this explicit application with an implicit juxtaposition. Because the context juxtaposes the misthological
impression indirectly rather than directly, picturing the misthological applicability differently in the intermediate
columns is exegetically necessary.
        Restated in exegetical terms, the many and few of 7:13-14 are intended to be interpreted as being directly
applicable to both unbelievers and believers alike. When interpreted as applying to unbelievers, which is an
application directly intended by the text, then the gate, way, and destruction are intended by the text to be interpreted
soterically. Conversely, when interpreted as applying to believers, which is also an application directly intended by
the text, then the key terms have a misthological import. Similarly, the everyone of Mt 7:24-27 is intended, by the
text, to be applied directly to both unbelievers and believers, with the corresponding understanding that the
destruction pictured by the fall of the house is soteriological or misthological, respectively.
        In contrast, the false teachers and bad trees and related bad fruit in Mt 7:15-20 are not to be understood as
providing a direct ethical juxtaposition with the good trees and good fruit. Granted, an obvious juxtaposition is
expressed by the text in contrasting good trees and good fruit with bad trees and bad fruit. That juxtaposition,
however, as contained within the actual text of this pericope is strictly soteriological. Soteriological juxtapositions
should not be confused with ethical juxtapositions. The text in and of itself, as confined to this column, generates
no misthological force. Similarly, those being rebuked and told to depart from the Lord in Mt 7:21-23 are intended
by the text to be interpreted as representing unbelievers exclusively. The Lord intends for this rebuke, as contained
in this section, to be understood strictly soteriologically, as an extension of 7:15-20. This text, in and of itself, makes
no direct contribution to the generation of the misthological impression. Instead, the misthological impression
generated over these two columns is generated by the power of the immediate context, found in the two outlying
columns, and by the power of the broader context, found in the Sermon on the Mount, a sermon concerned primarily
with misthological themes. Nevertheless, because the context intends an implicit ethical juxtaposition, the base of
the misthological dome has been pictured as laying directly on the text, not suspended in the air above it.
        Jesus is setting a high ethical bar. He did so with the rich young ruler as well. Peter responded that he and
the other disciples had met that requirement, and asked in essence, “What’s in it for us?” Jesus promised them
kingdom rulership as their reward (Mt 19:27-28). As one reads Mt 7:13-23 and imagines Peter setting there listening
to the Sermon, one can almost hear the gears in Peter’s mind grinding as he calculates and formulates his questions:
“What’s in it for us if we keep walking this narrow way that Jesus is talking about? What’s in it for us if, in addition
to performing the miracles that we are performing, we practice this kingdom way of living that Jesus is calling upon
His followers to live up to in this sermon?” Jesus certainly seems to anticipate such questions, as His response in
7:24-27 makes what He is saying explicitly applicable to Peter and the other disciples, when He repeatedly says
that what He has just said is applicable to everyone (Mt 7:24,26). If Peter were sitting there wondering, “Do you
intend this for us?” as he did on another occasion (Lk 12:41), then the answer must be, “Yes.” Accordingly, the
position adopted herein is that Matthew intends for Mt 7:13-27 to be understood on multiple levels. The highly
debated heart of this passage, the pericope in Mt 7:20-23, is intended to be taken foremost as an exhortation to
unbelieving disciples (cp. Jn 6:64-66) to come to saving faith (cp. Jn 6:38-40). Upon further reflection, though, His
Page 50                                                                                   Misthological Models: Part 2
believing disciples intuitively were expected to sense that Mt 7:15-23 holds implicit, though not explicit, application
for themselves.
       Possible confirmation comes from the conclusion of the Sermon on the Mount’s sister passage, the Sermon
on the Plain. Therein the focus is arguably limited mostly, if not exclusively, to misthological themes. Not only is
an appeal possibly made for His disciples to bear good fruit morally (Lk 6:43-46),89 but the rhetorical question is
again expressed: “And why do you call Me, ‘Lord, Lord,’ and do not do what I say?” (Lk 6:46) Building one’s life
morally upon the Lord’s teaching is the response necessary to demonstrate that one is doing what Jesus demands in
that context. Even if Luke has cut out some soteriological material (that would be equivalent to Mt 7:21-23) in his
abbreviated account in the Sermon on the Plain to focus his readers’ attention on the misthological dimension, the
parallelism between the two sermons is sufficient to suggest that the Lord-Lord invocation in Mt 7:22 is expected
to have a misthological dimension, though not necessarily limited to that dimension or even making that connection
directly.

                                                      Mt 5:19-20

      For
     A similar, although not identical argument, could be made regarding the for (gar) that introduces Mt 5:20 as
was made for the therefore (oun) that introduced Mt 7:24. “For I say to you, that unless your righteousness surpasses
that of the scribes and Pharisees, you shall not enter the basileia of heaven” (Mt 5:20).90 We should look back at
Mt 5:19 to see what the for is there for in Mt 5:20. Because Mt 5:19 is misthological, Mt 5:20 certainly would be
intended to have a misthological applicability as well. Interpreted misthologically, we could say that all believers
are in the kingdom soteriologically and will be in the kingdom realm spatially (v. 19), but not all believers will enter
the kingdom regally. Qualifying for the punctiliar regal entrance at the Bema into the future kingship requires that
the righteousness of the believers surpasses that of the scribes and Pharisees, both in terms of what they do and what
they teach: “Whoever does them and teaches them [i.e., the least commandments] will be called great in the
kingdom of heaven.”91

89
   In my book, Karpology, I argue for a soteriological understanding of the fruit bearing in Lk 6:43-46. That
perspective is retained here, especially regarding Mt 7:15-20. Doing so is not counterproductive to the antithetical-
juxtaposition perspective taken herein, however. What might be put on the table for further discussion, though, is
whether a blue understanding of the fruit bearing in the Lukan passage is the only perspective intended. Or is
dualism possible? Might Mt 7:15-20 be blue, when considered as a self-contained unit, but Lk 6:43-46 green?
90
   Unfortunately, the ESV leaves out the therefore in Mt 7:24, but at least it has the for in Mt 5:20. It should have
both.
91
   Neoclassically, the entry saying in Mt 5:20 would be primarily misthological and secondarily soteriological. Upon
reflection, the disciples are to see how this misthological text would have secondary soteriological application to
the unbelieving scribes and Pharisees. Inversely, given Mt 7:15-20, the disciples would be expected to perceive
Mt 7:21 as a soteriological text that has misthological application to themselves. The two texts complement one
another with their inverse relationship: The mistholic-soteric emphasis of Mt 5:20 combines with the soteric-
mistholic emphasis of Mt 7:20 to produce a chiastic balance in emphasis:

                                  A. Mistholic (Mt 5:20 interpretation)
                                     B. Soteric (Mt 5:20 secondary application)
                                     B. Soteric (Mt 7:20 interpretation)
                                  A. Mistholic (Mt 7:20 secondary application)

Although this is a valid interpretation of the text, some misthologists would be quick to assume that a misthological
interpretation is the only way to interpret the text. Soteriologists, contrastively, would be quick to argue that the text
must be understood exclusively in a soteriological manner. After all, are we not taught in hermeneutical theory to
limit ourselves to the singular meaning intended by the biblical writer? In counter point, neoclassicalists can
respond, “What about those occasions when a biblical writer does not intend to limit the meaning to one singular
understanding?” Even classical practitioners of hermeneutical theory acknowledge duality. Double-fulfillment (also
known as dual reference) is a classic example. Neoclassicalists do not have to limit themselves to one valid
Chapter 1. New Jerusalem                                                                                      Page 51
                                   Illustration 36. First and Last in the Kingdom




         Kingdom versus Kingship
       The duality intended by the text in Mt 5:20 is such that the unbelieving Pharisees are excluded from entering
the kingdom soteriologically, and believing disciples who fail to measure up in terms of their kingdom living and
doctrinal teaching will not enter the kingship regally. Basileia must be understood and pictured in this dual capacity.
Upon encountering this word in the text, Matthew expects his readership to be able to perceive its intended duality:
                               Illustration 37. Color Coded Translation of Mt 5:19-20
    19
      Whoever then annuls one of the least of these commandments, and so teaches others, will be called least
    inside the kingdom/basileia of heaven; but whoever keeps and teaches them, he will be called great inside
    the kingdom/basileia of heaven. 20 For I say to you, that unless your righteousness surpasses that of the
    scribes and Pharisees, you will not enter into the kingdom/basileia/kingship of heaven. (TM)

         Clearly, in this context the whoever of v. 19 must be restricted to those who have entered the kingdom

interpretation but can allow the text to generate multiple vantage points if contextually justified. That said, not all
interpretations are valid, much less equally valid. For example, I am skeptical of the misthological entry being linear
(contra Destiny, 294). Notwithstanding, I am mindful that, although an aorist is used for enter in Mt 5:20, the aorist
is used in Lk 9:23 for take up, with daily being supplied by the CT. Quibbling about that point aside, however,
punctiliar entry into the eschatological kingship is a reasonable misthological understanding: Basileia spatially
means kingdom in 5:19, but experientially and eschatologically it means kingship in 5:20.
Page 52                                                                                   Misthological Models: Part 2
since the discussion of v. 19 is limited to those who are inside the kingdom. Misthologists should not overlook
the blue dimension of the text. At the same time, the contrast in v. 19 between being least and great within the
blue range of the kingdom (pictured by the blue kingdom sphere) demands a red versus green differentiation
within the text (pictured in the illustration by the red outer darkness and the green Reward City). Soteriologists
cannot escape the red versus green dimensions of the text and, therefore, should not object to a green coloring
of basileia within v. 20 as part of its intended meaning. Since the enter into (eiserchomai) of v. 20 is being
juxtaposed not only with the being inside (en) of v. 19 but also with the least/great of v. 19, the duality of
basileia is readily explained. As to the emphasis versus logical order of this duality in v. 20, the disciples are to
sense the misthological (green) nature of basileia first in v. 20. The emphasis is: kingship/basileia/kingdom.
But the soteriological aspect has been placed first in this highlighting for the logical order:
kingdom/basileia/kingship.92 Kingship within the kingdom is pictured by residency in the city, thus associated
with the crown. The mop has been used to picture janitorial service in the kingdom as a form of being least.
       Commentators generally focus on the duality of righteousness in Mt 5:19-20: your righteousness versus
their righteousness. That duality is there, to be sure, but that duality is not the only one present in the text.93
Basileia is being used in a dual capacity also. Surprisingly, though, Dillow questions this duality: “Surely, if
‘kingdom’ meant something different in verse 20 from what it meant in verse 19, there would be some textual
indicator. But there is none at all.”94 Actually, there is, and Dillow himself supplies it: “Paradoxically, this man
who is declared to be ‘in’ the kingdom does not, according to Matthew 5:20, possess the righteousness necessary
to “enter” it!95 “In Matthew 5:19 Jesus speaks of a man who is ‘in’ the kingdom but who clearly does not have
the surpassing righteousness required by verse 20 which is necessary to enter it.”96 Well said! The proper
conclusion, therefore, should be that since the text has given very clear indication that the man is “in” the
kingdom but fails to “enter” it, he obviously is inside the basileia in a different sense than that denoted by
entering it. In other words, the text is practically shouting that you can be in the basileia in one sense but fail to
enter it in another.
       The overall tenor of Dillow’s discussion would support this very conclusion. He immediately proceeds
to argue, correctly, that to enter the kingdom can mean to enter the kingship, and he poses that entrance into the
kingdom, in such a case, means precisely the same thing as being great in the kingdom.97 Splendid assessments!
And in the preceding analysis, Dillow acknowledged that it can have multiple aspects in the same context:
“Even though in a particular passage one aspect of the kingdom may be prominent, it is still acceptable
and also probable that several other aspects may be lurking in the background.”98 Exactly! Those least in
the kingdom will at least be in the kingdom, in one sense, although they do not have the righteousness necessary

      Using color codes to restate this important point, the green understanding of basileia is predominant in v. 20,

99
   Dillow acknowledges that believers already have entered the kingdom in terms of personal salvation: “Most
important, in each place where entering the kingdom of God is mentioned, the call is always to those who
have already entered it in the sense of personal salvation” (Destiny, 253). They “have already entered the
kingdom in a soteriological sense” (Destiny, 303). I am denoting this soteriological entry in blue text and with
blue highlights. Dillow is inconsistent to then turn around and say that they really have not entered the kingdom
because it is only future, but only have entered certain aspects of the kingdom experientially. He does much better
when he then says, “We agree with Baxter’s conclusion that ‘Matthew 5:20 does not seem to raise the question of
‘soteriology’ as much as it raises the question of ‘sanctification.’ In other words, it is not so much how to get into
the kingdom [in the sense of personal salvation] that is in view as much as it is how kingdom-dwellers ought to
conduct their lives” (Destiny, 260; insert his). From a neoclassical vantage point, I can agree with both Dillow and
Baxter that soteriological entry is secondary, but that does not mean that it is absent! Soteriological entry is not
stressed as much as misthological entry, but it is present. Hence, entry into the soteriological sphere of the kingdom
is a present reality, thereby indicating that the kingdom is soteriologically present. So, when Paul says that believers
already have been transferred into this kingdom, I have no problem affirming that believers are presently in the
kingdom in the soteriological sense (Col 1:13). Again, Dillow says the same thing, “When one is “born again” (John
3:3-5), he is transferred into the New Covenant, the foundation for the future millennium (Colossians 1:13). He
enters the kingdom in the sense of personal salvation” (Destiny, 249). We already have entered the kingdom
soteriologically in other words. Rather than being content with that assessment, however, Dillow insists that our
present “entering into the kingdom” does not mean that we enter into the kingdom presently, only that we “enter
now into salvation” (p. 249, italics his), that is, “we are currently in the sphere of personal salvation” (p. 252). Paul
has a name for this personal sphere of salvation, and he supplies it in Col 1:13. He calls it kingdom and says that
believers already have been transferred into it. I will retain Paul’s terminology. There is a sense in which we already
have entered the kingdom and another sense in which we have not. Kingdom is being used in two senses. In Mt
5:20, the soteriological sense is the secondary sense, but it is present nevertheless. Soteriologically it is now;
misthologically it is future. Moreover, I will even allow a present experiential entrance: See Illustration 88. Truth
Table for Mt 5:19-20, p. 128.
Page 54                                                                                    Misthological Models: Part 2
but the underlying blue bedrock is lying just below the surface and juxtaposes above the surface in v. 19. Green
entrance into the basileia means the exact same thing as being great within the blue spatial sphere of the kingdom.
Those least within the kingdom will not have the same entrance as those great within the kingdom. Nevertheless,
both will be in the kingdom and thus have a different type of entrance into the kingdom—a kingdom that is common
to both of them, denoted in blue in this schema, both by the arrow and sphere. As to the time frame involved for the
distinctions between being least and great and the time frame when believers will enter into that demarcation, all
three occurrences of will in Illustration 37. Color Coded Translation of Mt 5:19-20 should be understood
eschatologically. Those believers who are called least in the future kingdom, as a result of the Bema, will not have
the misthological righteousness necessary to enter the kingdom misthologically. Naturally, they will be in the
kingdom soteriologically (v. 19), thus having the requisite soteriological righteousness, but they will not enter the
kingdom misthologically (v. 20). Being great in the kingdom requires a matching misthological entrance. Those
who only enter the kingdom soteriologically will be least in the kingdom. Those who are called least will be inside
the kingdom, but not inside the kingship. In the diagram, they will be consigned to the lower section of the
soteriological sphere in Illustration 38. Duality of Basileia.
                                       Illustration 39. Kingdom within a Kingdom




       An even better way to describe the duality of this word picture Jesus has given us is to use two spheres,
a yellow mistholic one inside a blue soteric one. Let us also take two representative believers, one called Lester
(representing least) and the other called Gregory (representing great). So we have Lester the Least and Gregory
the Great. Lester, represented in red, is saved by the blood of the Lamb. Lester will be in the kingdom but fail
to enter the kingdom. How can that be? The answer to Jesus’ riddle is easily answered once it is realized that
Jesus is talking about a kingdom within a kingdom.100 All believers will be in the outer kingdom, but only

100
      More technically correct, a basileia within a basileia. I am simplifying this illustration sufficiently to the point
Chapter 1. New Jerusalem                                                                                       Page 55
faithful believers will enter the inner kingdom. So Lester is standing inside the blue sphere but outside the
yellow sphere because unfaithful believers will not enter the inner kingdom. Lester will only make it in the
outer part of the kingdom, that is, the outer darkness.101 Gregory (or Greg for short) will rule from the Reward
City. Lester will be ruled over in the outer darkness.

                                                      Mt 25:31-36
       Also on the chart, the pericope regarding the sheep and the goats in Mt 25:31-46 contrasts believers who
perform good works with unbelievers who do not perform good works. Good sheep are contrasted with bad goats
in other words. Carnal believers are not part of the contrast. They are completely absent from explicit consideration.
Neither good goats nor bad sheep are included in the contrast, naturally. After all, the purpose of the passage is to
make a contrast, not provide a full spectrum. Insisting that carnal believers must be present or they do not exist
would be as shortsighted as saying that the four-sided prism only has one side, just because you happen to be looking
through one side at that particular moment.102 The passage is not talking about believers and nonbelievers in mere
positional terms. Their deeds are also taken into consideration. Neither bad sheep nor good goats are in the passage.
Nevertheless, from the broader context of the Mt. Olivet Discourse, Matthew invites his readers to contemplate the
question, “What would happen to bad sheep in such a situation?” A correlative fate is apparent. Just as the bad goats
are told to depart into the eternal fire, so the bad sheep are cast into the outer darkness. The two fates are not
identical, by any means. But an obvious similarity exists.

                                                      Jn 5:28-29
       Likewise, Jn 5:28-29 concludes by saying, “Those who did the good deeds to a resurrection of life, those
who committed the evil deeds to a resurrection of judgment.” When translated in this fashion, good believers are
contrasted with bad unbelievers. Within the broad context of the NT where this passage is to be compared to other
similar juxtapositions, the point of the passage is to contrast ethical opposites.103 Jesus is making a generalization
to contrast the nature, actions, and destiny of two groups that are polar opposites. The antithetical intent is to
contrast the nature and actions of those whose actions manifest their true nature. That there are those within both
groups who mask their true nature is not dealt with in this particular passage. The affirmation of two poles is not
necessarily a denial that a range occurs between those poles. Just as the mention of two resurrections, without
affirmation of a thousand-year interlude, does not prove that such an interlude does not exist, so the mention of two
ethical poles does not prove the absence of an intermediate range.

heirship within an heirship, life within life, love within love, body within body, etc.

    As explained in John in Living Color (p. 32), within the limited context of GJ, I interpret Jn 5:29 differently than

access to other passages to generate a secondary misthological lens over the passage, I insert deeds or works into
the translation and interpret it in the manner I am in the present discussion, as having ethical ramifications in terms
of our performance. On a much smaller scale, one may consider Mt 7:15-23 (blue) as opposed to Mt 7:13-27 (green)
to be a microcosm of this approach. The larger context provides the misthological lens. Without the misthological
juxtapositions found in the broader context of the NT to provide the additional vantage point, Jn 5:29 should be
interpreted solely soteriologically. (Similar arguments might be made regarding Jn 3:5.)
Page 56                                                                                 Misthological Models: Part 2
                                    Illustration 40. Darkness may Overtake You




        Indeed, as pointed out in Darkness, John has his own allusion to the outer darkness in the broader context,
wherein Jesus lets out a piercing warning in which a Misthologist can hear Him even now shouting: “Run for your
lives! Get out of the darkness quickly while you still can! Run now! Step out in service to the Light, or you will
lose your life to the creature of darkness!” Jesus explicitly warned His followers that this misthological darkness
could take their lives (Jn 12:25-26). Later, He would picture misthological death as occurring when a believer dries
up and is cast into the fire (Jn 15:6). Here, He pictures it in no less frightening words, “Walk while you have the
light, that darkness may not overtake [katalambano] you; he who walks in the darkness does not know where he
goes” (Jn 12:35). Jesus practically points His finger at this creature of darkness that is straining against its chains:
a misthological avenger that wants to slay them and take their lives (cp. katalambano in Dt 19:6); an accursed
creature in that it invokes God’s curses upon His disobedient children (cp. katalambano in Dt 28:15); a beast that
seizes its victims with almost demonic frenzy as it causes them to weep and gnash their teeth (cp. katalambano in
Mk 9:18). In other contexts, Jesus even gave this creature a name—the outer darkness.
        How do you escape the misthological darkness in GJ? By being good, that is, by walking in the light. In
Jn 5:29 versus Jn 12:25, misthological death is juxtaposed with misthological life as it pertains to believers. As a
believer, your life—your eternal life—is at stake—misthologically. The soteriological dimension of your eternal
life cannot be affected, of course, which is another Johannine dualism. Life/death, light/darkness, and good/bad are
various dualisms posed by John to polarize the options: sometimes soteriologically, sometimes misthologically,
sometimes jointly. In Jn 5:29 itself, however, the good/bad contrast is seen in a green light, contrasting a
soteriological and misthological fate. Bad believers (i.e., carnal believers who do not abide in Christ or walk in the
light) are not in view. The judgment entailed in Jn 5:29 is for bad unbelievers, thus the Lake of Fire, not the outer
darkness, is the risk. The outer darkness lurks elsewhere in GJ. Even so, for those who can see neoclassically, the
shadow of the outer darkness may be seen even in Jn 5:29. A correlative judgment awaits those believers who do
evil.

                                                     Mt 13:24-50
        Thus far, we have seen the good sheep versus bad goats in Matthew and the evil unbelievers versus good
believers in John. They would be color coded in green, to contrast the soteriologically unregenerate who perform
badly as opposed to the misthologically regenerate who perform well. What about the good seed versus evil seed
or the good fish versus bad fish in Mt 13:24-50? Should they be color coded differently? Probably not, at least not
if one is going to think comprehensively. Although I insisted upon what herein would be a blue lens in my protracted
discussion of this passage in Darkness, a fuller perspective would, at the very least, allow that an antithetical
dimension is intended in Matthew’s contrast between the good seed (which perseveres in producing a harvest in the
field) versus the devilish seed (which perseveres in poisoning the field). The good behavior of the good seed that
produces a harvest is implicitly contrasted with the good seed that produces life but no harvest (Mt 13:20-22). In
other words, the good seed that produces a harvest in Mt 13:30 is the very seed that produced mature fruit in
Mt 13:23. The performance of the evil seed, conversely, is made explicit, in the form of the evil ones who are the
stumbling blocks and those who commit lawlessness (Mt 13:41). So, if one is looking singularly at Mt 13:24-30,
within the confines of the pericope, then it is green antithetically. The preceding context (the parable of the sower)
would be marked as a red color for all. However, that red color coding is best reserved for that self-contained
pericope of the sower. The parable of the wheat and tares goes back to focus on the latter part of the parable of the
sower and is thus limited to the green part of the spectrum.
Chapter 1. New Jerusalem                                                                                        Page 57
        As in the case of the sheep and goats of Matthew and the good versus bad of John, so here, in the parable of
the wheat and tares, the good versus bad seed contrasts a soteriological image with a misthological one. The same
juxtaposition can be inferred regarding good versus bad fish in Mt 13:47-50, where their negative behavior is
implied by the designations righteous versus wicked. Both their nature and performance are considerations, thus
making this another green passage. In a sense, the bad fish could be considered as being taken out of the kingdom
because the kingdom spatially includes the sea in which they were caught. Both the tares from the field and the bad
fish from the sea are thrown soteriologically into the fire—the Lake of Fire. The fate of both involves weeping and
gnashing of teeth (Mt 13:42,50). Although carnal believers are not included in either pericope, Matthew’s readers
will not fail to note that carnal believers share a correlative fate since Matthew does not fail to drive home the point,
in all three passages regarding the outer darkness, that the unfaithful believers are cast out of the feast and also weep
and gnash their teeth in the outer darkness (Mt 8:12; 22:13; 25:30). A correlation, not equalization, is intended.
Both the unregenerate in the Lake of Fire and the regenerate in the outer darkness will have reason to weep and
gnash their teeth. Some of those reasons are apparent; some are not as apparent.

      Gospel of Kingdom
        A fundamental difference between kingdom writers and non-kingdom writers can surface in discussing the
gospel of the kingdom versus the gospel of grace. In terms of soteriology and misthology, neolegalists tend to view
the gospel of the kingdom and the gospel of grace as one and the same, thus pictured as completely overlapping in
the illustration of their position. (See Illustration 41. Overlaps, 58.) Kingdom writers, in sharp contrast, perceive an
extremely important differentiation. At the opposite end of the spectrum from neolegalists, some kingdom teachers,
such as Chitwood, make such a sharp contrast so as to say that the gospel of the kingdom and the gospel of grace
are totally separate. No overlap is found between the gospel of grace and the gospel of the kingdom. Reasoning
from this premise, Chitwood concludes that because Mt 13 is dealing with the gospel of the kingdom no
soteriological concerns or soteric-mistholic juxtapositions are present. All the parables therein are dealing strictly
and exclusively with misthological issues. Consequently, within the Chitwoodian view, the first soil type within the
parable of soils is a picture of a believer. Continuing then in the next parable with the Chitwoodian perspective, the
tares are believers. The leaven likewise represents believers who are opposed to the kingdom message. The bad fish
cast into the fire represents the misthological fate of such believers. This extrapolation continues to the other
parables as well. Thus, according to Chitwood, the goats cast into the age-long (aionios) fire would represent
unfaithful believers suffering a misthological, millennial fate (Mt 25:41), namely, a thousand years of metaphorical
burn time with the devil.104 Further, because the tares represent the Christian religious leaders of our day, who
correspond to the Jewish religious leaders of Jesus’ day, the Pharisees and Sadducees must be considered regenerate
believers as well. After all, they, like the rest of the Jews, were trusting in the salvation typified by the sacrificial
lambs. Chitwood’s extrapolation, when followed through to its logical conclusion, results in considering Judas a
regenerate believer. Indeed, he would even insist that Judas will be a ruler in the coming kingdom for all eternity!105

104
    Chitwood provides several arguments that the goats represent regenerate believers. Supposedly, because the
sheep-and-goat passage is dealing solely with “realizing or not realizing an inheritance in the kingdom,” it cannot
be dealing with soteriology (p. 51, emphasis his). Admittedly, inheritance of the kingdom is a reward while entrance
into the kingdom is a gift. Nevertheless, the error in this part of his argument lies in thinking that soteriology cannot
be juxtaposed with misthology. The second part of his argument is that aionios cannot be used to refer to eternal
life when talking about rewards. Rather, it must be limited to talking about age-long life when used in a
misthological capacity. But this misperception on his part is tied directly with his claim that rewards are only
millennial in scope. Having made the erroneous deduction that rewards are limited to the millennial kingdom, he is
obligated, by his hermeneutical grid, to limit aionios life to the millennial kingdom. Of course, if rewards are eternal,
as I have demonstrated herein from his own arguments, then one need not try to force such passages into a
Chitwoodian straightjacket. The goats are unregenerate unbelievers cast into the eternal fire. Aside from standard
Greek lexicons bearing witness to the fact that aionios does, in fact, mean eternal in such passages and apart from
my own arguments in Darkness to that effect, I briefly will get to the heart of Chitwood’s lexical problem in Part 3
of this series without having to do a lot of lexical legwork. (Also see my book, RAE.) His argument is fundamentally
flawed at the conceptual level. Arlen L. Chitwood, The Most High Ruleth: A Study About World Government —
Past, Present, and Future, revised edition (Norman, OK: The Lamp Broadcast, Inc., 2013).
105
    Since Chitwood believes that Judas was a believer and that all believers will rule in eternity, his position leads

       A little reverse engineering on such extrapolations is in order. A more reasonable premise from which to
begin is that those who trust in their performance for soteriological entrance into the kingdom have failed to comply
with the gospel of grace. Most legalists and neolegalists never have complied with such entrance requirements.
They frontload or backload the gospel of grace. Most of our leaders in Christendom today are soteriological legalists
or neolegalists. Therefore, most of them never have met the requirements for regeneration. Since these modern-day
tares are lost, we should understand that the parabolic tares are lost. After all, since these modern-day leaders
opposing the kingdom depicted by the tares are, by and large, unregenerate legalists of one type or another, and
since they correspond to the opposition that Jesus encountered, the reasonable supposition is that the Pharisees and
Sadducees, as a general rule, were unregenerate disbelievers as well. Confirmation for this deduction comes in a
variety of forms, not the least of which is that Jesus says that they were unbelievers! Repeatedly, in soteriological
contexts Jesus explicitly says, “You do not believe” (Jn 3:12; 5:38,45-47; 6:64; 8:45; 10:26,38).
       On the whole, Chitwood’s argument that the tares necessarily represent regenerate believers is woefully
inadequate. On one hand, his postulation that the tares today represent the leadership of the church repressing
kingdom teaching just as the Pharisees of old did in Israel is certainly reasonable. But presuming that they
necessarily are regenerate is unreasonable. Their soteric trust in the false doctrine of works righteous would lead to
the opposite presupposition. Furthermore, Chitwood’s assessment that the new believers in Jn 8:31 were the ones
seeking to kill Jesus is ludicrous (Jn 8:40). A better argument could be made for Chitwood’s attempt to identify the
tares as regenerate believers from Jn 3:3-5, if Chitwood took a soteriological interpretation of that text which,
unfortunately, he does not. Even if he did, however, such an argument still would fall very short, notwithstanding
the attempt of some Arminians to make that very argument in their rejection of eternal security, so as to insist that
formerly regenerate believers can be cast out of the kingdom into hell, thereby losing their regeneration.106 At times
Chitwood almost seems to entertain the ultraistic perspective in which unfaithful believers will be cast out of the
kingdom and burn in the Lake of Fire for a thousand years. My arguments in Darkness still suffice to refute such a
position.107 To that rebuttal, the neoclassical treatment in the present work may be added.
       Additionally, it should be observed that not all ultraistic writers believe that the tares are regenerate. Even
though ultraists would have found this text to be a handy proof text for their kingdom-exclusion position, if they
thought the context justified it, they do not opt for an ultraistic interpretation of this passage. For Lang, for instance,

it goes without saying that the tares are unregenerate, as he gives multiple examples of unbelieving tares. Govett

the excesses of Chitwood’s discussion. Therefore, Hollandsworth’s treatment poses a much more intriguing vantage
point. Nevertheless, one need not suppose that it is the only vantage point intended. For that matter, it may not be
the primary vantage point. Instead, it might be the secondary vantage point intended by the parable. Indeed, one
could even argue that this vantage point is derived by means of permissible application rather than parabolic
intention. Even from this weakened perspective, though, the misthological application that Hollandsworth makes
can be read with great appreciation. I will proceed from this last perspective to demonstrate that, even in its weakest
form, the misthological applicability that Chitwood and Hollandsworth seek is permissible.
                                         Illustration 42. Carnivorous Sheep




       In considerable harmony with a Chitwoodian perspective, but more so by way of application than
interpretation, it may be said that a correlative judgment awaits the regenerate wheat that functions like tares, even
if the parable itself does not address the possibility of such functional tares. For a similar differentiation, consider
the distinction made in Darkness (p. 626) between the wolves in sheep’s clothing versus functional wolves in
sheep’s clothing, that is, believers who function like werewolves. Such believers may be included in the expositor’s
application even if they are not part of the intended interpretation.

      Application versus Interpretation
       Even in terms of minimum permissible application, Chitwood’s exposition can be read with great
appreciation. One does not have to interpret the tares to be regenerate believers in order to concur with the primary
thrust of Chitwood’s application. Christian leaders opposing the gospel of the kingdom are going to suffer severe
consequences, regardless of whether they are regenerate. Contrary to Chitwood, we may pose that neither Jesus nor
Matthew intend for us to limit the identity of such tares to the realm of regenerate believers. Darkness concluded
with a warning to soteriologists. Picturing soteriologists who are opposed to misthology as regenerate tares is
certainly not difficult. Deciding whether Matthew specifically intends to picture regenerate tares as a secondary
application is more difficult, however. Is this merely a permissible application, or is it an application specifically
108
    Lang, Parables, 72-81.
109
    Govett, “The Wheat and the Tares,” Parables, 5 (emphasis his), 8, 24.
110
    Chitwood, Mysteries, 43- 56.

    See Sons of the Wicked One, 35.
Page 60                                                                                  Misthological Models: Part 2
intended by the biblical writer? My working hypothesis, at this juncture, is that Matthew intends for us to see a
soteriological-misthological (i.e., blue-yellow) juxtaposition as his interpretation that, nevertheless, allows a
secondary correlative (thus green) application to so-called regenerate tares. If so, the tares are intended, at a
secondary applicatory level, to apply to regenerate believers who are opposed to the kingdom message. In terms of
proper interpretation, though, the tares picture unregenerate Christian leaders who are opposed to the gospel of the
kingdom. This neoclassical synthesis between Chitwood and Dillow is advisable for two reasons.
                                       Illustration 43. Secondary Application




       First, Matthew has written a neoclassical piece of literature that utilizes this secondary application for
unfaithful believers in other such antithetical juxtapositions between faithful believers and unbelievers. In some of
these parables, the secondary application, however, is arguably absent. Earlier, in Illustration 41. Overlaps, one
might get the mistaken impression that the only way to get to the subset is to pass through the outer shell. To the
contrary, in the parable of the wheat and the tares, Jesus, via Matthew, removes the application to the second and
third soil types from the explicit range being pictured, thus making such a stark contrast between the gospel-
spreading believers and the gospel-resisting unbelievers that no direct application is intended for any in-betweens.
Jesus slices out and removes the in-betweens in order to expose the soteric core, the gospel of grace. Nevertheless,
even though this parable is best pictured with the secondary application far removed, as in the right-hand side of
Illustration 43. Secondary Application, one can presume that the secondary application is still viable, as long as one
remembers that it is secondary, not primary, and not even really necessary.112
       Second, these parables are anticipatory of the modern period in which we now live. Accordingly, one would
anticipate that the parable would picture our current reality prophetically, and the reality of our situation is that the
message of the kingdom is opposed by both unregenerate legalists and regenerate antinomians. Contemporary FG
antinomians do not necessarily teach that believers should live anyway they please; rather, they just undercut the
reason for living morally by saying that the Bema is nothing more than an awards podium or an educational
experience. They oppose the judicially punitive nature of the Bema and are thus antibemians.113 Given, then, the
historical circumstances exhibited by the literature and the current circumstances prophetically anticipated by the
parable, proposing a correlative application to regenerate wheat that functions like tares, in terms of its opposition
to the kingdom message, is a reasonable neoclassical model. Chitwood’s applying this parable to regenerate
believers is thus well received herein, but his interpreting them to be regenerate believers is not adopted.

Jn 5:28-29 is so remote that one may have to leave GJ and go outside that book to the broader context of the NT to
make a convincing case for that application.

for Christ in terms of eternal rewards. On the other hand, although Chitwood would acknowledge the judicial nature
of the Bema, he is antibemian in that he denies the eternal nature of rewards and thus undermines the importance
of the Bema by limiting its results to 1000 years.
Chapter 1. New Jerusalem                                                                                          Page 61
Application must not be confused with interpretation. Thus, in Illustration 43. Secondary Application, my initial
impression of the parable of the wheat and tares is that the secondary interpretation is far removed and unnecessary,
as pictured on the right of that illustration. Nonetheless, for the two reasons mentioned here, I am willing to entertain
that one can put this slice back in place and make application to regenerate gospel-resisting believers who function
like tares, as pictured on the left in that illustration. Yet this slice has been shaded differently to remind us, myself
included, that this application is both secondary and unnecessary.
        The difference between interpretation and application can sometimes be surprisingly subtle. At a kingdom
study in which those present were discussing the parable of the sower, I used that parable to justify my belief that
fallen forces (i.e., fallen angels and demons) can read our thoughts, since in the first soil type Satan knows if we
understand or if we believe what we have heard (Mt 13:19; Lk 8:12).114 At that study, a friend of mine, Clint,
countered by asking if I believe that the first soil type represents believers. I replied that I do not. So he proposed
that demons can only read the thoughts of unbelievers. I had not thought of making that distinction for that
application even though I do make that distinction in my interpretation. One interesting component of that
interchange is that I was assuming that I could apply what was happening in the first soil type to believers even
though I did not believe that those being pictured in that soil type were believers. Of course, making applications
in this way is certainly biblical and hermeneutically reasonable. Notwithstanding, such applications can be
challenged if prudent grounds can be raised to take exception to the applications made in this manner.
        Coincidentally, or perhaps not so coincidently, perhaps as a synchronicity,115 the very next day I read in
Ahriman Gate by Thomas and Nita Horn where they twice took the same position taken by Clint—that demons
cannot read the minds of believers. But the oddity that I wish to stress is that I was insisting that the first soil type
is applicable to believers in terms of nikology (i.e., spiritual warfare) but not in terms of misthology. The
inconsistency of my applicability did not even occur to me until a couple of days later. Did Matthew intend for the
first soil type to represent regenerate believers? No, certainly not in my original assessment. But given my own
nikological applicability of this first soil type to believers regarding demonic mindreading, I should not have
assumed, without justification, that the text could not have misthological applicability to believers. My
inconsistency was not necessarily in splitting the applicability but in my failing to realize that I was splitting the
applicability. I cannot automatically rule out a misthological applicability to believers just because I do not interpret
the first soil type as portraying believers. I should have known better because I automatically assumed nikological
applicability to believers in the first soil type even though I do not believe that the first soil type pictures believers.
        Scripture and hermeneutics teach us to learn from the experience of others and to derive what applications
we can from their experiences. For example, Jude takes the destruction experienced by various groups—unbelieving
believers (Jude 1:5), fallen angels (Jude 1:6), and unbelievers (Jude 1:7)—and apparently applies these texts to both
unbelievers and believers alike as a warning that they too can experience a corresponding fate. Hermeneutically,
though, Jude is not saying that they literally would experience the exact same fate. Properly interpreting a given
passage as talking about an unregenerate person does not rule out the possibility of making a corresponding
application to a regenerate person or vice versa. Even though I was not inclined to think that Matthew intended a
misthological application within the first soil type, I should not have presumed, merely on that basis, that a
misthological application cannot properly be made. The distinction between an intended application and a
permissible application must be noted. Permissible applications are many; intended applications tend to be very
few. Since Hollandsworth’s misthological treatment avoids some of the pitfalls that plagues Chitwood’s discussion,
I would now be open to consider the possibility that Matthew may have intended a secondary misthological
application of the first soil type. The secondary application may not be as far removed as I originally thought.
Indeed, Jesus and Mathew may have intended it.
        The gospel of the kingdom, accordingly, as pictured by Matthew ch. 13, revolves around a soteriological-
misthological juxtaposition. Picturing the gospel of grace as completely separate from the gospel of the kingdom,
as Chitwood does, is questionable. At its core the gospel of the kingdom may be pictured as soteriological in order
to generate such soteric-mistholic juxtapositions as found in this context, the juxtapositions occurring between the
soteriological core of this gospel and its outer misthological shell. Matthew has, as it were, cut a portion of the
misthological shell away in the parable of the wheat and the tares so that we clearly can see the contrast between
the soteriological core and misthological shell of the gospel of the kingdom. One does not have to go through the


114
      See C. S. Lovett, Dealing with the Devil, 36-37.
115
      For a discussion and example of synchronicity, see Horn, Exo-Vaticana, 496-498.
Page 62                                                                                    Misthological Models: Part 2
misthological shell to get to the soteriological core. Matthew has exposed the core and laid it bare, yet not isolated
the soteric core from the remaining mistholic shell. The core is still within the shell.116
       In GJ, on the other hand, John purposefully removes most of the outer shell so that he can focus attention on
the soteriological core of the gospel of the kingdom. With such a concentrated focus on the smaller core, it is not
surprising that John does not refer to the gospel of the kingdom anywhere in GJ. After all, one does not remove the
core of an apple from an apple and then display the core as being an apple. The soteriological core of the gospel of
the kingdom is that of soteriological entrance into the kingdom. The soteric core of the gospel of the kingdom is no
more the gospel of the kingdom than the core of an apple is an apple. Try handing an apple core to your friends and
asking them if they would like to eat your apple! They quickly will point out that an apple core does not an apple
make. Sensibly, John does not use the phrase gospel of the kingdom or the word gospel anywhere in GJ because he
is not dealing with the gospel as such, but generally focusing only on the core issue, the promise of eternal life as a
free gift. Just as one does not hand an apple core to one’s friends and pretend to have offered them a Golden
Delicious apple or an apple, so John does not pretend to be offering the gospel of the kingdom or the gospel when
he presents his thematic core message, that whoever ever believes in Jesus for eternal life has it forever and ever.
This message of life, otherwise known as the gospel of grace, is a part of the gospel of the kingdom, just as the apple
core is part of the apple, but the core is not to be confused with the gospel as a whole.
       The focus of the kingdom message is normally on the outer mantel, where the meat of that message is stored,
not on the soteric core, where the seeds of regeneration are found. Or to speak in terms of a baseball analogy, one
cannot get to home base if one does not first reach first base. But one does not stop at first base and talk as if one
has reached home base. Nor does one normally focus on first base, but on winning the game by getting to home
base. In GJ, John takes the role of a first base coach, which has its place. Getting to first base is certainly an important
and necessary part of the game. But in the rest of his writings (e.g., his epistles), he focuses on subsequent matters,
and in Revelation his focus is on reaching the home plate. Similarly, one cannot rule a kingdom if one does not
even qualify to enter it. Yet one does not stop at entering a kingdom and presume that one has reached the goal of
ruling that kingdom by virtue of merely having entered the kingdom. Reasonably, the gospel of the kingdom
includes the first-base instruction as a necessity, telling how to enter the kingdom, but the goal is home-base
rulership, which is the typical focus of the NT discussion.

       Not Taken Out
       Like Chitwood, Dillow rejects the proposition that the parable of the tares proves that there is a present
mystery form of the kingdom in which the wheat and tares grow together. 117 By posing that the future millennial
kingdom is in view, Dillow tries to circumvent the clear wording of the text, which states that the tares are taken
out of [ek] His kingdom (Destiny, 264-265). One would think that such a position would put the tares inside the
millennial kingdom, though, since the parable places the tares within the kingdom. Yet Dillow also rejects this
parabolic detail. For example, he quotes Nolland in defense of his proposition that the rooting out does not prove
that they were ever rooted in the kingdom. Preferring an inductive approach myself, I will not run roughshod over
these parabolic details but rather ask, with Hendriksen, “How can they be ‘gathered out’ if previously they were not
inside, in this case inside the church visible?”118


116
    Alternatively, the gospel of the kingdom might be pictured as the core and the gospel of grace as the outer shell.
See Illustration 39. Kingdom within a Kingdom, 54. This inverse hermeneutical representation would be best for
Hollandsworth’s model. One has to enter the kingdom soteriologically before one can begin to qualify to enter it
misthologically. Thus, placing the soteriological shell on the outside makes sense. From Hollandsworth’s
perspective, Matthew is focused on the misthological core, thus deeper matters, not the soteriological shell. John
would be focused on introductory matters. First base would be better pictured as the outer skin of the apple. In other
words, the gospel of grace deals with introductory material. The gospel of the kingdom would deal with more
advanced material.

Chapter 1. New Jerusalem                                                                                           Page 63
       Whether the kingdom in this passage represents professing Christendom is open to discussion, but trying to
maintain that the tares never were in the kingdom in some sense makes no sense in view of the parabolic details.
As to Nolland’s argument, its explanation self-destructs: “For a Son of Man who has a kingdom the background is
clearly Dn. 7:13-14....something future.”119 Nolland, therefore, supposes that the kingdom in question is exclusively
future, leading to Dillow’s argument, but Nolland’s interpretation is at odds with the two passages themselves. The
kingdom in Dan 7:13-14 is a kingdom the Son of Man will have, not something He presently has. Yet the parable
indicates that the tares are presently in the kingdom since they are gathered out of it. They are gathered out of the
present form of the kingdom, not the future kingdom, so that they cannot enter into the future form of the kingdom.
       Appreciably, in connection with Jesus’ promise in Jn 6:37, that He will never cast out the one who comes to
Him, Dillow approvingly quotes my assessment: “The one who does not come to Christ cannot be cast out from a
relationship with Christ because he never came into a relationship with Christ from which he could be cast out! As
Marty Cauley says, ‘An object has to be in a container before it can be cast out of the container.’”120 Very good, we
concur.121 You cannot be cast out of something if you were never in that something. In Jn 6:37, what is that
something? It is a saving relationship with Christ, as Dillow correctly has surmised. In Mt 13:41, what is that
something? It is the kingdom, as Matthew clearly has stated. Dillow is being inconsistent in posing that they are
gathered out of the kingdom without ever first having been in the kingdom. The tares cannot be gathered out of a
kingdom in which they were never present.
       Equally unconvincing is Chitwood’s attempt to circumvent the obvious by saying that what is at stake is
simply “entrance into or exclusion from the kingdom” (emphasis his).122 Not so! The parable deals with both
extraction and exclusion! The tares will be gathered out of His kingdom, as Jesus plainly says, and then cast into
the furnace of fire (Mt 13:41-42), and thereby excluded from the future form of the kingdom. Extraction proceeds
exclusion. The tares are excluded from the future form of the kingdom in which “the righteous will shine forth as
the sun in the kingdom of their Father” (Mt 13:43), to be sure, but first they are extracted from the form of the
119
    Nolland, Matthew, NIGTC, 560.
120
    Dillow, 963.
121
    In this assessment, Dillow and I are in agreement against those who, like Lenski, say that, in relation to the sons
of the kingdom cast out into the outer darkness in Mt 8:12, this does “not necessarily imply that these persons were
actually in the kingdom. One may be ‘thrown out’ when he attempts to enter a place without ever getting into it”
(Matthew, 320). Conveniently, Lenski fails to give any proof that this is the case, much less that it is Matthew’s
intent. As demonstrated in Darkness, “you have to be in something before you can be cast out of it” (p. 432).
English readers are encouraged to look up the word out, as used in the Gospel of Matthew, by consulting a biblical
concordance so that they observe for themselves that Dillow and I are standing on the high ground in asserting you
have to be in before you can be cast out and that Lenski’s proposal is groundless. The guest in the wedding feast
was in the feast before he was cast out of it (ekballo, Mt 22:13). This interpretation is in complete harmony with
Mt 8:11-12, where those being cast out (of the feast) are those present at the feast. Consequently, Dillow’s assertion,
that being gathered out does not demand that one first be in, is equally groundless. Accordingly, I agree with Lenski
(Matthew, 539), against Dillow, that in Mt 13:41 ‘‘out of his kingdom refers to the kingdom as it now exists in the
world.” Note the cast-out-into (ekballo-eis) contrast in all three passages regarding the outer darkness (Mt 8:12;
22:13; 25:30): Out (ek) proceeds into (eis). Sequentially, unfaithful believers are cast out of the feast and then
thrown into the darkness outside. Correlatively, in Mt 13:41-42, the tares are gathered out (ek) of the kingdom and
cast (ballo) into (eis) the fire (ek...ballo eis). The feast is not the kingdom; the fire is not the darkness; and the tares
are not believers. Nevertheless, the unfaithful sons and unregenerate tares suffer a correlating fate.
        This was my original assessment before Hollandsworth introduced a modified model as a synthesis of
Chitwood’s thesis that the tares are saved and punished temporarily in relation to the kingdom and my antithesis
that the tares are lost and punished eternally and that rewards pertain to both the kingdom and the city.
Hollandsworth proposes that the tares are punished permanently by being cast out of the kingdom of Heaven (i.e.,
out of the Heavenly City at the Bema) while remaining in the kingdom of God. His thesis is in full accord with the
better misthological models being developed in this series. The only thing left to quibble about regarding his model
is whether it has correlative, primary, secondary, or exclusive explanatory power. My present preference would be
for either correlative or secondary applicability.

kingdom that precedes that future form. The righteous also are extracted from the present form of the kingdom,
yet not for soteriological exclusion, rather for misthological transition. In this particular context, His kingdom (v. 41)
is the present mystery form of the kingdom, in which both tares and wheat grow together, while the kingdom of
their Father (v. 43) is the future misthological form of the kingdom. The kingdom of heaven (Mt 13:11,24)
comprises both the mystery and subsequent misthological forms of the kingdom.

      Everyone
        Morris equates the kingdom with the people of the world: “It clearly denotes the entire population of this
inhabited globe....Satan then brings evil people into God’s good world.”124 France likewise thinks that because the
field is not the church, but the world, “the parable has a wider perspective than simply the professing discipleship
community. Within ‘the world’ believers and unbelievers continue to exist side by side.”125 Carson also rejects the
common proposal that the kingdom is the professing church: “This is not the church...for Jesus’ reign after the
Resurrection extends to the farthest reaches of the universe (28:18).”126

      Professors
        Actually, although the above writers are correct that the field is all-encompassing, even to the point of
including the whole universe, their simple equation (field = word = kingdom) of the field/kingdom with the whole
population of Earth is open to question. Gaebelein takes a more restricted approach: “The field is not the Church,
but the world, and it is in the world that this place; in that part of the field where the good seed has been sown, in
the entire sphere of professing Christendom.”127 Wilson puts it this way: “All children of the devil are lost, but not
all the lost are children of the devil. The tares are individuals who have heard the true gospel, understood it, but
have rejected it. Having rejected the true gospel, somehow, sometime, somewhere, they hear a false gospel, the
gospel of Satan, believe it, accept it, and thereby become children of the devil.”128 Borland concurs: “The kingdom
of heaven must refer to the [professing] church, the mystery form of the kingdom....The field is the world, not the
church.”129 MacArthur states similarly that the field is “the world, not the church. This is a picture of the church in
the world, not the world in the church” (emphasis his).130 Wilkins says that although the wheat and tares coexist “in
this world, there is secondary application to the church.”131
        This is the type of position taken in Darkness. The tares are rejecters of true gospel who, in turn, become
infectors of the church, falsely professing to be believers. Restated in other terms, the field spatially is Earth, but
the wheat and tares within the field metaphorically (i.e., numerically) represent the sphere of influence within the
Earth comprising professing Christendom: true and false believers alike. The spatial versus numerical dualism is
suggested by the imagery of the parable itself. The field includes the dirt (a spatial dimension) and the items growing
in the field (a numerical dimension). The wheat and tares are within the field. They are part of the field, not the
totality of the field.

kingdom with the city. But if that equation is granted, then the extraction is no problem.

        Practicing Professors
       Some writers, while holding to the same spatial view, take an even narrower view of the numerical dimension.
Lenski, for example, equates the field with the world but not with all the people in the world, not even all professing
believers. In this passage, son “has the same idea of legal right and inheritance. These people are not merely the
unregenerate in the world, sinners as they were by nature.”132 Instead, they are the ones who have heard and rejected
the gospel and now oppose it. Chitwood’s discussion in this regard is excellent, though I would insist, counter to
him, that they are, primarily, unregenerate wolves in sheep’s clothing within the church. Just as in this context the
sons of the kingdom represent the mature wheat who promote kingdom teaching, and thereby qualify for kingdom
inheritance, so the sons of the wicked one are those who just as thoroughly oppose it. In this regard, Phillips likewise
believes it refers spatially “to the created world, the planet Earth.” But the parable “deals with three groups of
people: the children of God, the children of the devil, and lost humanity among whom they are sown....All lost
people have a sin nature, but Satan’s children have something else—they have his nature.”133 This more precise
model is the one being entertaining in the present discussion.
       Restated in other terms, the field is spatially the Earth, but these wheat and tares are the mature representations
of both types of plant. They stay in the field until harvest and are called sons. Metaphorically/numerically, they
represent active adherents of Christendom. In other words, the wheat and tares are practicing Christians—public
followers of Christ—not necessarily genuine believers. They represent practicing professing Christendom. As to
their removal, Govett puts it this way: “The field is then entirely cleared both of wheat and of tares. Christianity,
whether in reference to its true subjects, or its false professors, is removed from off the earth, never more to appear.
The field is never resown.”134 The Father’s ultimate objective for producing Christianity (i.e., for the sowing) is to
produce a Bride for Christ. Once this Bride has been procured, there is no more need for Christianity. The shadow
will be replaced by the substance.
                            Illustration 44. Spatial and Numerical Options for the Field
      1. Spatially, the field would be the cosmos.
         i) Minimally, the cosmos would be the planet Earth.
         ii) Maximally, the cosmos would be this universe.
      2. Numerically, the wheat and tares would be the people who are within the cosmos.
         i) Maximally, the wheat and tares would be everyone.
         ii) Intermediately, the wheat and tares would be professing Christians.
         iii) Minimally, the wheat and tares would be practicing professing Christians.

        Summary
       There are two basic options for the spatial range of the field. It could be the Earth or the entire cosmos, the
universe. The latter is most probable. There are three basic options for the people in the field: everyone, all
professing Christians, or only those professing Christians who put their Christianity into practice. In the latter case,
they look like Christians, but many of them are not actually believers.
       The first option is implausible. The second option is plausible. The third option is much more probable.
Therefore, the working premise taking herein is that the field represents the visible universe, which houses Earth,
and the wheat and tares are the committed Christians within that sphere. Thus, the tares are professing Christians
who put their Christianity into practice, but this does not necessarily mean that they are born-again Christians. Many
false Christians are certainly committed! The Bible acknowledges the problem of antichrists (1Jn 2:18,22; 4:3;
2Jn 1:7). However, antichrists can refer to those opposed to Christ or to false Christs. Antichrists are diligent in
both capacities. Likewise, anti-Christians can refer to those opposed to Christ or to false Christians who masquerade
and act like genuine Christians.

the local church. Hendriksen likewise does not see an irresolvable conflict between church discipline and the
application of toleration to the local church.
