Page 66                                                                                 Misthological Models: Part 2
                                          Illustration 45. Taren and Greg




       The antithetical contrast between the wheat and the tares does not deal with carnal Christians. Unfaithful
believers are not included in this particular parable. To be sure, carnal Christians were pictured in the parable of the
sower as the intermediate range. That parable dealt with four possible responses to the gospel of the kingdom and
used two types of carnal believers to picture the middle spectrum. The parable of the wheat and tares, in contrast,
leaves out such middle men in order to create an antithetical juxtaposition between unbelievers who imitate the
Christian faith and genuine believers who hold fast the Christian faith. Mature regenerate Christians are gathered
into the barn, the Reward City. The unregenerate Christians who imitate them are cast into the fire.
       To facilitate portraying this contrast in the illustration, a new figure is introduced: Taren the Tare (pictured
in black). According to the antithetical understanding of the text, the soteriological dimension of the kingdom is
assumed but not pictured by Jesus in this parable. The kingdom out of which Taren is taken is not the soteriological
kingdom, but a mystery form of the kingdom in which unregenerate and regenerate Christians work in the same
field, work in the same mill, and lie in the same bed (cp. Mt 24:40-41; Lk 17:34-36). At the time of the rapture,
genuine believers are taken, counterfeit believers are left behind. The Taren-Greg contrast acts similarly in that it
leaves out the blue sphere. A stark polarization is being made, not merely between unbelievers and believers, as in
the rapture passage just considered, where good works are not a factor, but a contrast is made that silently presumes
that unstated soteriological premise in order to move beyond it to include the behavior of the respective unbeliever
versus believer. Taren is transparently an unregenerate person who imitates mature, genuine believers. Certainly,
he has good works; otherwise, he would not look like wheat. He represents the religious lost who, for the time
being, are swelling the Christian ranks.
Chapter 1. New Jerusalem                                                                                       Page 67
                                      Illustration 46. Taren, Lester, and Greg




       Nevertheless, in order to give a full perspective that includes carnal believers, these summary illustrations of
the wheat and the tares will incorporate the illustrations from above, regarding being least and greatest in the
kingdom. Therefore, for sake of continuity with that earlier discussion, Lester the Least (pictured in red) will be
pictured in-between the juxtaposition between Gregory the Great (pictured in Green) and Taren the Tare (pictured
in black).135 Taren looks like a believer on the outside, but if you look at his inner nature, it is transparent that he
never had received the gift of eternal life. Taren gives many expositors a fit, even to the point that some deny that
he is in the kingdom. Yet, the more reasonable supposition is that he is inside the kingdom, in some sense of the
word. To assume otherwise would make virtual nonsense of the biblical picture of his being taken out of the
kingdom. Thus, Taren is best portrayed as presently being outside the soteriological sphere of the kingdom but
inside the mysterious sphere of the kingdom. He never entered the blue sphere, so he is pictured as standing outside
of it because of his lack of saving faith, but he is standing inside the transparent sphere of the kingdom because of
his Christian works.

135
    Since I am ascribing secondary applicability or at least correlative plausibility to Hollandsworth’s model, I
include his argument as grounds for including Lester within the discussion. Lester is a mature believer who does
not believe the message of rewards and therefore opposes the kingdom message and thus is excluded from the
kingdom rulership and extracted from the Heavenly City at the time of the Bema. Just as the universe will be free
of unregenerate tares when they are removed from the kingdom, the Heavenly City will be free of regenerate tares
when they are removed from the kingship.
Page 68                                                                                Misthological Models: Part 2
                           Illustration 47. The Mystery Sphere Inside the Earth Sphere




        The wheat and tares numerically comprise that mystery kingdom and are within the spatial Earth. So the
illustration takes the Earth, makes a transparency out of it, and places the mystery form of the kingdom inside of it
(and the soteriological and misthological forms of the kingdom inside of the mystery form) to help picture the subset
relationships. Thus, from inside out the composite result is:

                     Misthological kingdom ⸦ soteriological kingdom ⸦ mystery kingdom ⸦ Earth

These relationships are pictured as subsets, with the smallest listed first. But we are not finished yet. The field is
only part of the kingdom of Heaven, and the field is bigger than the Earth.

                        Illustration 48. The Mystery Sphere inside the Spherical Universe
Chapter 1. New Jerusalem                                                                                      Page 69
       Limiting Matthew’s perspective, much less Jesus’ perspective, to this planet as the extent of the cosmos is
unnecessary, however. Isaiah foretold of a new heavens and new Earth (Is 65:17; 66:22). Peter acknowledges,
“According to His promise we are looking for new heavens and a new earth, in which righteousness dwells”
(2Pet 3:13). Perhaps the only promise Peter has in mind is the OT background from Isaiah. Then again, why think
that Jesus had anything less in mind when He promised that the tares would be taken out of the present cosmos?
Would He not be anticipating this as a preparatory event to get ready for the new heavens and new Earth which
would be free of tares? The more reasonable proposal, then, is that Jesus and Matthew anticipate the removal of the
tares as part of God’s plan to prepare a new heavens and new Earth that will be tare free. Peter may have had this
parable in mind as an auxiliary passage when he quoted Isaiah. “His promise” of a universe free of sin logically
would include a field free of tares. The working hypothesis adopted herein, accordingly, is that the parable
anticipates a sin free universe with its tare free field. Therefore, the illustration has placed the field within our
spherical universe.
                        Illustration 49. The Mystery Sphere inside the Spherical Universe




       Picturing the tares as taken out of the universe but left within the sphere of Heaven would make little sense,
though. Naturally, one would anticipate that the tares not only are taken out of the universe but also out of the
heavenly realm containing the universe. Such a proposal is sensible in view of Isaiah’s anticipation of the sin free
heavens. Would Isaiah conceive of sin being removed from the realm of the first two heavens but not the third
Heaven? Of course not! Sin will be removed from the spherical domain of all three heavens: the atmosphere of
Earth, the universe, and the outer heavenly sphere. All three are heavens, as seen in the illustration. Interestingly
enough, in the Greek text the plural form heavens is used. It is called the kingdom of the heavens. Might a multiverse
be suggested or at least the three heavens implied? Anyway, although Taren is numbered among the plants growing
in the field, it must be concluded that when he is extracted from that number, and thereby removed numerically
from that sphere of reference, he is removed spatially from all three heavens in order to be cast into the Lake of Fire
outside of the realm of Heaven. So, when Jesus explains that His angels will gather the tares out of His kingdom
(Mk 13:41), we should understand Him to mean that the tares will be removed from this heavenly sphere in its
entirety and cast outside that sphere, where they were previously, to an outside sphere subsequently. Conversely,
the wheat is taken out of the field and placed into the barn (the Heavenly City). The wheat is not taken out of
Heaven. Rather, the “righteous,” that is the fruitful wheat gathered into the barn, “will shine forth as the sun in the
kingdom” (Mt 13:43), meaning the kingdom of the heavens (Mt 13:11,24,31,33,44-45,47,52). The rewarded
righteous believers will have full access to all three heavens. The unrighteous (unregenerate) tares will not be found
anywhere in that range, having been gathered out of the kingdom (of the heavens).
                                             MACC versus MTET
      In a number of the preceding diagrams, I have been content to depict the outer darkness spatially in
Crawfordian terms as the outer courtyard without specifying its location relative to Earth. Regardless of which form
of the Crawfordian model is adopted, the original or revised, a notable component of the Crawfordian model
regarding the present discussion is that the outer darkness will be childless. For Dillow this may be a small
Page 70                                                                                   Misthological Models: Part 2
consideration since he seems to pose a sterile, childless eternity anyway. But Crawford and I both propose a vibrant,
growing humanity. We can look forward to teaching and interacting with children as they are born throughout
eternity to those in physical bodies. A Heaven without children is not the Heaven I imagine. To the contrary, one
reason the fallen angels did not remain in their own domain but abandoned their proper abode (Jude 1:6) appears
to be because they wanted to reproduce, as much so as possible, beings in their own likeness. In other words, they
wanted to get in on the act of having children.136 They were willing to risk damnation to have children. They left a
childless heavenly domain to come to an earthly domain where there are children. They found an Earth with children
more desirable than a Heaven without children. As their punishment, they were told “they will witness the slaughter
of their beloved ones, and will weep over the destruction of their children” (1En 12:6). These fallen angels loved
their children. Let those believers who fail to serve the Lord contemplate the fact that they may end up in a place
and state that fallen angels considered so undesirable by comparison that they were willing to risk Hell. They were
on the outside looking in at the wonders of reproduction. In MACC (Multiverse Alcorn-Crawfordian-Caulian) those
in the outer darkness are literally on the outside looking in as well, watching from the heavenly perimeter to observe
the universes within the multiverse populated with children. Unfaithful believers will miss out on interacting with
upcoming generations, forever isolated from a growing, thriving humanity.
        As attractive as MACC is for a competing model of the outer darkness, my original model presented in
Darkness remains a live option. Therein I hypothesized that when the Lord comes from the heavenly outer realm
with the Heavenly City and casts unfaithful believers into the outer darkness, the outer darkness will be in the
southern hemisphere of Earth and expand subsequently through our universe. Thus, the outer darkness is initially
in a terrestrial (T) location and then moves to an extraterrestrial (ET) location. This TET (terrestrial-
extraterrestrial) model shows the migratory location of the outer darkness. The only clarification added herein to
that model is that the Heavenly City currently resides in a separate heavenly universe. Therefore, the designation
M for multiverse would be added to show the current location of the Heavenly City, so my model would now be
MTET (Multiverse terrestrial-extraterrestrial), with the first letter (M) showing both the current location of the
Heavenly City and the last three letters (TET) representing the future location of the outer darkness. Contrastively,
in MACC the first letter represents both the current location of the Heavenly City and the future location of the
outer darkness. The future location entails a return to the outer realm of the celestial sphere. Therefore, the last three
letters of MACC perform double duty: the ACC represents the last-name of three writers whose models have been
synthesized to produce this composite, and the CC emphatically stresses that the outer darkness is in a celestial
location.
        In my MTET model, in contrast, those who are cast into the outer darkness will be around children. Yet it is
not necessarily a rosy picture. Such subcomers could serve as janitors, mopping floors in shopping malls of remote
galaxies within our universe, being trampled underfoot by children in the sense of mopping up the dirt from their
feet. In Mt 5:13, subcomers are trampled underfoot by people misthologically. Mopping seems to be a good way to
picture this literally. In MACC, though, there would be little if any involvement with children. Perhaps, occasionally
a class of students might take a fieldtrip to the outer limits of the heavenly realm and visit a subcomer as part of
their history class. Or possibly students might visit that region as part of theological class, studying the ramifications
of misthology. Regardless of which model is preferred—MTET or MACC—a literal understanding of the Heavenly
City and a spatial understanding of the outer darkness is maintained.

                                                      Literalicity
        Speaking very rudimentarily, my MTET and MACC models might be considered roughly a synthesis of the
literal position taken by Alcorn (in regard to the nature of Heaven and rewards) with the clarification provided by
Dillow (in distinguishing a gift from a reward in regard to soteriology and misthology). Both of these writers are
excellent in their respective areas. Additionally, Alcorn can distinguish a gift from a reward in his book on rewards,
and Dillow well expresses literal scenarios, although he unfortunately takes them fictionally. As to the literalicity
of the Heavenly City, Alcorn correctly argues for a literal city.
        For one thing, the Bible says that Abraham “was looking for the city which has foundations, whose architect
and builder is God” (Heb 11:10). The context confirms that what he and other such like-minded individuals desired
was “a better country, that is a heavenly one. Therefore God is not ashamed to be called their God; for He has
prepared a city for them” (Heb 11:16). The most natural supposition is that what they were seeking was “Mount
Zion...the city of the living God, the heavenly Jerusalem” (Heb 12:22). Ruckman challenges this position in his
comments on Eze 48:35, saying that this proposal would cause a problem in that a city 1,500 miles square would
completely cover the Holy Land:

      The problem arises by pretending the “city” for which Abraham “looked” (Heb. 11:10) was “the heavenly
      Jerusalem” (Heb 11:8-9)....He wasn’t “called” to go to or “promised” Heaven (see Gen. 12:1, 13:14-18,
      15:18-21). The “they” of Hebrews 11:14-16 are saved people in the Church Age who are like Abraham in
      that they have faith in what God said (Rom. 4:1-25) and seek another “country”—a “heavenly” one with
      a heavenly city.137 (Emphasis his.)

I am indebted to Keith Hall for bringing this citation to my attention.138 As I shared with Hall, I am highly skeptical
that the they of Heb 11:14-16 does not apply to the OT heroes of faith, in which case Ruckman’s point is nullified.
Abraham was seeking for both: earthly land and a heavenly city. He was to gain both by sojourning in the former.
As to Eze 48:35, this passage is, of course, dealing with the millennial, earthly Mount Zion. However, it will point
typologically to the eternal Mount Zion.
                                                   Pyramidal City
       Abraham was seeking for this city, so he had to know something about it. Hall was assisting me in my
research regarding the possibility of early cultures being aware of the pyramidal reward city. He had suggested that
I consult Heron’s book, The Nephilim and the Pyramid of the Apocalypse. Heron’s thesis is that the Great Pyramid
in Egypt was built by fallen Nephilim. In turn I emailed Heron:

             I enjoyed your book, The Nephilim and the Pyramid of the Apocalypse. I am working on the thesis
      that Abraham was looking for the heavenly city (per Hebrews) as described in Revelation. Of course, if this
      city is pictured by pyramids built by the Nephilim and Abraham was aware of this, as seems reasonable,
      then this would collaborate this perspective. I am reading some early cosmologies now and have gotten
      Heaven’s Mirror which you cited. Any further recommendations you might make in that regard would be
      appreciated. I would be particularly interested in the possibility that the Heavenly City was originally
      located above this planet and any early cosmologies or mythologies that would confirm that early humanity
      was able to see the city.139

       Heron responded: “I don’t know about the Heavenly City being above this planet as that’s not in the Bible. I
think Abraham was looking forward to seeing that City just as we are.”140 Heron had spent several pages in Pyramid
defending the proposition that Abraham was looking for this literal, celestial city to the north of this planet.141 Even
if my hypothesis is incorrect that the Heavenly City was originally observable, my thesis that Abraham and other
early people had some knowledge of this Heavenly City may still be correct. Their knowledge of this city could

             In Graham Hancock’s book Heaven Mirror (page 66), he quotes from the Edfu Texts, which

Heron explains at length the Luciferian background of the pyramid and then concludes: “It is not unreasonable to
equate the Great Pyramid of Giza, in its original glory, with the New Jerusalem, as described by John in chapter 21
of the Book of Revelation. Thus I believe these ancient pyramids are copies of the City of God which in the future
is to come down out of Heaven: the Pyramid of the Apocalypse” (bold his).143 But why would Satan build such
an imitation? Heron hypothesizes, “All of the pyramids are counterfeit imitations constructed by Satan and his
Nephilim comrades as alters to their own pride, an earthly reflection of a heavenly reality.”144 As Heron notes, the
star shafts of these pyramids point to Draco the Dragon (i.e., Satan) and Osiris and Isis, which is a common
understanding. Not surprisingly, these Luciferian imitations of the Heavenly City have been constructed to point to
Lucifer.
       A competing theory by Hutchings, however, attributes the building of the Great Pyramid to Enoch rather than
to the Nephilim. The answer to the riddle, “What pillar in Egypt can be found in its midst but nevertheless at its
border that will become a sign and a witness to the Lord?” finds its answer in the Great Pyramid (Is 19:19-21), set
there as a sign and wonder by the Lord (Jer 32:17,20). This wonder of the world is a witness to the Lord. Attributing
this witness to Enoch rather than the Nephilim certainly seems more intuitive. Hutchings follows Clarence Larkin
and J. A. Seiss in attributing this witness of Enoch (a.k.a. Cheops). One reason why this pyramid does not have a
mummified body, whereas the other pyramids do, is because Enoch was taken by God before he died. “Enoch was
translated so that he would not see death, and the patriarch’s escape from death could have prompted others to
attempt to duplicate his transition.” However, “the spiritual truths embodied within it were corrupted in the lesser
pyramids and tombs.”145 “Every temple, tomb, and pyramid in Egypt manifested an effort to obtain eternal life
through works.”146 Quoting Heb 11:5-6, Hutchings rejects this corruption with splendid soteriology:

             “By faith Enoch was translated that he should not see death; and was not found, because God had
      translated him: for before his translation he had this testimony, that he pleased God. But without faith it is

What completely escapes Hutchings, however, is the fact that Hebrews is not talking about how to receive a gift
but obtain a reward—in the very passage Hutchings quotes no less! This same biblical context immediately goes
on to say:
      8
        By faith Abraham, when he was called, obeyed by going out to a place which he was to receive for an

What the Egyptians were looking for is the same thing that Isaac, Jacob, and Abraham were looking for—the
Heavenly Pyramidal City, pictured by the Great Pyramid that Enoch built! This city is a misthological reward
to those who soteriologically are qualified to pursue it. A fundamental problem with the Egyptian approach is
that they, like so many today, confused soteriology and misthology. They could not distinguish the gift from
the reward. Hutchings does well to call attention to this deficiency in their understanding. What he fails to
realize, though, is that eternal life is often a reward in Scripture. What Enoch and Abraham, along with others
with a biblical perspective, work to achieve is not the free gift of eternal life (which is absolutely free), but the
reward of eternal life (which qualifies them for residency in the Heavenly City). As the writer of Hebrews
reiterates:
      24
         By faith Moses, when he had grown up, refused to be called the son of Pharaoh's daughter; 25 choosing
      rather to endure ill-treatment with the people of God, than to enjoy the passing pleasures of sin;
      26
         considering the reproach of Christ greater riches than the treasures of Egypt; for he was looking to the
      reward. (Heb 11:24-26)

What is being sought by these biblical personalities is a reward, not a gift. Specifically, “they desire a better country,
that is a heavenly one. Therefore God is not ashamed to be called their God; for He has prepared a city for them”
(Heb 11:16). This Reward City is pictured by the Great Pyramid. Enoch built the latter to picture and obtain the
former. He won the prize of the upward call by his works. Those believers who want to obtain the same thing as
Enoch are going to have to put forth similar effort. The pyramid does not picture a gift, but a reward. The Egyptians
failed to perceive the gift aspect. But those following the Enochian way, such as Abraham, had not lost the
distinction. They believed in the Promised Redeemer for the gift of eternal life and followed the Enochian way in
order to obtain the much fuller experience of eternal life as a reward in the form of the Reward City.
        Hutchings believes that the white casting stones adorning the outside of the Great Pyramid when it was first
built were 144,000 in number. He takes these as picturing the 144,000 overcomers of Revelation and concludes that
in them: “We see the blueprint for God’s eternal New Jerusalem, as revealed in His Word, and in type in the Great
Pyramid.”148 If so, then the city should be associated, from top to bottom, with the habitation of overcomers, not
mere believers! How Hutchings misses this is truly mystifying; especially since in the same chapter in which he
speculates that the antechamber of the Great Pyramid represents the Judgment Seat of Christ (p. 102), he concludes:
“What else besides the Great Pyramid could the prophet have referred to in Jeremiah 32:19-20? ‘Great in counsel,
and mighty in work: for thine eyes are open upon all the ways of the sons of men: to give every one according
to his ways, and according to the fruit of his doings: Which hast set signs and wonders in the land of Egypt, even
unto this day.’”149 Biblically, the pyramid is a sign dealing with being rewarded according to one’s works. My
conclusion is that the external pyramidal shape represents the Reward City. The external misthological significance
is rather easy to decipher given the biblical affirmations as to the misthological nature of the city and pyramid.

fell from the Emerald Garden to Earth. Hutchings is evidently thinking along similar lines when he says, “It is the
belief of some scientists that a great catastrophe rocked our solar system approximately ten thousand years ago. If
this be true, then we can attribute it to the war in Heaven when Lucifer, an archangel who had dominion of one-
third of the universe, decided to take over the entire Kingdom of God” (Pyramid, 180). He cites Is 14:12-13 and
Eze 28:17-18 as already having taken place then and looks to Rev 12:7-8 as the final Star Wars episode. I will refer
to this Young-Ruined-Earth model as YRE and offer it as one more theory as to why the pyramid has a supposed
10,500 B.C. alignment. Basically, this YRE proposal is a synthesis of the GT (gap theory), otherwise known as the
RET (Ruined Earth Theory) and the YEC (Young Earth Creationism). YEC + RET = YRE. Accordingly, YRE
poses a very short gap of only about 6500 years between the destruction of Planet Lucifer and the creation of Adam.
In both RET and YEC, the creation of Adam took place about 4004 B.C., Allowing the possibility of death for
plants and animals after the creation week but before the fall opens up the possibility for such a synthesis. If seeds
could die during that period, then at least that form of death occurred before the fall.
     Fossils require cataclysm. Therefore, the fossil remains we have now would be attributable to one or more
variety of cataclysms. The destruction of Planet Lucifer would be the first potential candidate; it occurred before
the creation of man. Several remaining cataclysms are referred to as suns (i.e., ages) in some cultures within Mexico
and Peru. The water-sun (aka the flood) would be first post-Adamic cataclysm (around 1656 YFC / 2348 B.C.). The
wind-sun would refer to judgment that occurred at the Tower of Babel (around 1762 YFC / 2242 B.C.). The fourth
sun would have been the Earthquake cataclysm when the Earth divided during the days of Peleg (1996 YFC / 2008
B.C.), possibly in a collision with an asteroid or in a conflict with Mercury, the latter involving an electrical
discharge. Other large-scale cataclysms might include Sodom and Gomorrah in a conflict with Jupiter (2047 YFC
/ 1957 B.C.), a conflict with Venus at the Exodus, etc. This reconstruction is indebted in part to a personal
correspondence with Becker (7-5-2014), who provided me with a Power Point summation of this model, which she
had shown many years previously. I added 10 years to her suggested time periods to match with my time periods
from other sources and one of her own citations on the web. Thus, I place the flood in 1656 YFC rather than 1646
YFC. J. E. Becker, “Section II: The Ages of God.” Available at http://www.jebecker.com/section2.html. Accessed
7-5-2014. The 10-year difference starts with the birth of Caiman, which she charts as 315 but Berner as 325 YFC
(Intervenes, 236). Her Power Point presentation is a pictorial summation of her Section II, which implements
Velikovsky’s’ notes (www.varchive.org) among other sources. As to fossils, Becker postulates that fossils “earliest
plant fossils found in the rocks did not propagate with true seeds, but from spores.” J. E. Becker, “Section 1: In the
Beginning—Theories of Creation Held by Christians.” Available at http://www.jebecker.com/glimse1.html.
Accessed 7-5-2014. See her Section II for more details. Becker would attribute the earliest fossils to the first
cataclysm, a pre-Adamic cataclysm brought on by Lucifer’s rebellion. Whereas she uses a binary-star model to
explain the re-creation of the ruined Earth in her Section II, Elwell uses a Planet-X model in that regard. Douglas
A. Elwell, Planet X: The Sign of the Son of Man (Crane, MO: Defender Publishing, 2010).

        Horn surveys the models presented by Hastings and Heron and is open to both hypothesis: Either the Great
Pyramid was built by Enoch (so Hastings) or by the Nephilim (so Heron).155 Likewise, I am open to either
possibility. However, Horn’s presumption that we must choose between the two positions may be a little
presumptuous. Could not Hastings and Heron both be correct? Could Enoch have provided the brains and the
Nephilim provided the brawn for the joint building project? Enoch certainly did not move those blocks by himself!
The book of Enoch itself presents Enoch as playing the middle man between the unfallen Watchers and the fallen
Watchers. For example, God gives the archangel Michael a message to convey to the fallen Watchers (1En 10:11).
During this time, “Enoch was hidden before all this came to pass. No one knew where he was or what he was doing.
At this time, his activities were with the [unfallen] Watchers and his days were spent with the holy ones [i.e., angels,
Dan 4:13-14]” (1En 12:1-2). These unfallen Watchers, which would include Michael, one of the seven holy
Watchers (1En 20:4), send Enoch to the fallen Watchers saying, among other things, that “they will witness the
slaughter of their beloved ones, and will weep over the destruction of their children” (1En 12:6). He went and
delivered his message to him. They, in turn, asked him “to petition the Lord for them for forgiveness because from
this point they could not speak with the Lord” (1En 13:4-5).156
        More such interchanges occur, with Enoch acting as the messenger for God and the holy Watchers, on the
one hand, and the fallen Watchers, on the other hand. Enoch seemed to have some type of working relationship
with the fallen Watchers. Perhaps, during his hidden time with the unfallen Watchers, he obtained the blue prints
for the Great Pyramid. In return for supplying the building schematics to the fallen Watchers, they provided the
building material and laborers via the Nephilim. One incentive would be to learn what Enoch had learned from God
about the future. The Great Pyramid has eschatological implications. By following his directions in building it, they

                                                          Ufology
        Heron resumes and expands his discussion of the pyramidal city in his second book, Return of the Antichrist
and the New World Order. He devotes ch. 19 to defending the position that Heavenly Jerusalem is a literal,
pyramidal city.159 A week or so before getting Heron’s book, I was discussing another book I was reading at the
time, Alien Agenda, with my friend Doug Berner. This book concludes that the antichrist “will make an unusual
appearance to the world (perhaps in a UFO) when he ascends out of the Bottomless Pit.”160
        A number of Christian Ufologists and popular Christian writers (e.g., Tom Horn, L.A. Marzulli, Chuck
Missler, Bill Salus) suggest that when Antichrist makes his appearance, he will do so via a spaceship: “‘The lying
wonders’ of 2Thessalonians are, according to one theory, end-time flying saucers that will be piloted by creatures
who appear to be advanced humanoids but who are in fact evil spirits.”161 “If suddenly hundreds of ‘space craft’
visibly arrived in our atmosphere, they say, piloted by beings who appeared to be advanced humanoids, these beings
could arguably claim to have removed ‘believers’ into a high-tech ‘rapture’ and/or simultaneously present their
leader (Antichrist?) as the awaited messiah (Quetzalcoatl? Dionysus?).”162 According to the Bible, the Antichrist is
to come “with all power and signs and false wonders, and with all the deception of wickedness for those who perish,
because they did not receive the love of the truth so as to be saved. And for this reason God will send upon them a
deluding influence so that they might believe what is false” (2Thess 2:9-11). If Satan is behind the UFO phenomena,
and there is every reason to believe that he is, at least in part, then it only stands to reason that he would not pass up
the opportunity to lead the masses astray by using UFOs, in conjuncture with an official disclosure, to claim that:
(1) life on this planet is due to some form of panspermia; (2) he is responsible for helping the human race evolve to
its present point; and (3) he is here to help mankind evolve to the next level, through various means, such as genetic
enhancement.163 The mark of the beast may very well be a chip that, among other things, imparts what they will
term: beneficial genetic mutations.164
        In any event, Berner posed that when the Antichrist appears he will do so with a pyramidal shaped spaceship.
My first impression was that such a proposal was too farfetched to be creditable. First impressions are not
necessarily correct, however. Berner’s logic was impeccable. If the Antichrist is to be revealed with UFOs, then
what better way to maximize his deception than with a mothership that is in the pyramidal shape, imitating New
Jerusalem? At the same time as Berner made this suggestion, I was reading Alien Agenda, in which it was pointed
out that the Lord has his city in Revelation (New Jerusalem) and Satan has his city (Babylon).165 Putting two and

             A few years ago, I received an email from a young New Zealand Christian man who had read my

This last sentence should be understood in terms of what Heron said earlier in his book: “When these evil fallen
angels are cast out of Heaven, which is a spiritual realm, they will, I believe, lose some of their inherent abilities
and become manifest in this realm of the material. It is my feeling that they will be invisible no more and will be in
plain sight for all to see.”167 Such a proposal seems credible. If the UFO phenomenon is satanic in origin and is
transdimensional in nature, both of which are highly probable propositions, then the futuristic casting of Satan and
his fallen angels out of the heavenly realm certainly would suggest the removal of their ability to enter
transdimensional space. As a result, they would be confined to our dimension. When seen in this light, the biblical
passage describing this war would seem to be describing something akin to Star Wars:

      And there was war in heaven, Michael and his angels waging war with the dragon. And the dragon and his
      angels waged war, and they were not strong enough, and there was no longer a place found for them in
      heaven. And the great dragon was thrown down, the serpent of old who is called the devil and Satan, who
      deceives the whole world; he was thrown down to the earth, and his angels were thrown down with him.
      (Rev 12:7-9)

Rather than describing an intergalactic battle, such as depicted in Star Wars, the Bible predicts an interdimensional
battle in which the loser, the dragon and his forces, become earthbound. Why not take the passage literally, as
various other writers do?168 As stated before, if the literal sense makes sense, seek no other sense, lest it result in

       Horn summarizes Heron’s position: “He believes New Jerusalem is pyramidal, as opposed to cubic in shape,
and that fallen Watchers conveyed this design to the Nephilim following what they had seen in heaven.”171 As to
the proposal that New Jerusalem is a mothership for angelic forces, this anticipation is simply an extrapolation of
the argument advanced by others. For example, Hoggard defends the thesis that the chariots of God refer to angels:
“The chariots of God are twenty thousand, even thousands of angels: the Lord is among them, as in Sinai, in the
holy place” (Ps 68:17; KJV).172 According to Hoggard, the chariots of God are...angels. Constable comments:

angels and fallen angels are described in such terms. The chariots of the fallen angels are very destructive:

These flame-emitting chariots leap from the top of one mountain to the next, wielding terrible destruction
(cp. Rev 9:7-9). Such chariots literally might be described as jumping chariots (Nahum 3:2; KJV). Scripture
frequently has a duality. Such chariots might picture both physical and celestial chariots, depending upon the time
period involved. Indeed, because of such duality—whether it be typological, telescopical, or simply
hermeneutical—one seriously can entertain Hoggard’s suggestion that those Jews living then may lament that their
land is taken over by space aliens (Lam 5:2). The Redeemer of the Jews may come in like manner: “For behold,
the Lord will come in fire and His chariots like the whirlwind, to render His anger with fury, and His rebuke with
flames of fire” (Is 66:15). The Lord also has His flame-emitting chariots. Satan’s forces are not the only ones with
such spaceships. Nevertheless, the forces of the Lord will prove victorious. “Behold, he goes up like clouds, and

       Appealing to the chariot of the cherubim in 1Chron 28:18, Klein proposes that angels fly in spaceships.174
Lange takes this construction appositionally, an understanding shared by various translations (e.g., NAS; NKJ) and
commentators (e.g., NAC; PC), so that the cherubim are the chariot: “The cherubim themselves...exhibit as it were
a chariot.” Thus Lange believes this verse is “a very important passage for the right understanding
of Ezek. 1:15 ff.”175 Indeed, in reading Eze 1:10ff, one can understand why many think that cherubim fly like UFOs.
       Horn provides an interesting email correspondence that he had with David Flynn in 2005 regarding a related
passage in Revelation: “I saw a star from heaven which had fallen to the earth; and the key of the bottomless pit
was given to him. And he opened the bottomless pit; and smoke went up out of the pit, like the smoke of a great
furnace; and the sun and the air were darkened by the smoke of the pit” (Rev 9:1-2). Flynn poses: “If the star itself
IS the bottomless pit, Revelation 9 could be describing an extraterrestrial object—a mother ship—moving into orbit
around earth with myriad beings....Revelation 12 explains the surety of Satan and his angels coming down from
heaven to the earth in the future. For now, they wait...somewhere out in space” (emphasis his).176 This suggestion
is plausible, but a more probable suggestion is that the mothership is transdimensional rather than extraterrestrial.
These beings use wormholes (i.e., stargates, to use Horn’s term) “to navigate dimensions or heaven.”177 “The theory
is that hyperspace tunnels may connect: (a) remote regions of the universes [observe the plural universes, thus
multiverse] and/or; (b) different dimensions and/or; (c) different moments in time.”178 Note, for theological reasons,
I reject the last option, as I do not believe that spatial time travel is possible (see TOD and RAE). But the first two
options are reasonable. Therefore, I am open to the possibility that an interdimensional stargate above Babylon will
open from which the pyramidal mothership of the Antichrist will descend.
       Horn calls attention to the possibility that, in his oracle of Babylon, Isaiah may be suggesting the return of
the Nephilim via such a portal. According to the LXE, the Lord says that He will bring giants (gigantes) to fulfill
His wrath (Is 13:3). This is the same word translated as Nephilim in Gen 6:4. “Isaiah may literally be telling us that
when Babylon is invaded and destroyed in the last days, God will command (unleash from their prisons? Command
the ‘ruler’ to open their gate?) giant Nephilim to come forth as agents of destruction.”179 If the so-called prophetic
dreams of pyramidal shaped spaceships have any validity, their association with the Antichrist’s pyramidal
mothership would be enough to explain the pyramidal shape of the UFOs within the dreams. One not need pose
that all the ships are pyramidal in shape. Rather, their shape in the dreams could be attributed to the association of
these flying saucers with the pyramidal mothership. This supposition is more reasonable than supposing all the
spaceships are pyramidal in shape.
       Additionally, a further conjecture makes association between the rapture and the spaceships very plausible.
If the Antichrist is to be revealed via spaceships, why not postulate that the Lord is to be revealed in the same
manner? After all, Satan imitates the Lord. If the Lord is to be revealed in this manner, the natural supposition is
that Antichrist will be revealed in this manner as well. In other words, the Lord may use His angels and their
spaceships to facilitate the rapture.

gather together His elect from the four winds, from one end of the sky to the other” (Mt 24:30-31). Church
comments are reasonable: “The ‘sign’ of the Son of Man, could be His actual appearance in ‘the clouds of heaven.’

would cause problems needed to be taken aboard, re-educated, and initiated “into higher levels of consciousness!”185

and, consequently, lends support to the anticipation that the genuine rapture will be carried out visibly via
spaceships. The event is not false, only the explanation.
       Regardless, of how the truth will be blended with falsehood, posing two very different groups of literal

the Great (Rev 18:2). The city of confusion in Is 24:10 (KJV), therefore, is most likely a cryptic reference to

(Confusion) because that is the city and tower where the Lord confused their language. This association between
Is 24:10 and Gen 11:4-9 is normally not noticed because Isaiah cryptically uses the Hebrew word tohu (formless,

play with Gen 1:2. According to my YRE (Young-Ruined-Earth) model, Planet Lucifer had been rendered formless

was rendered formless and void. In this reconstruction, Planet Lucifer-Earth was not formless (tohu) when it was
first created, as it was to be inhabited from day one (Is 45:18) by the angelic sons of God, who sang for joy when it
was created (Job 38:7). Earth-Lucifer became formless when it was struck by Planet X (Gen 1:2). The structures
built by the angels on Planet Lucifer-Earth and other planets, such as Mars, were destroyed by Planet X. Earth will
experience a similar tohu catastrophe during the tribulation (Jer 4:23).189

                                                       Conclusion
       Since interpreters should provide plausible scenarios for their models, the present chapter has endeavored to
explore the various hypotheses associated with the contending models. Some primary differences between the
models offered by Dillow and myself were first reviewed. Oddly, Dillow and those following his train of thought
think that the rulership exercised by mankind will expand throughout the universe, but the population will not
increase. Other writers, such as myself, deduce that the population must continue to increase through the procreation
of those believers who enter the eternal state in physical bodies following the millennial phase of the kingdom so
that children are born throughout the eternal state. The latter model takes a literal view of various passages, including
the tree of life producing literal leaves and fruit. The leaves used for the healing of those in physical bodies, while
the fruit serves as a reward for the faithful in co-glorified bodies. Dillow and I concur that rewards are eternal. This
acknowledgment was shown to require that one acknowledge that death continues in the eternal state. This
proposition allows the possibility that a limited food chain exists in the eternal state, which allows one to suppose
a limited food chain existed before the fall.
       New material was then examined regarding the multiverse. If the population is to expand eternally, then
eventually it will exceed our present finite universe. One may reasonably predict, in accord with the multiverse
model, that humanity will expand to other universes. Possible means by which this expanding population might be
moved to other universes were entertained. Implications that this multiverse model might have upon the doctrine of
the outer darkness were also discussed. The multiverse model was found reasonable and integrated with various
other passages of Scripture. Multiverse is a multiple dimensional physical-spiritual reality. The multiverse model
is biblically necessary to explain the physical-spiritual phenomena found in Scripture. This multidimensional
understanding of the Bible has spatial implications that necessarily have implications for Heaven, Hell, Hades, as
well as the outer darkness. Not surprisingly, this multidimensional integration suggests that passages dealing with
such realities might have multidimensional interpretations requiring that they be seen from various vantage points.
       Whereas Dillow poses a childless eternity, Crawford and I pose that children will be born in eternity. As to
the outer darkness, my classic model in TOD postulated that the outer darkness will initially be on this planet and
then migrate to other planets in the present universe. Hollandsworth likewise pictures the outer darkness as being
on Earth. The multiverse model, however, is not compelled to place the outer darkness in this universe, much less
on planet Earth. Other misthological models, taking advantage of the multiverse model, were considered. The
Crawfordian model was pictured as having an outer courtyard to the Heavenly City. In the MACC (Multiverse
Alcorn-Crawfordian-Caulian) model, those in the outer darkness watch from the heavenly perimeter, from which
they observe the multiverse populated with children. Unfaithful believers will miss out on interacting with
upcoming generations, forever isolated from a growing, thriving humanity. The competing MTET (Multiverse
terrestrial-extraterrestrial) model allows very limited interaction with children and expanding humanity. Any
model posed by Hollandsworth, Crawford, or myself supposes a literal Heavenly City, of course. Correlation
between this heavenly pyramidal city and earthly pyramids was found plausible. Since this Heavenly City will
migrate back to this planet like a giant spaceship, inaction with UFO lore was appropriate. Given the literal nature
of the Heavenly City, Satan may imitate this city with a smaller version of his own to facilitate the great deception.

                                Chapter 2. Eternal Rewards
                                                        Introduction
       Chitwood’s argument against eternal rewards was quoted earlier and then destroyed by simple observations
about rabbits and skinned knees.191 Because of the extremely serious nature of his transgression against kingdom
teaching, however, a more detailed interaction is in order.192 This extended quote from Chitwood was provided
earlier. The first part of this chapter will break that quote up into parts, highlight it, and retain his original emphases.
Weaknesses in his argument will be further explored. Modifications to my previous arguments in TOD will then be
entrained regarding the applicability of litotic promises as misthological threats in the eternal state.
                                 Everyone Does Not Rule During the Eternal State
        The first paragraph of that lengthy quote was:

             Christ and His co-heirs, beyond the Millennium, will no longer rule over the nations, as this rule is
      pictured in Rev. 2:26, 27. Rather, the Gentiles comprising the nations will be brought into positions of
      rulership themselves with Christ and His co-heirs, as this rule extends beyond the earth, out into the universe
      (Rev 22:2, 5). And the Son, beyond the Millennium, will no longer sit on His Own throne, as seen in
      Rev. 3:21. Rather, He will sit on “the throne of God and of the Lamb,” from whence universal rule will
      emanate (Rev. 22:1, 3, 5).

       Where is Chitwood’s proof that Gentile nations will be co-rulers with Christ and His overcomers at the end
of the millennial kingdom? Assertion does not demonstration make. The ludicrous nature of his assertion is obvious
enough from the context of Revelation itself. At the end of the millennial age, the Gentile nations will rise up against
God (Rev 20:8-9). In the battle of Gog and Magog, they rise up against the throne of God, not to the throne of God!
Even at the introduction of the eternal state, the condition necessary to be considered a co-heir still has some
applicability: “He who overcomes shall inherit these things” (Rev 21:7).
       Given Chitwood’s interest in typology, an answer along those lines is fitting. As he explains, the Father takes
the remnant among Israel as His wife, and the Son takes the faithful among the church as His wife:

             God took Israel as His wife in the Old Testament theocracy. God had to possess a wife in order to
      reign in the theocracy, for that is the manner in which He Himself established matters in the beginning. God
      later divorced Israel because of harlotry. But, in the future restored theocracy, God will have remarried
      Israel (cf. Isa. 1:21-2:5; Jer. 3:1ff; Hosea 2:2, 3; 14:1ff; Rev. 19:1-6).
             After the same manner, Christ will one day take a wife. The Spirit of God is in the world today
      searching for a bride for God’s Son, for the Son can’t reign apart from possessing a wife. Again, this is a
      matter established by God in the beginning, and it cannot change (Gen. 24:1ff; Eph. 5:21-33; Rev. 19:7-
      9).193

             The Book of Ruth presents one aspect of this overall matter, and the Book of Esther presents the
      other. The Book of Ruth has to do with a Gentile who marries a Jew, with a redeemed inheritance in view.
      Ruth, in her marriage to Boaz, sets forth truths surrounding Christ and His wife yet future. And the entire
      Book of Ruth sets forth the overall scope of the matter from beginning to end, with the husband-wife
      relationship being brought to the forefront in the end.
             The Book of Esther then presents the matter as it relates to God and Israel. Esther was a Jew whom
      King Ahasuerus (who was not a Jew [note that it is God’s Son Who is a Jew and will so remain throughout
      eternity, not the Father]) had taken as his wife following the former queen’s (Vashti’s) refusal to fulfill her
      role as the king’s wife (1:9ff). Then the remainder of the book revolves around Israel in the latter days

       (Haman typifying Antichrist), the end of Gentile world power, and Israel restored to the nation’s rightful

        Chitwood has a splendid discussion in this regard. Boaz typifies Christ, and Boaz’s marriage represents that
of Christ and the church. So where does one read that Boaz opens his marriage bed to other women after he marries
Ruth? Nowhere! Yet Chitwood’s argument would have Christ, the antitype, opening His marriage to other women,
that is, the Gentile nations, at the conclusion of the millennium. But God tells us, “Let marriage be held in honor
among all, and let the marriage bed be undefiled; for fornicators and adulterers God will judge” (Heb 13:4). Is
Chitwood saying that God does not practice what He preaches? Does Chitwood intend to lead us to believe that at
the end of the millennial kingdom the Father and Son and their respective wives share a single marriage bed and
that this mingled marriage bed is then opened to others? Does Christ turn into a polygamist at the end of millennial
kingdom who is not satisfied with polygamy but also sleeps with His Father’s wife? Chitwood’s argument seems
to lead to some very unsatisfactory typological results. A universal rule is one thing; a universal marriage bed is
another.
                                         The Lake of Fire is not Temporary
         His next paragraph was:

              It is the overcomer’s promise to the Church in Smyrna which has to do with the lake of fire,
       something which can only be millennial within its scope of fulfillment. That is, the conditions alluded to
       for the nonovercomer in this promise will exist for the duration of the Messianic Era, not throughout the
       eternal ages beyond.

       To the contrary, the Lake of Fire is not millennial in scope. The unregenerate are not even cast into it until
the beginning of the eternal state. Consequently, because the Lake of Fire is eternal, the promise that overcomers
will not be hurt by the Lake of Fire would most naturally be presumed to be eternal also.
                                            Misthological Death is Eternal
         Yet Chitwood continues:

             Scripture deals with millennial rewards and/or loss, never with eternal rewards and/or loss. This
       should be easy enough for anyone to understand, for if rewards are eternal, so is loss of rewards. And loss
       of rewards involves an association with death (Rom. 8:13), something which the Scripture clearly reveals
       will be done away with at the beginning of the eternal ages beyond the Millennium (1 Cor. 15:26;
       Rev. 21:4).

       Part of Chitwood’s argument is excellent. Truly, the thesis that rewards are eternal requires that the loss
of rewards be eternal also. Furthermore, the loss of rewards involves misthological death; therefore,
misthological death must be eternal if rewards are eternal. Chitwood’s deductions are correct in that regard.
Moreover, Chitwood believes that unfaithful believers can be hurt by the second death. Although I disagree
with Chitwood concerning the particulars as to how he derives his conclusion, he is nevertheless correct.
Whereas he would say that they are hurt metaphorically by the second death, I would say that they are hurt
correlatively by the second death. Nevertheless, we agree that subcomers will be hurt by the second death
throughout the millennial age. We also agree that the outer darkness is a metaphorical picture of this
misthological death. Therefore, we agree that the outer darkness lasts throughout the millennial age. In this
aspect of his deduction, Chitwood has been more consistent than Dillow. Regrettably, Dillow draws back from
even allowing the outer darkness to last throughout the millennial age, attributing only a few short years to the
outer darkness.
       How a misthologist such as Dillow, who acknowledges that rewards are eternal and that the loss of
rewards is eternal, can fail to see that the outer darkness and its corresponding misthological death must also be
eternal is truly a mystery. Chitwood sees the matter more accurately than Dillow in recognizing that the
eternality of the rewards stands or falls with acknowledging the eternality of misthological death. Yet Chitwood
shrinks back just as far, if not further than Dillow, in trying to tame this doctrine by limiting it to a thousand

194
      Ibid., 96.
Chapter 2. Eternal Rewards                                                                                        Page 87
years. The kingdom message is betrayed in the house of its friends when friends of the kingdom message deny
that the promise of misthological life and its attendant threat of misthological death are both eternal.
Misthological tares in the field of the gospel of the kingdom may take on surprising identities. I hope that the
degree of treachery administered to the gospel of the kingdom in this matter in the house of its friends will not
cause it to rise up in wrath against those friends who try to tame it. The gospel of the kingdom is not a tame
lion. Woe to those misthologists who try to tame it. This doctrine has teeth.
        Where Chitwood goes astray is to conclude that this promise of no more death rules out all forms of death.
Dillow makes the same mistake. We already have seen that this erroneous notion leads to the fallacious deduction
that there will be no procreation or skinned knees in the eternal state. Wheat, as we know it, would even cease to
grow. Even more specifically, as it pertains to Chitwood’s analysis, the text is actually saying the opposite of what
Chitwood imagines. The promise of no more death is a litotetic promise of no misthological death (Rev 21:4). This
promise is only applicable to those who overcome (Rev 21:7). Using Chitwoodian logic, this means that those who
do not overcome will experience misthological death, specifically in the eternal state no less!
        Chitwood’s deduction is self-defeating: “There can be no such thing as Christians being hurt of the second
death beyond the Millennium, for Rev. 21:4 plainly declares that there will be no more death during this time.” Yet,
on the next page, he remarks about Rev 21:8, which follows shortly thereafter: “The second death for the
nonovercomer is having a ‘part in the lake which burneth with fire and brimstone’” (emphasis his).195 His
perspective is that these two verses, taken together, indicate that subcomers can be hurt only metaphorically by the
second death during the millennium. A more intuitive approach would acknowledge that if Rev 21:4 is talking about
the eternal state, then so is Rev 21:8. Verse 4 is litotetically promising that no death (of any form) will exist for the
overcomer during the eternal state, while verse 8 is misthologically warning that misthological death will continue
to exist for the subcomer. Chitwood’s assertion that subcomers can be hurt misthologically (in a metaphorically
sense) by the second death during the millennium is permissible (Rev 2:11; 20:6), though I would derive this
conclusion from a correlative vantage point. However, given his misthological interpretation of Rev 21:8 as dealing
with subcomers, the proper conclusion, in that case, would be that they will be hurt misthologically by the second
death throughout the eternal state as well. The outer darkness is eternal. Therefore, the misthological death pictured
metaphorically by that darkness is eternal. Correlatively, I reach that conclusion even regarding Rev 21:8.
        My major disappointment with Chitwood’s position is not that he applies Rev 21:8 to believers but that he
limits the application to the millennium. My minor point of disagreement is that he applies the verse metaphorically,
whereas I would apply it correlatively. We both apply it misthologically. In my assessment, the second death is
experienced literally by unbelievers for all eternity and will be experienced correlatively by subcomers for all
eternity. Rewards are eternal. That some Gehenna misthologists attempt to circumvent the eternal nature of rewards
is perhaps understandable. For they cannot have subcomers being submitted to a literal Gehenna or literally to the
Lake of Fire for eternity. Unconditional security forbids such a possibility. However, neither Chitwood nor I are
Gehenna misthologists in that both of us only allow metaphorical applicability (in Chitwood’s case) or correlative
applicability (in my case) of the Lake of Fire to subcomers. Therefore, Chitwood is not laboring under any such
literal Gehenna compulsion to limit his misthological application to the millennium. Regardless as to whether our
fellow moderate misthologists may prefer Chitwood’s metaphorical approach or my correlative approach, the
eternality of the misthological applicability of Rev 21:8 should be accepted. That this verse has eternal metaphorical
applicability is the most natural interpretation of Rev 21:8 given Chitwood’s approach or the most natural
correlation given mine. Regardless, the metaphorical application of the principle of correlativity to unfaithful
believers is eternal.
                                   Being Hurt by the Second Death is Eternal
         Chitwood continues with his forceful assertions:

            The overcomer’s promise to those Christians comprising the Church in Smyrna reads,

                 “He that hath an ear, let him hear what the Spirit saith unto the churches; He that overcometh
          shall not be hurt of the second death” (2:11; cf. Rev. 20:6).



195
      Chitwood, End, 484-485.
Page 88                                                                                    Misthological Models: Part 2
             There is a clear implication in this promise that those who do not overcome will be hurt of the second
      death. And any attempt to take this promise and make it mean something other than what it clearly states
      serves only to destroy the promise, something which the Lord sounded a solemn warning against (Rev.
      22:18, 19). The promise that those who do overcome will not “be hurt of the second death” would be
      meaningless unless this promise is taken at face value and allowed to mean exactly what it says, clearly
      implying that those who do not overcome will “be hurt of the second death.”
             “The second death” in the Book of Revelation is associated with the lake of fire (Rev. 21:8). And
      those who do not overcome (v. 7) are going to have their part in this lake of fire (v. 8). That is, they will be
      hurt of the second death by having a part in the lake of fire....

       Several problems are apparent within this argument. He insists that it is clear that he can throw this verse
into reverse and make it say the opposite of what it says. He has failed to own up to the litotetical nature of the
promise. He has overstated his case. He is the one making it “mean something other than what it clearly states.” To
prove this criticism, I will briefly unpack these allegations.
       For starters, the promise of the verse is that if (A) you overcome, then (B) you will not be hurt by the second
death. Neither in logic nor in Scripture can you automatically assume that you can take an if-then statement and
negate the protasis (the first part, the A part) and thereby safely negate the apodosis (the second part, the B part).
Darkness devotes chapter 21 in its entirety to a discussion of such litotes. One such example pointed out therein is
that Jn 6:37 cannot be thrown into reverse and made to say that the one who does not come to Jesus is cast out
(ekballo).196 One would have to come to Jesus first before being cast out is even a possibility. An object has to be
in a container before it can be cast out of the container. Unbelievers were never in Christ to be cast out of Christ.
Or as Barker exclaims: “Are we to understand by this statement that Jesus is implying that He will cast out some
who come to Him? Certainly not!”197 When Jesus says that He does not lose (apollumi) comers (Jn 6:39), this does
not mean that He loses noncomers. Unbelievers were not given to Him by the Father. The litotes are not dealing
with unbelievers. The affirmation is the point, not its opposite.198 Thus, my argument in TOD was just as the
soteriological litotes in Jn 6:39 is not intended for unbelievers, the misthological litotes of Rev 2:11 is not intended
as a threat for unfaithful believers. Litotes should not be thrown into reverse or made to stand on their head.
Logically, inversion is a fallacy.
       In that chapter, I used the example of Michael Jordan. Dillow picked up on that example and appropriately
commented: “If we say, ‘Michael Jordan is not a bad basketball player,’ we mean he is a very good basketball
player. However, the reverse does not follow, ‘If you are not Michael Jordan, you are definitely not a good
basketball player.’”199 Dillow likewise uses this and related illustrations to warn against assuming one can throw
Rev 2:11 into reverse. It is not clear that this verse can be thrown into reverse and applied to nonovercomers. Dillow
does not see any such inverse applicability, and it took me many years before I could see it. And the only way I
could do so was with the principle of correlativity, as explained in Darkness. Nevertheless, now that I do make that
correlation, I can engage Chitwood on his own turf. While he and I reach our conclusions differently, we both agree
that subcomers will be hurt by the second death. With that said, let us now get to the heart of the matter.
       Chitwood’s own argument should have forced him to conclude that subcomers will be hurt by the second
death eternally. With his earlier statement, “loss of rewards involves an association with death (Rom. 8:13)”
(emphasis his), Chitwood correctly acknowledges the misthological nature of this death—a death associated with
the loss of rewards. With his second part of that statement, “something which the Scripture clearly reveals will be
done away with at the beginning of the eternal ages beyond the Millennium (1 Cor. 15:26; Rev. 21:4),” he locates
196
    Nevertheless, believers can be cast out (ballo) misthologically even in this gospel (Jn 15:6). Therefore, a
correlation exists between being cast out soterically and misthologically. As always though, correlativity ≠ equality.

    Harold Barker, Secure Forever (Neptune: Loizeaux Brothers, 1974), 134.

the items in question, the Lord Himself never would. Although I am inclined to agree with this assessment, even

Chapter 2. Eternal Rewards                                                                                   Page 89
the removal of misthological death at the “beginning of the eternal ages” and extends the period being covered by
this promise into eternity future. His next statement, “those who do not overcome will be hurt of the second death”
(emphasis his), should lead one to conclude that subcomers will be hurt by the second death during the period of
time covered by the promise that overcomers will not be hurt by the second death which, according to his analysis
in Rev 21:4, is during the eternal state. His own argument, therefore, leads to the conclusion that subcomers will be
hurt by the second death during the eternal state. Yet he strangely thinks that his argument leads to the conclusion
that subcomers will be hurt by the second death only during the millennial kingdom. Since he misses the point of
his own argument, my restating it is certainly in order. The no-death-of-any-form promise in Rev 21:4 is eternal in
scope and limited to those who overcome. Therefore, given Chitwood’s line of reasoning, the conclusion he should
have reached is that those who do not overcome will experience some form of death associated with the loss of
rewards during the eternal state. Misthological death for subcomers continues throughout the eternal state because,
otherwise, per his own argument, the promise that overcomers would not be hurt by misthological death during the
eternal state is meaningless. Amazingly and inconsistently, though, Chitwood concludes the exact opposite!
       Let us take another look at his statements by highlighting his acknowledgement that the time period in view
in Rev 21:4 is “at the beginning of the eternal ages beyond the Millennium” in a different color. Then I will insert
that clarification, which he himself supplies, into his own statements:

           And loss of rewards involves an association with death (Rom. 8:13), something which the Scripture
    clearly reveals will be done away with at the beginning of the eternal ages beyond the Millennium
    (1 Cor. 15:26; Rev. 21:4)....
           There is a clear implication in this promise that those who do not overcome will be hurt of the second
    death [at the beginning of the eternal ages beyond the Millennium]. And any attempt to take this promise
    and make it mean something other than what it clearly states serves only to destroy the promise, something
    which the Lord sounded a solemn warning against (Rev. 22:18, 19). The promise that those who do
    overcome will not “be hurt of the second death” [at the beginning of the eternal ages beyond the
    Millennium] would be meaningless unless this promise is taken at face value and allowed to mean exactly
    what it says, clearly implying that those who do not overcome will “be hurt of the second death [at the
    beginning of the eternal ages beyond the Millennium].”
           “The second death” in the Book of Revelation is associated with the lake of fire (Rev. 21:8). And
    those who do not overcome (v. 7) are going to have their part in this lake of fire (v. 8) [at the beginning of
    the eternal ages beyond the Millennium]. That is, they will be hurt of the second death by having a part
    in the lake of fire [at the beginning of the eternal ages beyond the Millennium]. (Bold added)

        “Those who do not overcome (v. 7)” are not going to inherit the promise of not having any death (v. 4). For
verse 7 clearly states: “He who overcomes shall inherit these things,” that is, the things just mentioned in verse 4,
which promises no death of any kind. The no-death-of-any-type promise is limited to those who overcome. In this
context, this promise is, as Chitwood acknowledges, in relation to “the beginning of the eternal ages beyond the
Millennium.” Per Chitwood’s own words, then, “There is a clear implication in this promise that those who do not
overcome will be hurt of the second death [at the beginning of the eternal ages beyond the Millennium].”
Consequently, since he is denying the clear implication of the text to which his own argument leads, his solemn
warning applies to himself: “Any attempt to take this promise and make it mean something other than what it clearly
states serves only to destroy the promise, something which the Lord sounded a solemn warning against (Rev. 22:18,
19).” Chitwood unwittingly has condemned himself with his own words. Verse 7 clearly, per Chitwood, limits the
litotetic promise of verse 4 to those who overcome. The logical implication of Chitwood’s own analysis,
accordingly, should be that this litotetic outcome of no death of any type applies only to those who overcome;
therefore, subcomers must be subject to mistholic death during the eternal state, for that is the clear
implication of the limitation and the time frame under consideration.
        In his attempt to kill the doctrine of eternal rewards, Chitwood has killed his own argument against eternal
rewards and perhaps invoked a misthological curse upon himself in doing so. Let us hope that in his attempt to
mollify the doctrine of the kingdom, he has not brought down the wrath of the kingdom upon himself—for eternity.
His own warning gives pause to wander if he just might have proven himself to be a misthological tare. It would be
Page 90                                                                                   Misthological Models: Part 2
terrible if such a gifted expositor of the kingdom message were found at the Bema to have disqualified himself from
having the eternal rewards he otherwise could have had just because he denies that they exist.200
       The most surprising thing about his denial of eternal rewards is that denying eternal rewards is completely
unnecessary within his misthological model. Denying eternal rewards because of the no-death promise in Rev 21:4
is completely inexcusable. For the verse simply is teaching that no death of any type will exist in the eternal state
for overcomers.201
       Admittedly, some of our friends in the kingdom movement would not find a transition to an eternal
perspective so easy because they believe that subcomers literally will burn in Gehenna or the Lake of Fire for a
thousand years. Therefore, some misthologists holding this view of Gehenna tend to limit rewards to a 1000 years
because, otherwise, their model would tend to posit that such believers would burn eternally, which is the
perspective of Arminian theology, not kingdom theology. Whipple tried to have his cake and eat it too, that is, to
affirm eternal rewards but only temporary burning. But his approach is just as problematic as Dillow’s attempt to
affirm eternal rewards while only allowing a temporary outer darkness. Making certain negative rewards temporary
while affirming that rewards are eternal leads, not surprisingly, to various inconsistencies.202 For a quick solution
that would at least allow us to agree that all rewards are eternal, I will end this discussion with a consideration of
Chitwood’s bottom line. Although he and I take different routes to derive our conclusions and differ in technical
details, we nevertheless come out substantially at the same place.
                                       Agreement Regarding the Bottom Line
        Chitwood’s bottom line is:

             To distinguish between millennial and eternal conditions in this respect, note that those outside the
      gates during the eternal ages will be the Gentile nations, as the New Jerusalem rests on the new earth (21:24-
      27); but those outside the gates during the preceding Messianic Era, with the New Jerusalem in the heavens
      above the earth, will be the nonovercomers (22:14, 15). And the place which they will occupy is described
      at least four other ways in Scripture — through the use of Gehenna, the outer darkness, the furnace of fire,
      and the lake of fire....
             Through comparing Scripture with Scripture, it is plain that these are simply different ways of
      expressing the same thing. And since a literal casting into outer darkness, Gehenna, or a furnace or lake of
      fire could not possibly be in view (for these different places could not possibly be looked upon as referring
      to the same place in a literal sense), it is evident that metaphors are being used throughout. But relative to
      the unsaved and the lake of fire, this is simply not expressed other ways in Scripture as it is with the saved,

201
    Paul envisions a similar distinction. In Rom 2:7, God will reward eternal life to those who seek for three things:
glory, honor, and immortality (aphtharsia). Eternal life consists of these three components at the misthological
level. To be sure, all believers will have these three items at the soteriological level. For example, all believers will
have immortality (aphtharsia) in the form of an immoral body (1Cor 15:22). But overcomers will have these benefits
at a superlative level. Their bodies will not only be free from physical death but also from misthological death. In
Rev 21:4, all believers likewise will never die physically during the eternal state, but only faithful believers will
never die misthologically. Only for victorious believers is misthological death “swallowed up in victory” when
believers take on immortal bodies (1Cor 15:54). “Therefore, my beloved brethren, be steadfast, immovable, always
abounding in the work of the Lord, knowing that your toil is not in vain in the Lord” (1Cor 15:58). Why work?
Because your misthological immortality is at stake. Those believers who do achieve misthological immortality will
be subject to misthological mortality for eternity. Misthological death is eternal.

      Chitwood does not tell us what being cast metaphorically into the Lake of Fire would look like within his
model. What will it look like in the real world? In piecing together the various clues Chitwood has given us, an
event very much like Crawford’s model is suggested. If this perception of Chitwood’s model is accurate, then a
plausible misthopological understanding of the big picture emerges.
                                   Illustration 51. Chitwoodian-Crawfordian Model




During the time frame in which Chitwood acknowledges that the Lake of Fire (a.k.a., Gehenna/the outer darkness)
is applicable to nonovercomers, New Jerusalem is in the heavens above the Earth. So where is the Lake of Fire
metaphorically located in relation to New Jerusalem within Chitwood’s metaphorical model. Chitwood apparently
provides enough information to propose an answer:

             The picture surrounding an association between Gehenna and the lake of fire appears unmistakable.
      As Gehenna was the place of refuse for the earthly city of Jerusalem, the lake of fire is seen as the place of
      refuse for the heavenly city of Jerusalem. And as Gehenna was on the opposite side of the city from that
      side where God dwelled (south, as opposed to north [cf. Lev. 1:11; Isa. 14:13]), thus will it be with the
      counterpart to Gehenna in the heavenly Jerusalem. The lake of fire is used with respect to a place completely
      apart from Christ and His rule. And those “hurt by the second death” are seen occupying this place during
      the 1,000-year Messianic Era.203 (Emphasis his).

Chitwood’s metaphorical Lake of Fire would be outside of the city, specifically to its south, at the opposite end of
the city from where God dwells at the top of the city, thus below the city, and be a place where those rejected from
the city are cast. So Chitwood’s metaphorical model leads one to picture the Lake of Fire’s misthological counterpart
as a metaphorical area in outer space to the south of the city where those cast out of the city are confined. I appreciate
Chitwood’s misthopology.204 Drawing upon Crawford’s model to assist in completing the picture, the composite
result offers a feasible model. Insofar as Rev 21:8 applies metaphorically to unfaithful believers, this
Chitwoodian-Crawfordian model is very useful. With that qualification in place, this would make a good picture
