                                                     Technical Aside
    Before proceeding to the composite, however, a technical clarification (explained more fully in TOD) is in order.
In TOD, in technical contradistinction to Chitwood, I perceived being cast into the Lake of Fire or having a portion
in the Lake of Fire to be a strictly correlative rather than a metaphorical event for the nonovercomer. The unfaithful
believer is not pictured as being cast metaphorically into the Lake of Fire, but being hurt by the second death might
be said to be a metaphorical recasting of the correlative relationship as it applies to the unfaithful believer.
        These litotes do not have to be thrown into reverse to derive this principle of correspondence. Some litotes
can be thrown into reverse; some cannot be. Nevertheless, the correlation being made within TOD is not derived
by reversing the litotes regarding the second death. John stops far too short of explicitly asserting that believers will
be hurt by the second death (or be under its power) for one to conclude that he would expect such a crass treatment
of these litotes. His correlation is much more subtle and refined, so much in fact that he probably would not expect
us to see the correlation when thinking exclusively in terms of litotes. And he certainly intends for us to see Rev 2:11
and 20:6 as litotes. To see the correlation, at least as perceived herein, these passages must be seen dualistically as
litotetic-correlative, not simply as a litotes read in reverse.205
        Seen from this perspective, no believer will be hurt by the second death in terms of being cast into the Lake
of Fire. However, a correlation between being hurt by the second death and being hurt by misthological death can
be said to exist. The denial of any such correlation for the overcomer strongly hints at the affirmation of such a
correlation for the subcomer. That such a correlation is intended is suggested by other such similar indications.
                                         Illustration 52. Traditional Litotes




         The traditional litotetic approach to Rev 2:11 and 20:6 taken in TOD affirms that believers who overcome
will obtain the crown of life as opposed to the second death. Not all believers will overcome, but the litotes should
not be read in reverse so as to suggest that those believers who fail to overcome will be hurt by the second death.
The litotes are unidirectional. Some traditionalists might allow that believers can be hurt by the second death in
some undefined or indirect manner. Still, even in that case, whether that point is intended by the passage is open to
debate. In any event, whatever being hurt by the second death means for the unfaithful believer, it does not entail a
believer being cast into the Lake of Fire. This model presumes a literal casting into the Lake of Fire to be a
soteriological punishment limited to the lost. An unidirectional promise is intended.
                                          Illustration 53. Ultraistic Litotes




205
   In contrast to TOD, the present study will suggest that John does intend for a limited misthological inversion of
the soteric-mistholic litotes to be sensed by his readers. Limited inversions based on correlative antithetical
polarizations were not considered in TOD. Therefore, the present study is building upon that earlier work in TOD
and advancing beyond TOD to hypothesize that John may have intended, at least on occasion, an inversion that
factors in such considerations within the parameters of such a limitation.
Chapter 2. Eternal Rewards                                                                                        Page 93
       The ultraistic approach affirms that believers who overcome will obtain the crown of life as opposed to the
second death. However, since not all believers will overcome, the litotes within the ultraistic model are bidirectional
and fully readable in reverse so as to indicate that those believers who fail to overcome will be hurt literally by the
second death, even to the point of being cast into the Lake of Fire for one thousand years.206 In the ultraistic camp,
a bidirectional threat-promise is perceived. Chitwood is adopting this bidirectional perspective but then arguing that
the Lake of Fire only applies metaphorically to subcomers.207 Although he and I attain similar results, we do so
through differing hermeneutical mechanics.
                                          Illustration 54. Correlative Litotes




       The correlative approach to Rev 2:11 and 20:6 affirms that believers who overcome will obtain the crown of
life as opposed to the second death. Not all believers will overcome, but the litotes should not be simply read in
full reverse so as to suggest that those believers who fail to overcome will be hurt either literally or directly by the
second death. The second death, which exists at the opposite end of the litotetic spectrum, is but a shadowy pointer
to the misthological death that awaits believers who fail to obtain the crown of life. Broadly speaking, the Scripture
allows a typological parallelism in this sense between the second death and misthological death. Those believers
who fail to overcome will be hurt by the second death typologically, not literally or directly. Correlatively, to be
hurt by the second death would correspond to the Bema (Rev 2:11); whereas, to be under the power of the second
death would correspond to the outer darkness (Rev 20:6). Jointly, in terms of correlativity, the Lake of Fire is to the
unbeliever what the Bema and outer darkness are to the unfaithful believer. Believers can be hurt correlatively and
indirectly by the second death, but not directly.
                                        Limited Litotetic Reversal (LLR)
      In this discussion, I have used Chitwood’s own rationale to nullify his own objection against eternal rewards.
The question may be raised as to the legitimacy of his rationale and therefore the legitimacy of my rebuttal to those

206
    Like TOD, the present study still rejects the ultraistic notion that such litotes are fully reversible. Rather, the new
perspective being entertained is that soteric-mistholic litotes are only partially reversible. They can only be reversed
to the left end of the misthological spectrum, not into the soteriological spectrum so as to suggest that subcomers
are literally cast into the Lake of Fire, which is a fate reserved for noncomers. Hollandsworth’s model would be an
attractive mediation between the model I am posing and the ultraistic model. In his proposed model, the Lake of
Fire has limited applicability in that the burning is only experienced at the Bema. The Bema burning is punctiliar;
the results of the burning are linear. The Bema burn is but for a moment, but the hurt is eternal. Comparatively, in
his model such misthological litotes are fully reversible in terms of literalness but only partially reversible in terms
of duration. Thus, they are fully-partially reversible. For sake of simplicity, I will not be engaging Hollandsworth
model but proceeding under the presumption that the regenerate cannot not suffer the same fate as the unregenerate
even for a moment, thereby ruling out a literal dip in the Lake of Fire from discussion.
207
    In effect, Chitwood unknowingly has argued for a limited litotetic reversal in which the reversal of the soteric-
mistholic litotes is limited to the mistholic range of metaphorical applicability without transgressing into
soteriological spectrum. What he has done intuitively, I am now doing scientifically in the present work, providing
a testable hypothesis that, to some extent, does support his intuitive conclusion.
Page 94                                                                                  Misthological Models: Part 2
who do not share his precept, however. Basically, the question is whether the not-be-hurt-by-the-second-death
promise of Rev 2:11 and the no-death promise of Rev 21:4, which are litotetically limited to overcomers, can be
used safely to prove that misthological death is necessarily in store for subcomers. In Chitwood’s assessment such
no-death promises for overcomers necessarily imply the inverse for subcomers. I concur within limits specified
herein and with a different rationale as to how this conclusion is derived. Where Chitwood and I totally disagree is
regarding the duration of the application. He limits the applicability of misthological death to the millennial
kingdom; whereas, I extend it to the eternal state. But how can I agree with him as to the basic necessity of the
applicability without invalidating my own observation that litotes cannot necessarily be thrown into reverse?
Eternal Misthological Death
        One way of doing so is to validate the conclusion independently of the inversion. Various avenues exist for
doing so, such as proving that the loss of one’s soul (i.e., life) is a misthological issue or that the crown of life is
limited to rewards. Such elementary issues would be suitable for Misthology 101, which is hardly worth our time
in an advanced misthological discussion such as this one. Through such elementary analyses one may prove that
subcomers are hurt misthologically by death. This independent verification is readily available in Darkness and
need not consume our time here. More advanced misthological considerations would consider whether it is proper
to consider limited litotetic reversals as legitimate, at least on occasion and within certain parameters. My
hypothesis is that soteric-mistholic litotes are capable of limited litotetic reversals. This hypothesis is worthy of
scientific investigation since it is capable of falsification, being testable against the data.
        A full reversal of soteric-mistholic litotes demonstrability produces faulty results. Chitwood and I agree that
subcomers will not be literally hurt by the second death; that is, they will not be cast literally into the Lake of Fire
(neither for a 1000 years or eternity). Therefore, in our mutual analysis, one cannot take the promise that overcomers
will not be hurt misthologically by the second death and reverse it to pose that subcomers will be hurt
soteriologically or literally by the second death. We jointly reject such Arminian and Ultraistic notions respectively.
In denying the soteriological possibility of faithful believers being hurt literally by the second death, which for
believers is impossible anyway, Christ is promising faithful believers that they will have a misthological experience
that is at the opposite end of the spectrum.
                                    Illustration 55. Litotetic Spectrum of Death




       Our principle test subject for testing the limited litotetic reversal (LLR) of a soteric-mistholic litotes for the
present study will be the mistholic promise of no death of any type for overcomers as juxtaposed with the soteric
fate of unbelievers in Rev 21:4-8:

      And He shall wipe away every tear from their eyes; and there shall no longer be any death; there
      shall no longer be any mourning, or crying, or pain; the first things have passed away....He who
      overcomes shall inherit these things...But for the cowardly and unbelieving and abominable and
      murderers and immoral persons and sorcerers and idolaters and all liars, their part will be in
      the lake that burns with fire and brimstone, which is the second death.208

       John is using an antithetical juxtaposition between unbelievers being hurt by the Lake of Fire and
overcomers not being hurt by any form of death to create a litotetic contrast that is limited in its positive
misthological thrust to overcomers. The sheer logic of the litotetic limitation of the promise of no death for
overcomers requires that misthological death be necessarily inferred for subcomers. In making this inference, I am
not assuming the point to be proven, namely LLR; rather, I am deductively reasoning as follows: John’s limiting

208
   The NAS insertion of the word any is justified as we have seen that forms of death must necessarily exist in the
eternal state. Such forms of death are not applicable to the overcomer.
Chapter 2. Eternal Rewards                                                                                     Page 95
the promise of no death of any type to overcomers means that they will have a superlative experience of life, such
as wearing the crown of life and eating the tree of life. Not having this superlative misthological experience of life
would be a form of misthological death for believers who do not overcome. This deductive conclusion is absolutely
certain. Logically, therefore, it must be deduced that subcomers will be hurt by mistholic death. One does not
have to reach this conclusion by throwing the litotes in reverse, only by noting that the positive result of
misthological life is limited exclusively to overcomers. The very nature of this particular litotes demands that the
limiting of such life to overcomers implies misthological death for nonovercomers. This deduction proves that this
litotes must have inverse misthological applicability, within the misthological spectrum, to those believers who do
not overcome.
        Reaching the end of John’s epistle, then, and thinking through the logical implications of what he has written
leads one to think back to his promise in Rev 2:11 that “he who overcomes shall not be hurt by the second death”
so as to induce that John anticipates that his readers will be able to infer that subcomers will be hurt misthologically
by the second death. In short, he intends for this soteric-mistholic litotes to have an inverse applicability to
subcomers within the limitations of the misthological end of the spectrum. Either John intends it, or he is blind to
the deductive and inductive logical implications of his own writing! Rather than attribute misthological blindness
to John, I would rather credit Chitwood with astute misthological intuition when he correctly deduces that
misthological death must be in store for those who do not overcome and that John intends to be understood as
teaching such.
        Unfortunately, though, Chitwood fails to follow through on the logical implications of his own writings to
conclude, as he logically must, that this misthological death is not only millennial in scope but applies equally
forcibly to eternity. But that point aside, one reasonably may conclude that John intends that this soteric-mistholic
litotes (SML) be understood as having limited-litotetic-reversal (LLR) applicability to subcomers. The applicability
is limited to the misthological spectrum, of course. Restated in color-coded terms, John expects his readers to be
able to read Rev 21:4-8 and see the blue-yellow (i.e., soteric-mistholic) juxtaposition and perceive the green
(mistholic) overlap that applies secondarily.
                                 Illustration 56. Limited Litotetic Reversion (LLR)




       In Darkness, I was opposed to litotetic reversion because such reversions can result in logical and biblical
perversions. However, I did not consider the possibility of the biblical writer intending a limited reversion. Yet such
phenomenon certainly seems to be the case regarding soteric-mistholic life-versus-death litotes. The proposition
that (A) overcomers will not be hurt soterically by the second death litotetically signifies that (B) they will have
mistholic life. If (A) there is no death for overcomers, then (B) there must be no mistholic death for overcomers.
Litotetically, we jump from blue to yellow.
       Conversely, (B) mistholic death must necessarily be inferred as occurring (C) in the case of those believers
who subcome to defeat.209 If (B) mistholic death occurs, then (C) those believers who die misthologically
must not have overcome. We must not reverse the litotes to jump back from yellow to blue. We can only jump
backwards to green. When doing a standing broad jump, one can jump forward much further than one can jump
backward. The same rule applies to soteric-mistholic litotes. What applies physically to the broad jump applies
logically and biblically to the soteric-mistholic litotes. When moving in reverse, we can only move to the
intermediate range.210



209
   The B signifies not B. The negation of no mistholic would be the affirmation of mistholic death.
210
   Because Hollandsworth affirms the eternal nature of rewards and misthological death but limits the burning to
the Bema, I would regard his Bema-burn proposal as a healthy competing LLR model. My LLR model does not
require literal burning at the Bema. His LLR model entertains that punctiliar possibility.
Page 96                                                                                  Misthological Models: Part 2
                                Illustration 57. Limited Litotetic Reversion Imagery




        Even Ultraists would acknowledge that the litotes must not be thrown into full reverse to suppose that if (not
B) mistholic death occurs, then (A) soteric death is necessarily inferred. Do not jump back from the mistholic
crown to the soteric flames. Do not jump backward from the yellow to the blue, or, thinking in board-jump terms,
you will land on your back and hit your head. A children’s object lesson would make that point clear. Let the
children try it—if you have a padded, color-coded floor. Have them jump forward from blue, over green, to yellow,
with yellow being as far as they can jump forward. Then have them try to jump backward from yellow, over green,
back to the original blue area. You better have some padding for that backward jump.
        By avoiding a full reversion that transgresses from the mistholic realm into the soteric realm, a perversion of
the gospel is avoided, FG theology is maintained, and logical and scientific integrity is maintained. The theory of
litotetic reversion is subjugated to the data, not the data to the theory, with a limited litotetic reversion (LLR) for
soteric-mistholic litotes (SML).
                  Illustration 58. Limited Litotetic Reversion (LLR) Results in Mistholic Death




       What is at stake for believers is the crown of life versus misthological death in the outer darkness.
Misthologically, we know with certainty that misthological death is a possibility. A conditional promise
implementing a SML (soteric-mistholic-litotetic) structure must, by logical and biblical necessity, be invertible
within the misthological region of that promise.211 Promising no death to faithful believers because of their
faithfulness necessarily implies, given the fuller misthological discussion, that unfaithful believers will experience
death within the misthological spectrum. But the rationale for this conclusion is not that of assuming that a direct
inversion is necessarily envisioned.
Eternal Misthological Tears
        For example, Rev 21:4 also promises that faithful believers will no longer have any tears during the eternal
state. I cannot invert that promise to assume, at least not with deductive certainty, that unfaithful believers will have
tears in the eternal state. Rather, the inversion regarding tears is limited to induction and, therefore, to probability.
The conclusion regarding misthological death, though, can be made with deductive certainty because, within the
misthological realm, the absence of life inevitably indicates the presence of death. However, we cannot assume,
with the same degree of certainty, that the absence of joy necessarily indicates the presence of tears. Whereas the
absence of misthological life unquestionably indicates the presence of misthological death, the absence of
misthological joy does not automatically prove the existence of misthological tears. One can be sorrowful and yet
not be tearful.

                                              Basketball Analogy
      Let us revisit the basketball analogy and refine it in a manner that might better approximate the current
discussion. Consider someone who asserts that anyone who plays pro basketball is not bad as a basketball player
but means that such a player would be among the greatest basketball players. Further suppose that the person
making this statement only considers pro basketball players to be among the greatest in the sport. Such a person

211
   The reason a soteric-mistholic litotes (SML) must be reversible in the misthological spectrum is because the
misthological result is a reward contingent upon performance and limited to those believers who achieve that level
of performance. The misthological outcome must be invertible, not because litotes are necessarily invertible (they
are not) but because misthology limits the outcome to those who qualify for the reward.
Chapter 2. Eternal Rewards                                                                                        Page 97
would not necessarily mean that college basketball players are bad at basketball, only that they are not among the
greatest basketball players. So we cannot throw this not-bad litotes into reverse so as to assume, from that
perspective, that anyone who does not play pro basketball is a bad basketball player, only that such a player is not
among the greatest.
        Similarly, when Jesus promises that overcomers will have no tears in eternity, He means that they will be
among the happiest people in eternity. Accordingly, when He affirms that only overcomers will be among the
happiest people in eternity, one might argue that He does not necessarily mean that subcomers will have tears in
eternity, only that they will not be among the happiest people in eternity.
        The litotetic nature of the promise indicates that something more than merely not being a bad player or not
having tears is being insinuated. Rather, being a great player or a joyful ruler is being indicated. This something-
more perspective hinders me from concluding that if one does not overcome, tears necessarily must be inferred.
Those opposed to my eternal-tear hypothesis technically have a valid point in objecting that since the litotes is
promising something more than no tears and since it is this something more that is limited to overcomers, I cannot
insist, based on the litotes alone, that subcomers will shed tears in the eternal state. Litotetically, I can only prove
that subcomers will not have this something-more, namely, superlative joy. With nothing more than these litotes to
work with, I cannot prove deductively that subcomers necessarily will weep eternally. I can only speak in terms of
inductive probability. The SML results on other matters leads me to believe that misthological tears are eternal,
even if only expressed on rare occasions.

                                                       WYSIWYG
       On the other hand, Chitwood does not speak in litotetic terms when discussing Rev 21:4. To express his
position in computer terminology, he takes what I would call a simple non-litotetic WYSIWYG (What-You-See-
Is-What-You-Get) approach. In the computer industry WYSIWYG is a term used to describe an image such as text
on your computer screen that corresponds very closely to its actual appearance when printed. The text promises no
tears and no death for overcomers. So, if you qualify for the promise, you will have no tears and no death.
Conversely, if you do not qualify for the promise, you will have tears and death. What you see in the text, tears and
death, are what you will get, as a closely corresponding result, if you do not meet the qualifications required by the
text. The corresponding result may either be literal, metaphorical, or correlative, depending on the nature of the
promise. This approach is elegant in its simplicity. Therefore, some may immediately infer with deductive certainty
from this WYSIWYG approach that subcomers will shed tears in eternity. The weakness in this approach is that it
assumes that the text is not litotetic. Detractors, naturally, will object by asserting that the text is litotetic and thus
deny that tears on the part of subcomers are necessarily eternal.
                                      Illustration 59. S-M-L Litotetic Spectrum




       Since I concur with my detractors that the text is litotetic and recognize that litotes cannot be necessarily
assumed to operate in reverse, I have derived my conclusion that the tears on the part of subcomers are eternal from
other means than a simplistic WYSIWYG. Nevertheless, the principle of correlativity, as discussed in Darkness
allows the formulation of the limited-litotetic-inversion theory being hypothesized herein. A litotetic WYSIWYG
can achieve the same results inductively that the simplistic WYSIWYG does deductively. Specifically, a litotetic
WYSIWYG can conclude that the tears on the part of unfaithful believers continue into the eternal state. This theory
of limited litotetic reversion needs now to be put to the test scientifically.
       Expressing this theory in programming terms will facilitate this examination. Defining our terms is the place
to begin. Let S represent the soteriological range of values, M represent the correlating misthological midrange
range of values (having litotetic WYSIWYG characteristics in the present situation), and L the litotetic range of
values. The data set moves in ascending value from S to M to L. As to the function of the literary expression, if a
Page 98                                                                                 Misthological Models: Part 2
literal result is rendered impossible because a literal value would transgress into soteriological range, thus yielding
a nonsensical value, then the effect must be metaphorical or correlative.
        Because of the litotetic nature of the literary data being examined, the value for S is expressed by a negation,
such as no death or no tears. A litotes is a promise that makes a no-S or not-S statement. For example, if you
overcome, you will have no-death; that is, you will not-be-hurt by the second death. Soteriological death or being
hurt literally by death in the Lake of Fire is the S value. Being hurt literally by the second death is impossible for
any believer.212 Hence, in denying that faithful believers will be hurt by the second death, which is literally
impossible for believers even if they are unfaithful, the text is assigning an L (litotetic) value to overcoming
regarding such death as it applies to faithful believers. The L value is the something-more aspect that is greater than
a literal S (soteric) value. In this case, L would be a metaphorical promise of misthological life: Life > Death. For
instance, the overcomer is given a crown of life instead of misthological death.
        However, my theory of limited litotetic reversal (LLR) postulates that in an S-M-L promise, the failure on
the part of the believer to obtain the L value means that the believer will be assigned the M value instead. Thus, if
you are (B) a believer and (C) overcome, then you will have (L) life in the L range. But if you are a (B) believer and
(~C) do not overcome, then you will have misthological (D) death in the M range. The soteriological death from
which overcomers are delivered is reasonably inferred to have a corresponding negative value for the subcomer that
virtually simulates, and indeed mirrors, the negativity implied by such imagery, yet falling within the appropriate
range of values, where L > M > S.213 Jointly, these acronyms combine to yield a LLR-SML (limited-litotetic-
reversal—soteric-mistholic-litotetic) model. The reversal is limited because one cannot perform a mistholic reversal
on a soteric benefit.
        In the case of tears, literal approximation is doubtlessly reasonable and therefore preferable. Use the literal
sense unless it yields nonsense lest your alternative results in nonsense. Therefore, this limited-litotetic-reversion
theory contends that the biblical writer intends to imply, via limited imitation, that subcomers literally will shed
tears in the eternal state. They will shed tears like unbelievers but not exactly like unbelievers. The sorrow
experienced by the unfaithful is experienced in a controlled, virtual environment where they are not subject to the
literal flames associated with the sorrow of the unbelieving.
        If you are a subcomer, you will be hurt by the second death in a metaphorical manner that correlates to the
manner described by the text. In a manner similar to—but not identical with—the way unbelievers are cast into the
Lake of Fire, unfaithful believers will be cast into the outer darkness. Because unfaithful believers cannot literally
be cast into the Lake of Fire during the eternal state, the correlation must be metaphorical in its application
to unfaithful believers. In the case of tears, though, there is no need to assign a metaphorical value. Thus, one
would infer that subcomers literally will have tears in the eternal state. Even so, subcomers will not cry unceasingly
for all eternity since M > S; nevertheless, there is no logical constraint preventing unfaithful believers from weeping
intermittently throughout eternity.
        Contextually, this no-tear promise is limited to overcomers and pertains to eternity. Consequently, one may
deduce, with absolute certainty, if the non-litotetic WYSIWYG perspective is correct, that subcomers will
experience tears and death during the eternal state. I already have proven this to be the case regarding misthological
death, even apart from the simple non-litotetic WYSIWYG perspective. That is, I did so from a litotetic premise,
which was more difficult. Nevertheless, if I drop the litotetic perspective in order to adopt the simple non-litotetic
WYSIWYG vantage point, my point regarding the tears would be proven immediately: Subcomers will experience
misthological tears during the eternal state.
        Notwithstanding the attractiveness of applying Occam’s Razor to adopt such a simplistic WYSIWYG
perspective, I cannot, in good conscience, simply adopt that vantage point to prove my point regarding the tears. A
scientist cannot force the data to fit his theory. Fidelity to the data is paramount, and my analysis in Darkness in
favor of a litotetic perspective is far too strong to be ignored. I cannot opt for a simplistic WYSIWYG at the expense

212
      The possible exception of Hollandsworth’s model is mentioned but not pursued in the present discussion.
213
      For those who prefer logical summations:

Chapter 2. Eternal Rewards                                                                                       Page 99
of the litotes. Instead, I am postulating a correlative litotetic WYSIWYG model in which the eternal tears are
perceived as a secondary function of the litotetic thrust. In other words, the theory being proposed in the present
treatment is that John intends for his readers to perceive an appropriate WYSIWYG applicability in the limited
reversal of soteric-mistholic litotes as it applies to believers who fail to achieve the desired results. Not only are
negative mistholic consequences intended for those believers who fail to achieve the positive mistholic results, but
those negative consequences are the very terms negated in the soteric protasis.
       First and foremost, John wants overcomers to be assured litotetically, and thus implicitly, that in not
experiencing either tears or death in the eternal state, they will have supreme joy and life in the eternal state.
Secondly, in limiting this promise to overcomers, John wants subcomers to be warned. with equal implicit clarity,
that they will experience both misthological tears and misthological death in the eternal state.

                                             Testing the LLR-SML Theory
        This theory is testable against the data. If it is correct, then whenever one finds a soteric-mistholic litotes
(SML) in Johannine literature (or at least in Revelation), one should be able to perceive that the negative matter
being denied as applying to overcomers has an intended inverse misthological application to unfaithful believers
that is so strong that not only does the positive something-more value not apply to the subcomer but the negative
imagery itself applies in some manner to the subcomer as well, either literally, metaphorically, or correlatively.
        I pulled the litotetic chart from Darkness and refitted it with soteric and mistholic columns for the present
analysis. As a reminder, those litotes that formally state the negation are formal litotes; whereas, informal litotes
that merely imply the negation are functional litotes.214 The terminology formal litotes refers to those constructions
that explicitly state the negation, and functional litotes refers to those constructions which do not. In the chart below,
Jn 15:20b, 1Jn 3:17, Rev 3:10, 7:17, and 21:4 (concerning the tears) are litotes in function rather than in form and
are denoted as such by the bold italic font. They are litotes in essence since they state the opposite by negation of
the negative idea or concept. They might thus be appropriately called conceptual litotes.

      Although most of the litotes are not applicable, I have included the full range of data for anyone wanting to
perform a peer review of the data. As can be readily seen from the chart, the only applicable litotes are those
found in Revelation, and even here the data is limited to a few passages: Rev 2:11; 3:5,10; 20:6; 21:4; 21:27(?).

      Rev 2:11; 20:6; and 21:4 are Invertible
       As ready noted in Darkness and herein, Rev 2:11 supports LLR-SML (with the latter acronym being color
coded here as S-M-L): “He who overcomes shall not be hurt by the second death.” Or as stated in Rev 20:6, “the
second death has no power” over overcomers. Believers who overcome will have misthological life, which falls
into the L range. Believers who do not overcome will experience misthological death, which falls into the M range.
John expects his readers to be able to discern, upon reflection, that the promise of not being hurt by the second death
Chapter 2. Eternal Rewards                                                                                    Page 101
has correlative negative applicability to unfaithful believers. They will be hurt misthologically by the second death
in a metaphorical manner. Chitwood and I concur on that matter. This misthological death is certainly millennial in
scope. However, in Rev 21:4 the no-death promise is made to overcomers (cp. Rev 21:7) in regards to the eternal
state. Therefore, consistency would dictate that misthological death is intended to apply to unfaithful believers
during the eternal state as well.
       A similar argument could be made regarding no tears. The no-tear promise made to overcomers in Rev 21:4
pertains to the eternal state. Because it is litotetic I cannot beg the question by assuming that the inverse applies to
nonovercomers. That is the point to be proven. However, I can substantiate that point by pointing back at Rev 7:17.
The litotetic promise of no-tears for overcomers in that passage pertains to the millennial kingdom. Yet we know
that those cast into the outer darkness will weep and gnash their teeth in that place. In all three occurrences where
the term outer darkness is used, it is immediately followed with the negative mistholic clarification: “In that place
there shall be weeping and gnashing of teeth” (Mt 8:12; 22:13; 25:30). In Mt 8:12, you are either a diner or a weeper.
Jesus does not pose a neutral ground of simply being a non-diner. In Mt 22:13, you are either dressed appropriately
and allowed to dine at the dinner, or you are cast outside and weep. Again, you are either a diner or a weeper. In
Mt 25:30, you are either a ruler or a weeper. Jesus does not pose a neutral ground of simply being a non-ruler. The
typical supposition that the tears of subcomers are wiped away flies against the data. The wiping away of tears is
promised to overcomers, not to subcomers. Therefore, at a minimum, the reasonable conclusion is that subcomers
will weep during the millennial period. Yet, here again, consistency would dictate that misthological tears are
intended to apply to unfaithful believers during the eternal state as well since they are seen to apply to unfaithful
believers with the same litotetic structure in Rev 7:17 as they are in Rev 21:4.
       John would intend for his readers to infer that the negative being denied as applicable to overcomers during
the applicable time frame would be applicable to subcomers during that respective time frame. In Rev 7:17, he
would intend for his readers to expect that subcomers would experience tears of regret during the millennial
kingdom. Likewise, with logical consistency, using the same literary device, he would expect them to infer tears of
regret on the part of subcomers during the eternal state in Rev 21:4.215 The other litotes in Rev 21:4 would follow
suit. Subcomers will mourn and cry and experience emotional pain in the eternal state.

      Rev 3:5 and 21:27 are Invertible
       The much-debated Rev 3:5 presents an interesting anomaly: “He who overcomes will thus be clothed in white
garments, and I will not erase his name from the Book of Life; instead, I will confess his name before My Father
and before His angels” (Rev 3:5; TM). In Darkness, in my chapter on litotes no less, I considered the proposal by
those misthologists who suggested that John intends to imply that there is a sense in which believer’s names could
be erased misthologically from the Book of Life. Specifically, I interacted with Fuller’s proposal that their
name/reputation is erased misthologically. Several problems with that theory were notable in that discussion from
TOD: (1) Rev 20:15 explicitly states, “If anyone’s name was not found written in the book of life, he was thrown
into the lake of fire.” Therefore, the blotting out of one’s name would seem to have soteriological repercussions.
(2) The names are recorded permanently, even from the foundation of the Earth (Rev 13:18; 17:8). The supposition
that some names actually are erased seems extraneous to such permanence. (3) The litotes is a promise, not a threat.
(4) Litotes cannot be assumed to operate in reverse.

      Names
      Obviously, my new theory of limited litotetic reversal (LLR) for such soteric-mistholic litotes (SML)
challenges my previous objections as to the possibility of a name being erased misthologically. In LLR, I am posing
that SML is a specialized type of litotes that is intended to operate in reverse to a well-defined, limited degree—a
model that had not occurred to me in Darkness. This LLR is intended to convey an implicit threat. As to the first
two objections, they are largely beside the point in LLR because LLR presumes such a soteriological foundation
and incorporates it in its SML functional values. Accordingly, both the soteriological impossibility of blotting out
the names and the soteriological permanency of the names are affirmed by LLR. Objections one and two do not

present any problems for my LLR-SML model.
       Dillow presents both Fuller’s interpretation and the basic litotetic interpretation as equally feasible options,
concluding: “Both interpretations are exegetically sound” (Destiny, 672-676). According to Dillow, you can choose
either interpretation. Accordingly, he should have no objection to my LLR-SML model because it has even more
explanatory power since it demonstrates that you can choose both! LLR-SML adopts the positive litotetic
understanding as the primary understanding intended by the biblical writer, and accepts Fuller’s argument regarding
the negative implications as the secondary intended meaning, all the while eliminating the weaknesses in Fuller’s
argument. In LLR-SML, we have the best of both worlds. The name can be erased misthologically while retained
soteriologically. Rev 3:5 is a promise-threat: Foremost, it is a promise to the overcomer; inversely, it is a threat to
subcomers.
       In the context, as Dillow points out, Jesus warns, “I know your deeds; you have a reputation [anona] of being
alive, but you are dead” (Rev 3:1; NIV). “He who overcomes shall thus be clothed in white garments; and I will not
erase his name from the book of life, and I will confess his name before My Father, and before His angels” (Rev 3:5;
NIV). “He who overcomes will thus be clothed in white garments, and I will not erase his reputation [anona] from
the Book of Life; instead, I will confess his name [anona] before My Father and before His angels” (Rev 3:5; TM).
The conceptual parallels in Mt 10:33 and 2Tim 2:12 contain the negative flipside of what happens to those believers
who do not overcome. Jesus confesses those believers who confess Him, and He denies those believers who deny
Him. He denies them rulership.
                          Illustration 61. Inerasable Name versus Erasable Reputation




       Given this LLR-SML understanding of Rev 3:5, I will amend my subsequent statement in Darkness slightly
regarding the multiple meanings of the Book of Life (BOL). The BOL is used in four different ways in Scripture.
(1) Book of earthly citizenship: it may refer to citizenship on Earth (secular usage). (2) Book of earthly life: it may
refer to life on Earth—(Ex 32:32-33; Ps 69:28a; 139:16). (3) Book of misthological life: it may refer to life in the
Heavenly Jerusalem—(Ps 69:28b; Is 4:3-4; cf. Rev 3:5b; 3:12; 21:27; 22:19?); Lk 10:20 (secondarily?); Phil 3:20
(primarily); Heb 12:23; Rev 3:5b (primarily), 13:8 (primarily?); 17:8 (secondarily?); 20:12,15 (secondarily?); 21:27
(primarily).217 (4) Book of soteriological life: it may refer to life in Heaven—Dan 12:1-2; Lk 10:20 (dualistically);

216
   LLR-SML = limited-litotetic-reversal—soteric-mistholic-litotetic.
217
   I am amending this statement as it was made in Darkness to the more comprehensive term misthological life in
the Heavenly City rather than mere millennial life in the millennial city. This is more consistent with the fact that
rewards are eternal and with the fact that Abraham was looking for a heavenly city (Heb 11:10,16), not a millennial
city, which God had prepared as a reward for him and others like him. Abraham would have been shocked by
Chitwood’s proposal that his sacrifice of Isaac would be misthologically meaningless after 1000 years and that all
believers are allegedly given access to the Heavenly City for all eternity. Abraham rightly would recoil from such
misthology. Unlike Chitwood, with his over-refined millennial distinctions, Abraham was looking for an eternal
reward. Also, I am splitting Rev 3:5 between categories three and four, the first reference to onoma in that verse
now falling into category three, while the second usage remains in category four. Additionally, Rev 21:27 should
probably be considered as operating functionally in the third category, not the forth. This verse implements a soteric-
mistholic antithetical juxtaposition in which believers who qualify morally to enter by the gates into the city are
contrasted with immoral unbelievers outside the city. This verse is not a simple soteriological contrast. I charted it
correctly in Darkness (p. 106) but possibly posed a false dichotomy between the soteric and mistholic possibilities
(p. 1060, n. 919). I am now inclined to see Rev 21:27 as operating in both capacities, but primarily in the
misthological category. Only, but not all, of those names who are recorded soteriologically will enter the city.
Alternatively, the Lamb’s book of life (LBOL) may be a misthological book (see Rewards are Eternal). In any case,
Chapter 2. Eternal Rewards                                                                                    Page 103
Phil 3:20 (dualistically); 4:3 (dualistically?); Rev 3:5b (dualistically); 13:8 (dualistically?); 17:8 (dualistically?);
20:12-15 (dualistically?); 21:27 (dualistically).218
       The Book of Life is a record of citizenship in an earthly city or nation, or in the Heavenly City of Jerusalem
or in Heaven itself. Evidently, the Book of Life was used in the secular world to denote citizenship. One’s name
could be blotted out of this book of birth, and one could lose one’s citizenship. It was also used in the OT as a
metaphor to denote those who had physical life, and of one’s name being erased upon death. This metaphor was
later used in the OT for those whose names are recorded as citizens of the Heavenly Jerusalem. Finally, it was used
to describe those who possess eternal life and thus have citizenship in Heaven.
       There were similarities and differences in the four types of usage. Citizenship in the Heavenly Jerusalem is
a reward. All believers have their name in the Book of Life in the fourth sense, but only overcomers have it recorded
in the third sense. Rev 3:5 integrates both senses. Not being able to have one’s name removed soteriologically is
the soteriological foundation for the litotetic promise that it will not be removed misthologically in the case of
overcomers. Likewise, concerning Rev 21:27, it should be taken in the fourth sense with a third sense result: “And
nothing unclean and no one who practices abomination and lying, shall ever come into it [the city], but only those
whose names are written in the Lamb’s book of life [LBOL].” In this verse, their names are not said to be written in
the LBOL from the foundation of the world. Logically and lexically, only ≠ all (see Darkness). The fact that only
those who are citizens of Heaven will be allowed into the New Jerusalem does not mean that all citizens of Heaven
will be allowed into it. Citizenship in Heaven is only one of the requirements. Yet it is the requirement which
excludes the lost. That it excludes all the lost does not mean that it includes all the saved, however. That only the
saved enter does not mean that all the saved can enter. This assessment, even as it was made in Darkness, is in full
accord with this newer LLR-SML model.
                           Illustration 62. Recorded from the Foundation of the World




       In Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep, I presented two favorable misthological options for the Book of Life.
In one model I posed that the names recorded misthologically could be pictured as being recorded in what I termed
the Book for Life form the foundation of the world.219 One possibility is that since these names are recorded
misthologically from the foundation of the world (cp. Mt 25:34), misthological security is indicated. Their names
will never be erased, and the names of subcomers will never be added. Rewards, and loss of rewards, are eternal.
The names recorded soteriologically in the much larger Book of Life likewise were recorded before the foundation
of the world. That model is still feasible.

        On the other hand, the newer LLR-SML increases the attractiveness of the second model in which the names
within the Book for Life (BFL) are not regarded as being recorded permanently until after the Bema. Thereafter,
their names will be recorded there for all eternity as those for whom the city has been prepared. Until then, a
believer’s name might be erased misthologically but not soteriologically. The type of permanency suggested by the
recording of the names from the foundation of the world applies only soteriologically to the Book of Life, not
misthologically to the Book for Life. In other words, names may be erased misthologically but not soteriologically.
        Indeed, in an article taking the typical soteriological view of the Book of Life, in which the names are
considered to be written there permanently from the foundation of the world, Wilkin interjects an editorial note with
this alternate possibility: “This is the view of many pastors and theologians. Another option is that when a person
believes in Christ his name is added. It depends on how one understands Rev 17:8. Is the Book of Life from the
foundation of the earth, or is the writing in the Book of Life from the foundation?”220 Perhaps a duality is intended
along a soteriological-misthological boundary. Soteriologically, the names are written from the foundation of the
world. Misthologically, the book is from the foundation of the world, but the names are added subsequently.
        Then again, a further synthesis is possible so that there is an additional duality in the misthological realm. In
SML, the Book of Life is only found in one soteriological stratum. Thus, names in the Book of Life would either
be erasable or not erasable but not both—erasable and inerasable. The names of those who will come into
soteriological possession of eternal life are written from the foundation of the world in that book. Their names are
there permanently, never to be erased in the S stratum. However, the Book for Life is found in two misthological
strata. The names might be erasable in one stratum (the M stratum) but not erasable in the other stratum (the L
stratum).
                        Illustration 64. Soterically Inerasable and Litotetically Not Erased




       Promising not to erase the names of overcomers from the Book of Life is a soteriological promise fraught
with misthological meaning, both positive and negative. Erasing believer’s names from the Book of Life is
soteriologically impossible, of course. The very writing of their names in that book from the foundation of the world
suggests permanency. Unconditional security demands it. In that regard, their names are inerasable. So why promise
not to do something that was impossible anyway? Why promise not to erase their names because of their
performance when their names cannot be erased regardless of their performance? According to the LLR theory, the
Lord did so for two reasons: first, to indicate that He would not erase overcomers’ names misthologically. However,

220
      Ron Rosso, “God’s Sovereignty and Man’s Free Will: Another                               Look.”    Available     at

in order for His not erasing the names of overcomers to be a reward, not erasing their names in this capacity must
be limited to overcomers. In order words, when seen in that light, the positive promise necessarily indicates the
inverse for those believers who do not qualify for the promise: Subcomers names must be erased misthologically.
After all, this reward is limited to overcomers.
                                        Illustration 65. Book of Life in SML




      Placing the Book of Life in an SML chart that graphs the quantity/quality of eternal life yields a three-layer
composite which shows that the names of believers written freely in the Book of Life at the first level are recorded
permanently and cannot be erased (pictured by the broken eraser in the blue area). Correlatively, the reputations of
overcomers are recorded in the top layer and, at the very least, cannot be erased from the Book for Life after the
Bema, once the rewards are dispersed (pictured by the broken eraser in the yellow area). At that time their
reputations will become misthologically inerasable because rewards are eternal. Jesus is hinting strongly at their
misthological permanency by saying that, as a reward, He will not erase their names. What about believers who do
not overcome? The implication is that their names must be erasable misthologically. The reputation of every
believer in this middle level will be erased from the Book for Life.
      Since the names in the Book for Life are plausibly recorded before the foundation of the world, just like they
were for the Book of Life, it may be best to picture the eraser as being broken for both books (i.e., the Book of Life
and the Book for Life) before the foundation of the world (in the S and L strata). The names (i.e., reputations) of
subcomers, on the other hand, are misthologically erasable with the result that only the names (i.e., reputations) of
overcomers are recorded in the Book for Life from the foundation of the world. The records in the Book of Life are
permanent. No more names are added to it or taken away from it. Likewise, after the Bema, those believers who
have their reputations erased misthologically will never have the misthological rewards promised to overcomers.
Rewards, and lack thereof, are eternal.221 The quality of eternal life experienced by subcomers will never rise to that

221
   Plantinga presents a freewill defense (FWD) for the problem of evil, arguing that it was logically necessary for
God to create a cosmos in which evil would occur in order to create creatures that were capable of moral good.
Alvin Plantinga, God, Freedom, and Evil (Grand Rapids: Eerdmans Publishing Company, 1974), 30. As explained
in Darkness (pp. 218-220), I find such rationale to be unpersuasive. God is capable of moral good, but He is not
capable of evil. Furthermore, in the eternal state, believers will be like God in this respect. They will not be capable
of sin. Plantinga’s premise that creatures capable of moral good must be capable of moral evil is false. Additionally,
he wrongly restricts morally significant actions to those actions that are evil or not depending upon whether they
are performed. Would Christ have been guilty of evil if He had not died on the cross? No. Yet His death was most
certainly morally significant! We cannot say that it would have been wrong for Christ not to have died on the cross
Page 106                                                                                Misthological Models: Part 2
of overcomers. Their names (i.e., reputations) will not be rewritten in the Book for Life at the end of the millennium
if they are erased at the Bema.
        In Woolly Wolves, this Book for life was entertained as preferable, hypothetical, conceptual working model.
In the present series, the reality of such a Bema Book is affirmed as being pragmatically necessary. See Bema Book
in Part 4 of this series (MMP4). The Bema Book is the edited form of the Book for Life. Initially, the Book for Life
is the same size as the Book of Life. It is a misthological copy of its soteriological counterpart. At the Bema, those
believers found unworthy of having their names in the misthological Book for Life will have their names erased
from that book. Whether this is a literal erasing, I cannot say definitively at present. The Book for Life might just
be a conceptual record and the erasing merely metaphorical. Then again, I certainly can entertain the possibility of
a literal record from which names are expunged at the Bema. For sake of illustration, I will assume that the Book
for Life is a literal copy of the Book of Life. Both books are literal. If so, the names of subcomers will be removed
literally from the Book for Life at the Bema. The resulting, smaller, edited copy of the Book for Life is what I
would term the Bema Book. This Bema Book is literal; moreover, it is already in existence, with the names of
overcomers written from the foundation of the world, of that I can be reasonably certain, as explained in Part 4.
Perhaps, after the Bema is completed, the Bema Book will be brought forth and shown to be in perfect accord with
the edited Book for Life in order that the prophetic certainty entailed within the Bema Book, written from the

for us. He did it out of grace and love, not because He owed it to us. His death for us was both morally free and
significant though He could have chosen not to die for us. One must not limit morally significant events to obligatory
events. God would not have been guilty of evil if He had chosen not to die for our evil.
       What might be entertained, however, is the possibility that for an action to be misthologically significant it
must be performed in a context of evil: for example, in a position where evil is possible or in opposition to possible
evil. Certainly, Christ’s death was misthologically significant in this regard. Likewise, we are in a battle. To do
good we must battle the evil inside our flesh, the evil outside of us in the world, and the devil himself. The time for
earning misthologically significant rewards is now, not in eternity future when all opposition from evil has been
removed and the possibility for our committing evil is nonexistent.
        In his chapter, “The Problem of Evil,” Craig recaps Plantinga’s freewill defense but commendably advances
beyond it to note that the knowledge of God is the chief objective, an incommensurable good, that includes the
knowledge that “when God asks his children to bear horrible suffering in this life, it is only with the prospect of a
heavenly joy and recompense that is beyond all comprehension,” as Paul says in 2Cor 4:17: “For momentary, light
affliction is producing for us an eternal weight of glory far beyond all comparison” (p. 547). J. P. Moreland and
William Lane Craig, Philosophical Foundations for a Christian Worldview (Downers Grove: InterVarsity Press,
2003). I prefer a soul-making-theodicy (SMT) argument over the FWD for the explanation of the problem of evil.
In a qualified-end-justifies-the-means (QEJTM), God allowed evil so that we could know Him better, which
includes knowing Him as a rewarder (Heb 11:6). Moral evil was for the greater good (GG), in other words, and
misthology is a large part of that greater good. See MMP4 and MSIH. The only way for the Father to form a Bride
for Christ was to allow the presence of moral evil. Believers form the body of Christ. But only by overcoming evil
do believers become misthologically suitable to be a Bride for Christ. Just as it takes heat and pressure to turn coal
into a diamond and an irritant to cause an oyster to produce a pearl, moral evil was necessary for believers to be
made into a mature Bride. Only by overcoming such evil could faithful believers become experiential and
misthological partakers in the divine nature (2Pet 1:4-8). See MMP3.
       For eternal life to be gained at the misthological level, it must be gained in a misthologically significant
context. For Chitwood to pose that God is going to do away with rewards by dispensing them willy-nilly at the end
of the millennial kingdom is silly. God does not cease to be a rewarder at the end of the millennial kingdom. One
thing we learn about God as a result of all this evil is that He is a rewarder (Heb 11:6). He rewards misthologically
significant actions with an eternal weight of glory far beyond all comparison—not a mere millennial measure of
glory that may be computed to last 1000 years and compared to our lifetime of suffering on Earth. This eternal
reward is incommensurable, not measurable! Eternal life is knowing God (Jn 17:3). Bridal believers are going to
know Him much better and much more intimately than non-bridal believers. Faithful believers will have a
misthological experience of eternal life that far exceeds those believers who are content with the soteriological
dimension of eternal life. A simple syllogism will suffice for summation:
       1. God’s nature is to reward.
       2. God’s nature does not change.
       3. Therefore, God’s nature to reward us does not change.

foundation of the world, may be shown to be in infallible agreement with judgments worked out in real time at the
Bema.
      After I had created the above model, I then read where Hollandsworth has posed three books as one
possibility: (1) physical life (body), (2) eternal life (spirit), and (3) spiritual life (soul). Whipple had made the same
suggestion,222 and I had mentioned such possibilities in TOD and rejected them:

               The misthological Book of Life may be considered by some to be the book of millennial life
        and by others the book of soul life. In the case of the latter, the books accordingly would be labeled
        the books of physical, soulical, spiritual life and pertain respectively to the salvation of the body, soul,
        and spirit. Another possibility would be to assume multiple entries in one Book of Life. For example,
        it could be argued that just as the tree of life in Rev 22:19 has fruit that is limited to the overcomer in
        the city but leaves that are available to those outside the city, so the Book of Life might have double
        entries: one for those who (will) have eternal life as a gift and another for those who still are potentially
        qualified to inherit eternal life. The latter entry could be erased (Rev 3:5). Rather than suggest multiple
        books or multiple entries in one book, the present writer believes the singular book (with exclusively
        soteriological entries) is being used in two senses in Revelation: soteriologically and misthologically
        (the latter litotetically). (TOD, 990, n. 127)

      In Woolly Wolves, however, I warmed up to the multiple book hypothesis. Names could be erased
misthologically from the latter book, the salvation of the soul being a misthological theme. If that is the case, then
the names left in the latter book would entail the permanent record after the Bema, thus the Bema Book. Yet these
names would have been recorded as successful overcomers from the foundation of the world. Hollandsworth and I
appear to have come to much the same conclusion. Then again, he suggests, “Perhaps instead of three books, God
has one book with rows and columns, much like a ledger or spreadsheet.”223 This suggestion is also plausible and
was entertained in TOD.

in Christ for eternal life have been recorded permanently in the BLS from the foundation of the world. But what
about check marks? (1) In row one, Jane Smith has not yet been conceived, so she has no check mark yet, except

recorded soteriologically from the foundation of the world in the first column, and her check mark as an overcomer
has been recorded misthologically from the foundation of the world in the last column. These two columns are
static. The inner three columns are dynamic in that these entries are only made as they become applicable. The
middle column (soteriological life) is dynamic-static in that once a check mark is entered in this column (at the
inception of regeneration), it cannot be erased. (2) In row two, John Smith has been born but has not yet believed,

foundation of the world. (5) In row five, Paul Smith has died but failed to qualify for the crown of life. Therefore,
his check mark is erased from the potential column. He only has soteriological life.

        Crowns
        If the names (i.e., reputations) of subcomers are erased literally at the Bema, then obviously their names must
have been recorded in this sense before the Bema. In this capacity, the names of all believers must be recorded
before the Bema. These names must have been recorded previously; otherwise, they could not be erased. Jesus
likewise warns, “Hold fast what you have, in order that no one take your crown” (Rev 3:11). Are these crowns
merely possessed potentially so that we only lose the crown that potentially could have been ours? Or might we
actually possess crowns in some capacity before the Bema, and might they be taken away from us if we do not pass
the Bema? Do we merely lose the crown awaiting us, or do we lose the crown we already have? Along with everyone
else, I have always assumed the former. Yet, quite accidently, while I was researching a UFO anomaly, in which
evidence was presented that fallen angles take the souls of the damned to power their UFOs, Keith Call posed a
proposition that lead me to consider the latter position. Call responded, “My theory about demonic interference
holds that they are attempting to prevent Christians from earning crowns which are currently resting on their
heads.”224
        I mistakenly thought that Call was referring to crowns currently on the heads of believers, an idea that I
immediately brushed aside until I remembered, synchronistically, that I had a note on my desk to remind me to
mention that the minas and talents subcomers lose at the Bema are minas and talents that we currently possess.
“Therefore take away the talent from him, and give it to the one who has the ten talents. For to everyone who has
shall more be given, and he shall have an abundance; but from the one who does not have, even what he does have
shall be taken away” (Mt 25:228-29). “Take the mina away from him, and give it to the one who has the ten minas.’
And they said to him, ‘Master, he has ten minas already.’ I tell you, that to everyone who has shall more be given,
but from the one who does not have, even what he does have shall be taken away” (Lk 19:24-26). How do you lose
something that you have but do not have unless, in a dualistic sense, you both have it and do not have it? As is
frequently the case, the Scripture encourages us to look at the matter dualistically. Losing what one does not have
refers to losing what one might have gained potentially in the future. Losing what one does have refers to losing
what one currently has before one loses it in the future.225
        When I inquired further, Call clarified that he simply had meant the crown that believers are trying to win
are the crowns currently being worn by the fallen angels, so believers are trying to take away the crowns currently
resting on the heads of these fallen angels. This is a plausible theory that I also hold. However, given that our
miscommunication had caused me to wonder about the alternate possibility, I thought it might be prudent to at least
mention it, inasmuch as my model of the outer darkness allows the possibility that all believers are currently in New
Jerusalem eating from the tree of life, but at the Bema unfaithful believers will find these privileges, which they are
currently enjoying, taken away from them in accordance with Rev 22:19: “If anyone takes away from the words of

224
    Personal correspondence, 6-28-2014.

    My hypothesis is that believers currently have a soulical crown of life that can be taken away from them at the

that they potentially may receive at the Bema but will not receive until that future point in time if they qualify. The
soulical crown refers to bringing over your past capacities to the future in such a way that your capacities developed
in the past, and which are a part of you, will enhance your future enjoyment. The misthological crown, contrastively,
is a future reward for your past performance. This future crown is objectively external to who you are presently.
Because it is future, it is not a part of you—yet.

        In Darkness, I posed the standard misthological view in which we do not currently have the crown that we

      This biblical picture explains how believers can lose the crown of life. We do not have it yet, but by
      pressing on in the race, so as to not be disqualified for the prize set before us, we will obtain that crown.

      eternal life will flat line into misthological death. Qualitatively, in terms of having misthological life,

      quantitative possession of eternal life as a free gift and entering the kingdom and living within it, their
      experience of eternal life cannot drop below the soteriological threshold. (Darkness, 1087, n. 1277.)

      Souls
      Death can be defined as the loss of life. However, when defined from that perspective, death normally would
not be defined as the loss of potential life but as the loss of actual life—life actually possessed. Could it be that if
we are unfaithful, we actually lose our life that we currently possess at that moment at the Bema? Obviously, in
misthological circles we know it is possible to lose one’s soul-life at the Bema:

      Then Jesus said to His disciples,” If anyone wishes to come after Me, let him deny himself, and take
      up his cross, and follow Me. For whoever wishes to save his life shall lose it; but whoever loses his
      life for My sake shall find it. For what will a man be profited, if he gains the whole world, and forfeits
      his soul? Or what will a man give in exchange for his soul? For the Son of Man is going to come in
      the glory of His Father with His angels; and will then recompense every man according to his deeds.”
      (Mt 16:24-27)

        We currently possess that soul-life. Might the misthological loss of this currently-possessed soul-life have a
literal, even visible, fulfillment? If our names or reputations are literally in a book, from which they literally and
visibly can be erased at the Bema, as a number of writers pose, then why not allow the possibility that unfaithful
believers will just as literally and visibly lose their soul-lives? Perhaps seeing one’s name erased from the book will
suffice for a visible representation of what it means to lose one’s currently-possessed soul-life. Then again, just
maybe, there is something to the synchronicity in which all these strands of thought just happened to come together
in a so-called accidental manner while I was reading a book by Redfern, describing how unbelievers can lose their
soul-lives. If synchronicity, biblical consistency, and correlativity are allowed a voice, then perhaps crown-shaped
orbs of soul-light will be visibly seen to be taken from unfaithful believers and given to faithful believers. If the
research done by Redfern is correct, then it is not so much a question of, “Can this occur?” but “Will this occur?”
        If the unregenerate can lose their souls in a manner described by Redfern, then why can the regenerate not
lose their souls in some similar manner, especially since Christ is warning believers about losing their souls in a
manner that is applicable to both unbelievers and believers? For such reasons, the examples cited by Redfern will
be allowed a fair hearing. Redfern relates the NDE (near-death experience) of Paul Garratt:

           While in his practically deceased state, however, Garratt underwent a bizarre and frightening so-
      called near-death experience, which he briefly discussed in a letter to his local newspaper, after his
Page 110                                                                                  Misthological Models: Part 2
         return to full health. It was this communication to the press, in which Garratt described seeing
         numerous UFOs in some hellish realm while the doctors worked frantically to kick-start his heart....
               Garratt also experienced this ubiquitous tunnel of bright light [experienced by other NDEs], but
         he was suddenly and violently hauled from its comforting glow and enveloping warmth into a far
         darker and much colder dimension. Garratt told the doctor who was consulting for the Collins Elite
         that he felt himself falling at high-speed—which, with hindsight, later made him wonder if he was
         actually descending into the very pits of Hell itself. But, if his final destination was Hell, then it was
         certainly not a Hell like that described in the pages of the Bible or traditionally portrayed in Christian
         teachings.
               As the doctor listened carefully and took copious notes, and as both a microphone and a hidden-
         camera recorded his every word, Garratt said that he was confronted by a never-ending, utterly flat,
         light blue, sandy landscape that was dominated by a writhing mass of an untold number of naked
         human beings, screaming in what sounded like torturous agony. Above them he saw a purple sky filled
         with hundreds of flying saucer-like objects that pulsed and throbbed, almost as if they were living,
         breathing entities in their own right. The objects busily raced back and forth across the skyline in a
         fashion that Garratt likened to seemingly metallic soldier-ants or worker-bees performing never-
         ending, vitally important tasks.
               Garratt never felt the calmness and tranquility that others who have experienced NDEs have
         reported. Quite the opposite, in fact; his mind—or his disembodied life-force, perhaps—was in utter
         turmoil as he watched the flying saucer-like craft suddenly slow down their movements to an eventual
         standstill high above the mass of people below, and then bathed each and every one of them in a green,
         sickly glow. What happened next was even worse, Garratt told the doctor: small balls of light seemed
         to fly from the bodies of the people into the green glow. These small balls were then “sucked up
         into the flying saucers.” At that point, an eerie and deafening silence overcame the huge mass of
         people, who duly rose to their feet as one and collectively stumbled and shuffled in the hundreds
         of thousands across the barren landscape—like in a George Romero zombie movie—towards a
         large black-hole that had now materialized in the distance. Suddenly, Garratt felt disorientated and
         the next thing he knew he was groggily coming to his senses in a hospital bed. Thanks to the work of
         the doctors, he firmly believed, he had been spared the wrath of the Grim Reaper and the Devil himself.
               Garratt admitted to the doctor at the University of South Carolina that he was not a particularly
         religious man, but he had come to a terrifying conclusion about his experience: either it had all been a
         horrible and vivid nightmare provoked by his subconscious as he neared death, or he had briefly visited
         the gateway to Hell itself, a place where living-machines matching the descriptions of flying saucers
         literally harvested human souls—those countless balls of light that he saw exiting the bodies of the
         huge throng, which he also concluded were being utilized to fuel those piloting the craft. Then, after
         the reaping was over, the now soulless and zombie-like multitude took its inevitable, stumbling
         steps that led towards the bleak and foreboding dark hole that had appeared in the distance, and
         finally on to the road to Hell. (Italics his.)226

One UFO abduction victim, Tammy Stone, related repeated apocalyptic nightmares of a disturbing afterlife in which
“Gray-like entities fed—vampire-style—on human souls.229 Some report that the entities seemly want to eat or take
their souls from them.230 This graphic picture of losing one’s soul goes beyond the biblical data; nevertheless, it
may not necessarily go against the biblical data. Good science would factor in all plausible data, so formulating a
lose-what-you-already-have BEMA model that could accommodate this range of data seems reasonable.
        If there is any truth to this UFO and NDE data and if a correlating misthological reality applies to believers,
then one may theorize that when Jesus warns unbelievers and believers of losing their souls and warns believers
that the talents they currently possess can be taken away from them and given to faithful believers, He may be
indicating that, among other things, the subjective capacity of unfaithful believers to experience life will be
diminished before their objective capacity to experience life is implemented. Will they be soulless zombies? No.
They will weep and gnash their teeth, not only for their objective loss but for their subjective loss as well. Thus,
they certainly are not zombies. Not only will they miss out on receiving the crown of spiritual life that they
potentially could have achieved, but they will forfeit the crown of soulical life they currently possess. If fallen
angels can take the souls of unbelievers from them, then perhaps the Lord’s angels can take the souls of unfaithful
believers from them, especially when Jesus commands His holy angels: “Therefore take away the soulical-talent
from him, and give it to the one who has the ten soulical-talents.” Perhaps those believers losing their soulical-talent
literally will see their soulical-talent taken away from them by an angel and given to another believer in the form
of a small, crown-shaped ball of light. If that is what it looks like, what might it actually feel like?
        Suppose a scholar who has mastered the biblical languages and has accumulated much biblical knowledge is
found guilty at the Bema of having taught a false gospel. That scholar, who prizes his knowledge but who failed to
exercise it properly, might conceivably find that knowledge taken away from him and given to a faithful believer
who then would add that scholar’s knowledge to his already existing knowledge. The faithful believer’s capacity to
exercise his gift of knowledge would be greatly enhanced by this additional knowledge. The unfaithful scholar will
not be a zombie, but he will dearly miss the knowledge that he worked so hard to acquire and will weep bitterly as
that knowledge is taken away and given to another believer. Moreover, the loss is eternal. He will not be able to go
back and regain the knowledge he has lost. Furthermore, since he has proven to be a poor steward of the knowledge
that he was given, one need not assume that he will be given opportunity to resume his studies. The knowledge he
prized so much and the opportunity to acquire further knowledge at the university level will be taken away
permanently. No longer will he have the opportunity to study to show himself approved. Let us be careful with the
knowledge we acquire. Those believers who cannot perceive the basic threshold differential between a gift and a
reward certainly expose themselves to tremendous misthological danger. Perceiving two thresholds is even better.
