                                        Illustration 67. Book of Life in SML




Two thresholds are seen in the chart. One threshold is soteric, the other mistholic. The primary point of a SML
construction is the SL segment; it composes an explicit, positive promise for overcomers. The LM segment is
secondary, negative, and implicit; it presumes a negative inverse is partially applicable to subcomers. The inverse
drops below the misthological threshold that separates rulers from non-rulers, but it does not drop into a buffer
zone, M, that separates the S and L zones. The M zone is decidedly not neutral, however, in regard to its
misthological implications. Misthologically speaking, the M zone is definitely negative. It is not a zone where
reputations are merely absent; it is a zone where names are erased. It is not a zone where tears merely are not wiped
away; it is a zone where tears actually are present. Whereas L is the zone for the rulers, M is the zone for the
weepers, those believers who are disqualified from being kingdom rulers.
                                          Illustration 68. A SML is a SMJ




       The (SML) soteric-mistholic-litotetic construction is simply a specialized form of the (SMJ) soteric-
mistholic-juxtaposed expression in which the (J) juxtaposition implements a (L) litotes in the antithetical contrast.
In both the SML and the SMJ, the M component is not explicitly stated but rather is merely implied contextually
and, therefore, is secondary. The M element is in the background, not the foreground. Therefore, in an SMJ passage,
such as the sheep and the goats, one would not expect the M category to be expressed explicitly.
       In terms of practical application of this hermeneutical theory, one is not surprised that, in a soteric-mistholic
contrast such as that found in the goats versus sheep (Mt 25:31-46), for example, goatish sheep (sheep that act like
goats) are not included. Contextually, such poorly performing believers are implied, and the outer darkness is
applied to them contextually, but one would not expect the goatish sheep (comprising the midrange data) to be
mentioned explicitly in a SMJ pericope making an antithetical contrast.
Chapter 2. Eternal Rewards                                                                                      Page 113
         Rev 3:10 is Invertible
        The only SML text that remains to be considered to test the LLR-SML theory is Rev 3:10.231 Contextually,
Christ seems to be saying that overcomers will not have to go through the hour of testing/trial (peirasmos) that
comes upon the world during the tribulations to test/try (peirazo) the world because the overcomers have persevered
and not denied Christ’s name. In Darkness, one reason I opposed throwing this litotes into reverse is because doing
so would yield the faulty conclusion that subcomers would have to go through the tribulation. It is provable that the
rapture is unconditional (see DFS), so such reversion is impossible. At that time, though, I had not considered that
a limited reversal might be indicated by the data and thus that the promise might contain a veiled warning. Even so,
the text immediately proceeds to provide a warning to this church in the very next verse: “I am coming quickly;
hold fast what you have, in order that no one take your crown” (Rev 3:11). Obviously, a warning cannot be derived
from the litotes by throwing the litotes into full reverse, as correctly perceived in Darkness. But given the SML
(soteric-mistholic-litotetic) structure, LLR (limited litotetic reversal) is itself properly and completely opposed to
full reversal. And this litotes falls into that structure since an unconditional rapture is part of the soteriological
benefit promised to all believers. Being raptured falls within the S range of the SML litotes; receiving a crown is in
the L range. Darkness presumed a simple SL (soteric-litotetic) litotes. The SML model had not yet been developed
fully, although observations were being made that eventually would lead to the formulation of this model.
        The earlier SL model used in Darkness would only treat a soteric-mistholic-litotetic text as moving forward
in the positive direction. That the biblical writer would intend for his soteric-mistholic-litotetic to be inverted
partially, so as to include a secondary warning, was not considered formally, only correlatively. The SML litotetical
model had not yet been developed sufficiently to allow formalizing a working hypothesis. Therefore, using the
hermeneutical tools available to me at the time, I had concluded:

                This promise simply may be considered a litotes. During the time when the world is being tested,
         these faithful believers will be receiving their crowns instead of experiencing the tribulation. Judgment
         begins with us (the house of God), but when it moves to the world, we will be removed to Heaven to
         receive our crowns. Thus, the Lord is promising these overcomers that He will crown them rather than
         test them. Testing during that period is contrasted with being crowned during that period. When it
         comes time for the world to be tested, it will be time for overcomers to be crowned. Contextually, this
         verse is best considered a conceptual litotes. (Darkness, 505-506.)

                 I understand the...passage as a litotetic promise to overcomers, and the nature of litotes is such
         that it is unsafe to assume that they can be thrown into reverse. In fact, given that Lang is considered
         one of the ablest proponents of the partial rapture position, his concession made concerning the nature
         of litotes (in his comments on Rev 2:11) is monumental: “It is not safe to reverse divine statements
         and draw a negative inference from a positive statement, as is done by inferring here that a believer
         who does not overcome will be hurt of the second death” (Revelation, 96; emphasis his). Even partial
         rapturists acknowledge that throwing litotes into reverse is unwise! (Darkness, 1079, n. 1208.)

       These arguments are still fully viable against full reversal, of course. Nevertheless, as has been shown herein,
and even allowed through the principle of correlativity within Darkness, a believer who does not overcome will be
hurt correlatively by the second death. Hence, the litotetic promise about not being hurt by the second death is
reversible misthologically to the correlative extent. Given the soteric-mistholic-litotetic nature of the data, the SML
model is better able to perform the analysis than a simple SL model. Naturally, a SL model could not allow a
reversal because any reversal would have been a full reversal and failed to allow the possibility of misthological
failure without implying soteriological failure, which is an impossibility. But an SML can accommodate a secondary
reversal from the L range back to the M range. In fact, SML would expect the applicability of a limited inversion
for those believers who do not qualify for the L range of the promise. The LLR (limited litotetic reversal) model is
thus the more robust model, having greater explanatory power, and is better suited for the literary structure.
       The presumption, then, would be that Jesus intends for the testing of Rev 3:10 to apply correlatively to
subcomers. Do the facts confirm this hypothesis? We know from Jesus’ words in Lk 8:13 that some believers fall
away in their time of testing (peirasmos) and that those who stand with Jesus in His trials (peirasmos) will be given

231
      LLR-SML = limited-litotetic-reversal—soteric-mistholic-litotetic.
Page 114                                                                                Misthological Models: Part 2
thrones in the coming kingdom (Lk 22:28-30). James also notes, “Blessed is a man who perseveres under trial
[peirasmos]; for once he has been approved, he will receive the crown of life, which the Lord has promised to those
who love Him” (Jam 1:12).232
       The fact that God does not tempt (peirazo) us in Jam 1:13 should be read in conjunction with the noun test
(peirasmos) in Jam 1:2, which apparently describes a test that comes from the hand of God. The LXX repeatedly
uses this verb to affirm that God does indeed actively test His children. “God tested Abraham” (Gen 22:1). God
tested Israel (Ex 15:25; 16:4; 20:20; Dt 13:3; Judges 3:1). David even invites God to test him (Ps 26:2). In the NT,
Paul affirms that God allows us to be tested (1Cor 10:13). Lest it be thought that God merely passively allows the
NT believer to be tested, let it be remembered that the writer of Hebrews uses this verb to remind us that Abraham
was tested (Heb 11:17), which takes us back to the Genesis account in which God was the active agent performing
the test: “God tested Abraham” (Gen 22:1). In fact, Jesus tested Philip (Jn 6:5-6). Therefore, one duality that can
be seen in this singular verb is used in a dual sense: God does not tempt us, but He does test us. God tries us in one
sense of the word, but not the other.
       Closer to home, in Revelation, Jesus had just exhorted: “Do not fear what you are about to suffer. Behold,
the devil is about to cast some of you into prison, that you may be tested [peirazo], and you will have tribulation
ten days. Be faithful until death, and I will give you the crown of life” (Rev 2:10). In the context of Rev 2-3, church-
age believers are promised and warned that they will be tested in the present church age and that the crown of life
is at stake, depending on whether they successfully pass the present testing. The promise that they will not be
disqualified from obtaining the crown of life by the future tribulational test by no means indicates that they cannot
be disqualified from winning the crown of life by the present church-age test. Contextually, the promise that they
will not be disqualified from the crown by the future test entails the warning that they may be disqualified
from the crown by the present test. Taken in combination, this is precisely what the promise of Rev 3:10 means
in conjunction with the warning of Rev 3:11. Verse 10 is reversible in relation to the present phase of testing, not
the future phase of testing. This is exactly what one would have predicted with the LLR-SML model.233
       Unfaithful church-age believers can no more be tested by the future tribulation than they can be cast literally
into the Lake of Fire. Literarily, LLR-SML rejects both possibilities. However, LLR-SML also anticipates that just
as believers can be hurt by the Lake of Fire (as a correlative picture of the results of their being judged negatively),
so they can be hurt by the future tribulation (as a correlative picture of the present tribulation that can cause
them not to pass the judgment successfully). The LLR-SML theory successfully passes the tests put to it and has
better explanatory power than the simple SL model. The LLR-SML theory is, therefore, adopted herein as a
legitimate hermeneutical tool for digging into litotes that fall into the SML literary structure.
Implementation of the LLR-SML Theory
       The hypothesis of a correlative litotetic WYSIWYG model, in which the eternal tears are perceived as a
secondary function of the litotetic thrust, has been shown to be entirely reasonable. If the LLR-SML model is
correct, then the tears in the text that do not apply overcomers must apply to subcomers. What you see in the text is
what you will be seen in the eyes of subcomers—tears. Theses tears are not metaphorical but literal. Moreover, they
are seen in the eyes of the subcomers during the eternal state.

The options are only two: be a weeper or a ruler. You will not weep if and only if (⇔) you obtain a crown of life.
If you do not obtain it, you will weep because of your failure to do so. From LLR-SML we would expect tears in
the eternal state for those believers who fail to achieve the positive end of the spectrum. The thing being denied as
applying to overcomers, in this case tears in the eternal state, will apply to subcomers. The threat is implicit but
nevertheless very real. The text does not pose a neutral non-weeper, non-ruler possibility. Having no tears and not
weeping in Rev 21:4 are part of the things promised to the overcomers in Rev 21:7 and is thus part of the soteric-
mistholic contrast.
                                                      Rev 21:7-8
       The antithetical juxtaposition in Rev 21:7-8 is between faithful believers in verse 7 and unbelievers in
verse 8. The cowardly unbelievers in v. 8 who have their portion in the Lake of Fire are not to be interpreted as
unfaithful believers. Verse 8 is a picture of the unregenerate in the eternal state. Verse 7 is a picture of the faithful
regenerate in the eternal state. Unfaithful regenerate believers are not part of the apposition.

       Contrary to Chitwood, in terms of proper interpretation, verse 8 technically is not a metaphorical picture of

                                     Illustration 71. Rev 21:7-8 in Three Colors




        Whereas Chitwood would see Rev 21:7-8 as only being one color, yellow, that is, misthological, I perceive
it to be yellow-blue with the green misthological result. Thus, I see three colors. Verse 8 is blue (i.e., soteriological)
as it pertains to unbelievers, which is its proper interpretation, and secondarily green (i.e., misthological) as it
pertains to unfaithful believers, which is its correlative application. We both perceive a misthological understanding
Page 116                                                                                Misthological Models: Part 2
of verse 8. But, in terms of this color scheme, he sees a simple yellow misthological text; whereas, I perceive a
composite green misthological text. From this perspective I can justify a limited litotetic reversal (LLR) for this
soteric-mistholic juxtaposition of this SML text.
                                    Illustration 72. Caulian-Crawfordian Model




        In my perspective, verse 8 does not make this application to unfaithful believers directly, but indirectly, not
merely millennially, but emphatically eternally. Verse 8 is to be interpreted literally, not metaphorically. Just as
unbelievers are literally excluded from the city (in the Lake of Fire), so unfaithful believers are literally excluded
from the city (in the outer darkness). The realm of their exclusion is different, but the duration of their exclusion is
the same—forever.
        Because I derive this metaphorical application indirectly rather than directly, and secondarily to literal
interpretation, I generally prefer to refer to this secondary application as being correlative rather than metaphorical,
which helps distinguish it from other approaches, such as that by Chitwood. His direct approach has the benefit of
simplicity. But in failing to see the underlying soteric-mistholic juxtaposition, his model loses something of the
literary beauty implemented by the biblical writer. Thus, in the Caulian-Crawfordian model of Rev 21:7-8, the
literal separation (↔) of the noncomers from the city (pictured on the left) finds a correlative (≅) application to the
literal extraction (→) of the subcomers from the city (pictured on the right). Just as unbelievers are literally outside
of the city in the Lake of Fire, unfaithful believers are literally outside the city in the outer darkness.

showing the full spectrum and might even be the best illustration for a children’s object lesson. The picture on the
left describes the literal separation as pictured in Rev 21:7-8 between overcomers and noncomers. The center picture
Chapter 2. Eternal Rewards                                                                                    Page 117
shows the corresponding metaphorical application to subcomers. The picture on the right shows literal application
to subcomers. The beauty of this illustration is that it combines the strengths of each of our positions, while
maintaining technical accuracy.
                                                  Oscillating SMJ
       While I was concluding this section on antithetical polarizations and the SMJ model, my younger son,
Jonathan, asked for my input regarding Rom 2:1-16, and especially 2:7-10. He has been studying through Romans
and trying to distinguish the soteriological material from the misthological counterparts. As a science major
studying biology, his desire to approach the text scientifically—categorizing the data and dissecting as necessary—
is understandable, even desirable. He had been consulting the discussion of Romans by the FG commentator Lopez
in GNTC but found that discussion unconvincing. Lopez poses that obtaining eternal life, in the manner described
by the text, is nothing more than a mere hypothetical possibility. In terms of my color coding schema, Lopez would
regard the entire passage as simply soteriological and thus blue. Jonathan was singing the blues, but not the soteric
blues, as he was reading Lopez’s discussion. A multi-colored perspective certainly seems much more reasonable.
Simple Coloring of Rom 2:1-16

       The therefore of verse 1 may go back to God’s wrath in Rom 1:18 being expressed against all sinful humanity,
so that the verse is green in its applicability. Consequently, the judgment of God, thrice referenced in Rom 2:1-6,
certainly appears to be universal in scope, as is confirmed by the explanatory phrase of verse 6, where God will
reward every man according to his works. Believers are not excluded. The context is not limited to soteriology but
certainly encompasses misthology. Verses 7-10 show that the general principle of judgment, which is embedded in
v. 6, applies to everyone, not only the Jew and Gentile, but believers and unbelievers alike, in a judicial manner.
Put another way, the green of v. 6 is derived by the yellow-blue judicial combination of vv. 7-10. Both believers
and unbelievers will stand before God’s judgment seat.
       Verse 7 commences with an explanation as to the obtainable nature of this amazing reward. The positive
prospective of actually earning eternal life (in the form of glory, honor, and immortality) as one’s reward is set forth
Page 118                                                                                     Misthological Models: Part 2
                                                                                       234
as the obtainable goal. Obtaining this reward is a real possibility for the believer. Of course, as Paul goes on to
demonstrate, it is impossible for unjustified sinners to please God by their works. However, believers have been
justified and consequently can live lives that are pleasing to God. They can seek for glory and honor by doing good
works, and as the text goes on to state, “Glory and honor and peace will be rewarded to everyone who works what
is good” (Rom 2:10; TM). Believers can qualify for eternal life as a future reward in the form of glory and honor,
if they do the good works that are necessary (Rom 5:21).
        Dillow’s discussion of this passage is excellent (Destiny, 221-228). My color coding above appears to be in
substantial agreement with his treatment. Believing Gentiles can be justified misthologically (Rom 2:13). This
assessment is certainly in accord with other passages. As explained more fully in Darkness, the justification and
condemnation in Mt 12:37 applies soteriologically to the lost and misthologically to the saved. The principle is that
judgment, whether it results in condemnation or justification, is always based on works. Believers and nonbelievers
come into the same kind of judgment—a judicial judgment, no mere awards podium. Nevertheless, that judgment
operates in deferent spheres of planes. The lost will come into soteriological judgment and experience soteriological
condemnation. The saved will come into misthological judgment and experience either misthological condemnation
or misthological justification. An example of the latter type of judgment and justification is found in 1Cor 4:4-5.
Paul acknowledges that he is not justified by his own judgment; rather, his justification will be determined by God’s
judgment of his actions and motives. Paul affirms justification by works. He teaches a misthological justification
before God based on works.
Rom 4:2-22
     Paul also admits that Abraham experienced justification by works in Rom 4:2. “Since Abraham was justified
by works, he has something to boast about, but not before God” (TM). This is a first class conditional sentence and

234
    As explained in TOD, Paul notes that the believer may walk in newness of life (Rom 6:4). But he also warns of
the possibility of a death contingent upon moral obedience (Rom 6:16). The outcome (telos) of unrighteousness is
death (6:21). But in the same verse he presents the positive parallel: obedience results in (eis) righteousness (6:16).
With this potential positive outcome in mind, Paul urges believers to “present your members as slaves to
righteousness, resulting in (eis) sanctification” (6:19). The causal connection is clear. Moral obedience results in
righteousness, and righteousness in turn results in sanctification. And the outcome (telos) of this practical
sanctification is eternal life (6:22).
        The misthological nature of this telolic spectrum of eternal life is thus very apparent. Eternal life is the future
reward for practical sanctification. Alternatively, telolic death is the outcome for unrighteous behavior. The
parallelism between telolic life and telolic death as dual possible outcomes for the believer strongly suggests a
misthological view of this telolic death. Telolic death is the absence of telolic life. Therefore, I do not fault
Arminians and Calvinists for finding an eschatological dimension to this death. The broad context suggests this is
indeed the case. Even so, must this misthological death necessarily entail soteriological loss? Paul anticipates this
question in the next verse with a contrast not only between life and death, but also between soteriological and
misthological bestowments. With life and death for the believer hanging in the contextual balance, Paul reassures
his Christian readers, “The wages of sin is death, but the free gift of God is eternal life in Christ Jesus our Lord”
(6:23).
        Death, whether soteriological or misthological, is a wage. Eternal life, in contrast, is first and foremost a free
gift. Since it is eternal, once it is received it cannot be lost. The dynamic qualitative force of this life must not be
stressed to the detriment of its static quantitative stability. The durative nature of this life must be maintained at the
static level. Otherwise, it would not be eternal life.
        However, Paul’s emphasis in Romans is not on the fundamental nature of this life at the static level. The
other three times Paul uses the full expression eternal life in Romans, he uses it misthologically to describe the
telolic nature of this life.  “He will reward eternal life to those who seek for glory and honor and immortality by
persevering in good work (Rom 2:7; TM). Eternal life is a reward for good work.  “Grace might reign through
righteousness resulting in (eis) eternal life (Rom 5:21; TM). This verse, which is the last verse of ch. 5, introduces
the same causal link found in ch. 6. Experiential righteousness results in eternal life (6:16-20).  And as noted in
6:22, eternal life is the outcome or end result (telos) of practical sanctification. In these three verses, eternal life is
an object that is bestowed as a reward. For immortality, see footnote 201. For obtaining personal glory and honor
as our reward, see Rom 2:7,10; 5:2; 8:17-18; 2Cor 4:17; Eph 5:17; Phil 2:16; 1Thess 2:12,20; 2Thess 2:14; 2Tim
2:10; Heb 2:7,9-10; 1Pet 1:7-8; 1Pet 5:4,10.
Chapter 2. Eternal Rewards                                                                                 Page 119
assumes the reality of the premise; the if may appropriately be translated as since. James also teaches the
misthological justification of believers based on their works (Jam 2:21,24-25).
        In Rom 4:22 Paul says, “Therefore (dio) also it [faith] was reckoned to him [Abraham] as righteousness”
(Rom 4:22). According to this verse, Abraham continued to believe in order that he might obtain the realization of
the promise (Rom 4:18). Rather than become “weak in faith” (4:19) or “waver in unbelief” (4:20), he “grew strong
in faith” (4:20) so that he was “fully persuaded” (4:21). The ultimate reason his faith was “imputed to him as
righteousness” in 4:22 is because it had persevered and matured in faith. If we want our faith misthologically
imputed (logizomai) to us as righteousness in the end, we must persevere like Abraham. After all, misthological
imputation is not a foreign concept to Paul. He uses the verbal form (logizomai) misthologically in 2Tim 4:16 and
the nominal form (logos) misthologically in Phil 4:17. This cognate form for imputation is also used misthologically
on other occasions as well, even in Pauline literature.
        Jonathan concisely observed that Paul quotes Gen 15:6 in two capacities in Rom 4. First, in Rom 4:3, Paul
uses it to show that Abraham’s faith was imputed to him as righteousness soteriologically. Then, later in the context
and referencing a much later point in Abraham’s life, Abraham’s faith was imputed to him as righteousness
misthologically in Rom 4:22.235 See both Destiny and Darkness for more detailed explanations; Dillow and I concur
with this assessment. Or as James would put it, the subsequent misthological imputation of Abraham’s faith was
the fulfillment of the early soteriological imputation of his faith (Jam 2:23).

within the yellow disk. In this model the sphere is the soteric center of mass around which the judicial context
revolves with staggering misthological ramifications.
                               Illustration 80. Super Simple-Complex Color Coding




       Or yet another way to derive the super complex model from the simple model is to use a technique so simple
that a child could easily do it with two crayons: one yellow and one blue. In this crayon representation of the chart,
the bottom, baseline color is blue except for the times that the yellow is the bottom, baseline color. The blue crayon
moves to the top when the yellow crayon moves to the bottom. This supper simple-complex crayon technique has
the advantage of demonstrating that the entire chart is blue, yellow, and green. The blue crayon goes all the way
across, representing the entire chart as being blue. Likewise, the yellow crayon goes all the way across, representing
the entire chart as being blue. Additionally, the blue and yellow colors overlap throughout to produce green,
representing the full length of the chart. When Lopez sees the entire chart as blue, I can see it from that vantage
point also. But at the same time, I can see the entire chart from the yellow vantage point and then again the entire
chart from the composite green vantage point—all this with just two crayons to represent the richness of the text.

Rom 14:10-12
       The theme of Rom 2:1-16 is that the judgment of God is rendered upon all, both believers and unbelievers
alike. This passage is anticipatory of Rom 14:10-12: “But you, why do you judge your brother? Or you again, why
do you regard your brother with contempt? For we will all stand before the judgment seat of God. For it is written,
‘As I live, says the Lord, every knee will bow to Me, and every tongue will confess to God.’ So then each one of
us will give an account of himself to God” (TM). The o-man-why-do-you-judge question of Rom 2 paves the way
for the o-Christian-why-do-you-judge question in Rom 14, where it is meet with this response: “Therefore let us
not judge one another anymore” (Rom 14:13). Believers, as part of humanity, are going to be judged by God.
Consequently, neither humanity in general nor believers in particular need to try to play God in the meantime by
judging one another in a manner what would usurp the judicial judgment to which all will be subjected before God
in the end. Everybody is going to be judged before God in a judicial manner, and that judicial judgment certainly
includes believers.
                                  Illustration 81. The Bema is a Judicial Subset




       The Bema of Christ for believers in Rom 14:10 is but a subset of the accountability which the entire human
race must render to God as each human being stands before God in judgment (Bema ⊂ judgment). This is not to
say that every human being will be judged at the same time or that believers must necessarily be judged at the same
time as unbelievers. What it does demonstrate, however, is that the Bema partakes of the judicial nature of the
judgment rendered in Romans. The world will not stand on God’s awards podium to hear His voice of praise for
their sins! The Bema is not an awards podium in this context. Everyone, lost and saved, will be judged judicially.
For the lost, this judgment will focus on the soteriological outcome. For the saved, this judgment will be limited to
Chapter 2. Eternal Rewards                                                                                      Page 123
the misthological sphere. In Illustration 81, the broader blue strips are soteriological. The narrower yellow strips
are misthological. Within this larger circle of yellow and blue strips, resides a smaller sphere that is composed
exclusively of yellow misthological material.
                    Illustration 82. Misthological Semantical Sphere within a Judicial Context




       Or if one were to turn the illustration sideways, as in Illustration 82, the three-dimensional nature might be
easier to grasp. The sphere represents the Bema judgment where God judges believers. The judicial context of
Rom 14 is pictured as the disk round about this Bema judgment of believers. In this three-dimensional view, I have
not submerged the misthological sphere within the judicial dimension. While completely immersing the Bema
within a judicial semantic range for what judgment means would be preferable for what it means in Romans, I am
leaving part of the sphere out of the judicial range for the sake of passages outside of Romans. This protruding
yellow misthological material might be gleaned from non-judicial elements in other texts that focus on the athletic
imagery of the Olympian Games, which Paul uses in other epistles.
       After all, the judicial understanding of the nature of the Bema does not entail taking the Bema as exclusively
judicial in every misthological context in every epistle. Rather, the judicial element is present within certain
misthological contexts. The sphere for the semantic range, as to what the Bema entails for believers, draws its source
from both judicial and non-judicial texts. But as seen in a comparison of Rom 2:1-16 with Rom 14:10-12, the
judgment of God has blue soteriological applicability to unbelievers and yellow misthological applicability to
believers and therefore is strongly judicial in nature. In this particular illustration, picturing the athletic imagery
versus the judicial element for the semantic range of the Bema, the part of the sphere within the disk represents the
judicial emphasis on the nature of this judgment found in Romans, while the part of the sphere protruding out of
the disk represents the athletic imagery as applied to believers in some contexts outside of Romans. The green
portion of the sphere (namely, that part of the sphere found inside the 3D disk) represents the judicial nature of the
Bema as applied to believers. The soteric blue of the disk combines with misthological yellow of the sphere to
produce green. The yellow part of the sphere outside of the disk does not have the soteric-judicial filter and is thus
pure yellow, representing being rewarded as an athlete rather than judged in a court of law. Those believers who
think that they will be merely awarded, not judged, at the Bema have severely misunderstood the nature of the
Bema. The Bema is not merely athletic yellow but most emphatically judicially green! Unbelievers who fail this
judgment are cast into Hell, and believers who fail are cast correlatively into the outer darkness.
                                                      Conclusion
       Various weaknesses in Chitwood’s arguments against eternal rewards have been demonstrated. He has failed
to prove that the Gentile nations who live until the end of the millennium will co-rule with Christ. To the contrary,
they will in large part rebel at the postmillennial battle of Gog and Magog. His typology backfires in suggesting a
universal marriage bed. Monogamy, not polygamy, should be expected typologically. His proposal that unfaithful
believers are only hurt by the Lake of Fire for a thousand years is counter intuitive to his metaphorical application
of this punishment to unfaithful believers. His deduction that rewards cease if all forms of death cease is correct.
However, the second death does not cease. Therefore, one should suppose that rewards do not cease. The second
death is correlatively applicable to subcomers. The promise to overcomers that they would not be hurt by the second
death is misthologically limited to overcomers. Revisions in the present work to my original model in TOD now
acknowledge that the biblical text intends a partial reversion of the litotetic promise so that it implies a misthological
Page 124                                                                             Misthological Models: Part 2
threat to those believers who fail to overcome. The misthological correlation is intended to be made via limited
litotetic inversion. This observation was shown to be true in all cases of soteric-mistholic litotes. Throughout
eternity, the positive promises made to overcomers will not apply to subcomers.
        In the previous chapter, the problem of contemporary FG antinomians (antibemians) was noted. They are
not experiential antinomians in that they do not teach that believers should live anyway they please. They call for
personal holiness in the believer’s present experience. Nevertheless, they undercut the reason for living morally by
saying that the Bema is nothing more than an awards podium or an educational experience. They are misthological
antinomians. They severely diminish the doctrine of rewards as an impetus for holy living. To a lesser extent,
nevertheless to some extent, the same charge can be leveled at Chitwood. He discourages holy living by teaching
that rewards do not matter after a thousand years. To the degree he makes and teaches that mistaken limitation, he
is a misthological antinomian. To a lesser degree, nevertheless to some degree, some misthologists, like Dillow,
acknowledge the objective reality of eternal rewards but diminish their subjective importance so as to conclude that
if you do not live for Christ, you will not regret it in eternity. To the degree they give the false impression that
subcomers will not regret not living for the Lord, they are being misthological antinomians as well. Hearing the
Lord say, “Well done,” becomes increasing problematic to the degree one minimizes the biblical emphasis on
rewards and their eternal objective and subjective repercussions.
                              Chapter. 3 Least and Greatest
                                                    Introduction
       A natural question arises as to being called least in the kingdom in Mt 5:19 and being least in kingdom in
Mt 11:11. One of the books I was reading at the time I was working on this material was a book on logic that Nick,
a coworker, had recommended. We work in the computer industry. Nick was a philosophy major in college; I was
a math major. I described the problems of interacting with multiple variables within a text with Nick, who promptly
suggested that fuzzy logic would be the better tool over binary logic for the theory I am posing. The light did not
click for me immediately, though, at the time he suggested it anyway, perhaps because Nick prefers truth tables and
I prefer diagrams. Although not limiting my illustrations to classical Venn diagrams, I felt that my illustrations were
sufficient for the task at hand.
                                             Binary versus Fuzzy Logic
       Within a week or so after his suggestion, however, I received an email from Martha Lever inquiring about
Mt 11:11. The synchronicity was most interesting because I had just been discussing this very text at length with
Scott Crawford during this same time period. I preferred one interpretation, Scott preferred another, and Martha
suggested an attractive third possibility. If it were simply a matter of choosing one interpretation over another, I
might not have been attracted to Nick’s proposition of fuzzy logic. But given three attractive possibilities and
anticipating that Jesus might have intended all three vantage points, the fuzzy-logic summation became attractive
immediately. My hypothesis is that occasionally Jesus purposefully uses fuzzy logic to give a range of meaning to
the text. He masterfully uses ambiguity to allow legitimate multiplicity.
Binary
        Binary logic is the classical two-valued logic in which the values of the variables are either true (1) or false
(0). If something is true, it is assigned a truth value of 1. If it is false, the truth value is 0. For instance, the Bible
promises eternal life to all who believe in Jesus for eternal life as a free gift. Therefore, if (A) you believe in Christ
for the gift of eternal life, then (B) you have eternal life (A  B). If A is true (1), then B is true (1) also. Jesus is
speaking in binary terms. You either have believed in Him, or you have not. As a result, you either have the gift of
eternal life, or you do not. You cannot partially believe in Jesus and partially believe in your performance for eternal
life and expect to receive eternal life as a free gift. You must believe exclusively in Jesus for nothing less than
eternal life as a free gift.
                                        Illustration 83. Binary Light Switches




       Light switches are a good way to illustrate basic binary and fuzzy options. As to binary options, the off
position can be used to represent a negative answer, such as no or false (with a binary value of 0). The on position
represents a positive answer, such as yes or true (with a binary value of 1). “Will you enter the kingdom?” is a
soteric-binary question, for example, that is answerable with a no or yes. Thus, it can be represented with off-on
Page 126                                                                                Misthological Models: Part 2
blue light switches. On the other hand, “Will you enter the kingship?” is a mistholic-binary question that can be
answered with corresponding yellow off-on switches (Acts 14:22). You will either rule in Heaven, or you will not.
Fuzzy
       Yet binary terms are not the only manner in which Jesus describes the life He offers. In other capacities, this
life can be had (i.e., experienced) in degrees. Jesus said that He came that we might have life (i.e., in a binary
capacity) and have it abundantly (i.e., in a degreed capacity). Abundantly is a fuzzy term, a term associated with
fuzzy logic. Speaking in logical terms, Jesus offers us both binary and fuzzy life.
       Fuzzy logic is as a form of many-valued logic with truth values that range between 0 and 1. In other words,
the value is on a scale ranging from zero to one. Consider the question, “Misthologically speaking, are you an
overcomer?” Some believers are more of overcomers than other believers, experientially speaking. The question
might be answered by posing that all misthological overcomers get at least a score of 0.51 on a fuzzy scale between
0 and 1. With a numeric value of 0.50, the answer to the question is more true than false.236 Unless the answer to
the misthological question is more true than false using this scale, then you are not considered an overcomer in the
mistholic sense of the word and, therefore, subject to the outer darkness.
                                        Illustration 84. Fuzzy Light Switches




       When it comes to fuzzy (i.e., quantity) options, a yellow radial dimmer would be a good way to represent
degrees of rulership. Rulership would be any value greater than 0 on this yellow scale. For example, Peter poses the
possibility of faithful believers achieving an abundant entrance into the kingdom: “For in this way [i.e., by being
faithful] the entrance into the eternal kingdom of our Lord and Savior Jesus Christ will be abundantly supplied to
you” (2Pet 1:11). All believers will enter the kingdom, but not all believers will enter the kingdom abundantly. An
abundant entrance into the kingdom might range anywhere from a 0.01 to 1.0 entrance into the kinship. Yet just as
there are degrees of reward in Heaven, there are various degrees of punishment in Hell. And just as there are degrees
of punishment in Hell, so there are degrees of punishment in the outer darkness. So three radial buttons would be
logical considerations for appropriate contexts. In the illustration the color gets darker for the respective spectrum
of the bar as the value increases.
                                       Illustration 85. Binary-Fuzzy Slide bar




     Additionally, to deal with the full misthological spectrum, and thus cover both the negative and positive
dimensions of the misthological data, we need a slide bar spanning that joint mistholic range. For sake of illustration,
236
   And even that fuzzy answer is relative. See theory of relativity as it applies to the misthological spectrum
(Darkness, 691).
Chapter 3. Least and Greatest                                                                                 Page 127
I am assigning abundant entrance a value of 0.51 or higher on this slide-bar scale of 0-1. But since a sharp categorical
distinction is being made between the two possibilities, the binary perspective still comes into play. As seen in these
two illustrations, Jesus’ fuzzy (i.e., degree-oriented) statement about being least versus greatest in the kingdom
could be understood in two different capacities. On the one hand, it could refer to being least in the soteriological
kingdom. The slide bar addresses this possibility. On the other hand, being least could refer to being least in the
regal kingship. The radial button pictures this potentiality.237
                                       Illustration 86. Binary-Fuzzy Slide bar




        To help visualize and personalize this spectrum, a new personality will be introduced: Lex the Least. Relative
to this illustration, he is the lowest ranking kingdom ruler, who just barely qualifies for kingdom rulership. He is
least in the kingship, but at least he is in the kingship. On the radial dial he would score 0.01 in the misthological
region; on the sliding scale, he scores 0.51 on the joint soteric-mistholic sliding scale. We already know about
Lester the Least. Saved by the blood of the Lamb, Lester is least in the kingdom, but at least he is in the kingdom—
by the skin of his teeth, so to speak. And we have been introduced to Gregory the Great (or Greg for short). Gregory
is the greatest because he did great on the Bema test. So when Jesus cryptically refers to someone who is “least” in
the basileia as being greater than John the Baptist, who is Jesus referring to as being least—Lester or Lex? Fuzzy
logic helps formulate the question. Another feature of fuzzy logic, its mufti-valued aspect, helps put the answer in
proper perspective. Fuzzy logic is a multi-valued logic system that can accommodate multiple variables.

                                              Least is Lex after the Bema
        Martha Lever, however, suggests another possibility that, like Scott’s interpretation, is also in harmony with
my misthological ranking of kingdom in Mt 11:11 (see Darkness, 765). In this interpretation, those least in the
kingdom refers to the lowest ranking rulers. Those who are least (in ranking in the future ruling echelon of the
kingdom) will still be greater than John the Baptist is in any present time period. Notwithstanding how great a
prophet John the Baptist was at the time Jesus spoke this enigmatic saying, Jesus was speaking to them in the
present.242 Kingship would be a good translation for basileia in that case. Those who are least in the future kingship
are greater than even John the Baptist was during his earthly ministry. No role that even the greatest of us are
fulfilling now will be anywhere as near as fulfilling as the least fulfilling role that the ruling faithful will exercise
then. This perspective takes kingship regally to be futuristically exclusive of unfaithful believers. Subcomers will
be in the kingdom but excluded from the kingship. From this perspective, Jesus is not referring to Lester but to Lex
when He refers to the least in the kingdom. Restated in terms of variables, T = future and K = kingship; therefore,
L = Lex.243
        Jesus appears to be using fuzzy logic to allow a range of possibilities but not every possibility. The TV (truth
value) for a believer exercising present rulership is considered false (0). But the other three values are true (1); that
is, the other three interpretations are reasonable, each of which plausibly may be intended by Jesus. For the sake of
the present discussion, an equal truth value for each of these three interpretations suffices. So all three true
statements are given the highest possible value: 1. Likewise, the false value is given a 0; no truth value is ascribed
to it. That interpretation is flat out false.

        In certain passages, however, one interpretation may be regarded as truer than the others. Fuzzy logic can
assist in that relative ranking. In such a case, one interpretation would be regarded as primary while the others would
be secondary. For example, in Mt 5:19-20 only two possibilities were regarded viable. This table needs more
variables: Let E = enter (with the values in its respective column designating the type of entrance into the basileia
being described in v. 20), and R = righteousness (which is either soteriologically imputed or experientially
imparted).
        The first interpretation on the chart interprets kingdom futuristically. The pseudo Lordship Salvationist
interpretation, which conditions future entrance into the kingdom on one’s experiential righteousness, must be
discounted completely as having no validity whatsoever, therefore, having a value of false 0.0. This interpretation
is not only false, but deadly, since it teaches a false gospel. Despite its popularity, kingdom teachers wisely steer
clear of this false gospel.
     The next interpretation on the chart interprets basileia temporally. Strangely, Dillow denies that the kingdom
is present (inaugurated), yet he allows a present experiential entrance into the kingdom way of living and takes the
“kingdom program” as suffering present violence in Mt 11:12 (Destiny, 303).244 His not allowing a present
inaugurated value to the kingdom seems to be at odds with his allowing both present experiential value to entrance
into the kingdom and the present suffering of the kingdom program. Even from Dillow’s own analysis, the kingdom
does seem to be present in some capacities. Despite his straining his argument by denying that the kingdom is
present, while at the same time trying to maintain that it is entered presently, assigning a positive true value (i.e., a
TV > 0.51) to this present entering the kingdom is possible with an adjustment to his argument.
        If entering the kingdom in Mt 5:20 actually refers to a kingdom way of life presently, then a reverse logical
order would be expected. Entering this way of life would be the means, not the result, for having an experiential
righteousness that exceeds the scribes and Pharisees. Nonetheless, the Dillowian argument for a present basileia
need not be assigned a negative truth value (i.e., a TV< 0.51) because his argument may be amended to suggest that
when a present basileia experience is the vantage point intended in this passage, then such entrance is referring
simply to a present experience of eternal life, namely, abundant life. In order to experience abundant life presently,
we must live obediently. Abundant life in the present is the result of having a high degree of experiential
righteousness. Eternal life is knowing God (Jn 17:3). Those believers who obey God know Him more intimately
(Jn 14:23), and thus experience life more abundantly, than those believers who do not. This tweak to Dillow’s
argument allows a positive outcome on the table with a TV of 0.6.
        Entrance into the kingdom is predominantly future in the NT, but even in my perception Jn 3:5 is an obvious
exception. This passage is decidedly soteriological and probably referring to present soteriological entrance into
the kingdom. However, if one can stress the soteriological nature of this present entrance with sufficient clarity
while allowing other secondary possibilities, then perhaps one might even allow that this verse sees entrance from
multiple vantage points. For the believer, the present entrance into the basileia realm in Jn 3:5 is not only
unconditional soteriologically but also contingently experiential in the present and conditionally regal in the future.
Thus, in deference to the scholarship of Dillow and France and for the sake of Jn 10:10, a positive truth value to the
present-experiential interpretation for Mt 5:20 is allowable. The kingdom (way of) life can be experienced presently
in a limited capacity in that we can have intimacy with the King. Put in Lester-Lex terms, Lester is presently
regarded as least in the basileia realm from God’s vantage point because his teaching and doctrine are not sufficient
to enter into basileia intimacy with God. Only kingdom teachers and followers can have kingdom intimacy with
the Father. Why think that those believers who reject the kingdom message will have kingdom fellowship with the
King?
        The two remaining futuristic interpretations, nevertheless, are giving higher ratings than the temporal
interpretation because the emphasis on entrance into the basileia appears to be principally eschatological in the NT.
Whatever taste of kingship experienced presently in terms of fellowship with the King hardly compares with what
will be experienced in the future. One of these superlative futuristic interpretations is primary in its focus and is
given the highest rating—true 1.0—as compared to the secondary understanding that is given a value of true 0.9.
The two interpretations complement one another nicely.
        From Lester’s vantage point, unbelievers do not have binary soteriological righteousness necessary to enter
the kingdom of Heaven at all and thus will be cast into Hell. Accordingly, Lester will be thankful to have made it
244
   Dillow takes least as being misthologically future in Mt 11:11 (Destiny, 967). Thus, he collectively interprets
the kingdom in Mt 11:11-12 as being both present and future. Yet a present-future kingdom certainly sounds
inaugurated.

at least into the kingdom, even though he did not qualify to be a kingdom ruler. Alternatively, viewed from Lex’s
experience, those believers who do not have sufficient fuzzy, present, experiential righteousness to qualify for even
the lowest degree of kingship will not enter the kingship at all and thus be cast into the outer darkness. Lex will be
thankful to have entered the kingship at least, even though as the lowest ranking kingdom ruler.

                                            Lester is Judged at the Bema
       This tentative allowance of a misthological interpretation of Jn 3:5 above from a neoclassical perspective
walks a tightrope with my “Warning to Misthologists” in Darkness (pp. 958-959) against a neoclassical
interpretation of Jn 3:5. Because the present work has advanced in ability to delineate and illustrate the soteriological
and misthological distinctions within a singular passage, a provisionary neoclassical approach is being permitted.
Additionally, the scope of the context has been shown to play a key role in a number of passages as to the number
of vantage points intended by the text.245 This phenomenon is especially pronounced in the case of Jn 5:28-29, for
example.
                                      Illustration 89. Truth Table for Jn 5:28-29

                C          G               E                RL                     RJ                  TV
                GJ Good       Evil       Deeds not a factor Deeds not a factor True                          1.0
                NT Good deeds Evil deeds Deeds are a factor Deeds are a factor True                          0.9
                  C = context   G = good   E = evil   RL = resurrection of life   RJ = resurrection of judgment

        28
          Do not marvel at this; for an hour is coming, in which all who are in the tombs shall hear His voice,
        29

        committed the evil [deeds (if C = NT)] to a resurrection of judgment. (Jn 5:28-29)

      In both Jn 3:5 and Jn 5:28-29, the range of the context (C) is a particularly significant fuzzy value necessary
to generate the secondary vantage points. Whether or not the NAS is justified in inserting the reference to deeds for
good versus evil is depending upon the scope of the context considered. Is the context limited to the Gospel of John
(GJ) or the Synoptic Gospels (SG) or expanded to include the whole New Testament (NT)? In the following
equations, let N = neoclassical and S = soteriological.

       For Jn 5:28-29, if C = NT, then Jn 5:28-29 is neoclassical; otherwise, it is soteriological.
         (C = NT)  N.
         (C = GJ)  S.
       For Jn 3:5, if C = GJ + SG, then Jn 3:5 is neoclassical; otherwise, it is soteriological.
         (C = GJ + SG)  N.
         (C = GJ)  S.

When truth is relative to the context, the scope of the context plays a critical factor as a fuzzy variable in determining
the relative truth value intended. Within the limited scope of GJ, Lester is not judged in Jn 5:28-29. This passage is
simply a modest expansion of Jn 5:24 and thus soteriological. The promise of no judgment for believers in Jn 5:24
is paired with a judgment for unbelievers Jn 5:28-29. Within the larger scope of the NT, however, Lester is judged
implicitly in Jn 5:28-29. But one has to have the broader context to insert an implicit reference to deeds/works.
Then one has to observe the function of soteric-mistholic juxtapositions in the NT. The ability of John to produce a
literary masterpiece that can communicate at different levels, depending on the depth perception of his readers,
should not be underestimated. Similar arguments might be made about Jn 3:5.




245
   For example, the perspective adopted herein is that John may not have intended his readership to see that Lester
could be hurt misthologically by the second death in Rev 2:11 until they have read the fuller context and reached
the end of Revelation. On a microcosmic scale, Mt 7:15-23 may be perceived as simply soteric but Mt 7:13-27 as
soteric-mistholic. The immediate context provides the secondary misthological lens for the soteric component.
Chapter 3. Least and Greatest                                                                                Page 131
                                       Illustration 90. Truth Table for Jn 3:5

                               C               W                    E             TV
                           GJ       Spiritual Birth        Soteriological True 1.0
                           GJ + SG Misthological Birth Misthological True 0.6
                           W = water                E = enter           K = kingdom

       First and foremost, Jn 3:5 is definitely a go-to-heaven-when-I-die type of passage: “Jesus answered, ‘Truly,
truly, I say to you, unless one is born of water and the Spirit, he cannot enter into the kingdom of God.’”
Soteriological entrance into the kingdom is conditioned on new birth. This soteriological perspective must not be
confused or compromised by additional vantage points. As a further safeguard against compromising the
soteriological integrity of this text, a misthologist who feels compelled to allow a secondary vantage point to Jn 3:5
might acknowledge that, although John lays the framework for allowing a potential misthological lens over this
soteriological text in the broader context of GJ, he does not develop that vantage point within this narrow context
of ch. 3. Rather, he makes that vantage point contingent upon a broader context, probably requiring at least the
Synoptic Gospels (SG) for its justification.
    If so, within the limited context of the Gospel of John (GJ), Jn 3:5 may be interpreted differently than when
interpreted in the broader context of the NT. In the limited context of GJ, a misthological understanding of Jn 3:5
is probably lacking sufficient justification even for a secondary orientation. In the broader New Testament (NT)
context, however, where other passages are able to assist in generating a secondary misthological lens over the
passage, one is potentially justified in entertaining both experiential and misthological dimensions. Without the
soteriological-misthological discussions found in the broader context of the NT, however, or at least without SG,
Jn 3:5 should be interpreted solely soteriologically.
    As to what misthological birth might refer to, various options exist. Water baptism marks the birth of a Christian,
where Christian is defined as a public follower of Christ. The believer experiences soteriological new birth at the
point of saving faith. Subsequently, in taking the step of baptism, the believer becomes a Christian, a public follower
of Christ. Water baptism thus might be seen as a new birth experience—from a misthological point of view. Israel
passing through the Red Sea was their baptism into their march to the Promised Land, the land of reward. So that
imagery might be intended also. Then again, just as Eve was formed from Adam’s side to co-rule with Him, blood
and water flowed from Jesus’ side in the formation of His Bride (Jn 19:34). His Bride has been redeemed by His
blood and born by His water for the purpose of ruling with Him.246 In GJ no less, John will go on to show that
believers must be washed by Jesus if they are going to have a misthological part with Him (Jn 13:8)—in the rulership
of the coming kingdom. Paul also talks about Jesus performing a misthological washing of His Bride (Eph 5:26).
Isaiah talks about being washed eschatologically for misthological life in Jerusalem (Is 4:3-4).
                                   Illustration 91. The Duality of Kingdom Birth




    That being born again has a misthological scope is rather apparent in 1Pet 1:3-5, though the exact nature of that
misthological dimension is open for discussion. One possibility might be to regard this passage as describing a

246
   Adam typifies Christ. Through the historical account of Adam, Christ is telling us His story. Christ suffered and
died for the church so that we might achieve misthological maturity as the Bride of Christ. God did not bring a little
girl to Adam to become his wife, but a mature woman. Christ was looking for a suitable, mature companion for
Himself. The animals did not qualify for Adam, and the angels could not qualify for Christ. They were not born of
the water and blood that came from His side. To create a Bride, His side had to be opened in His sacrifice.
Page 132                                                                                  Misthological Models: Part 2
misthological new birth if Peter is saying that we are (A) born again to a living hope (B) through the resurrection
of Christ.247 Everything necessary for our salvation from hell was accomplished by Christ’s death, as denoted by
Christ’s shout, “It is finished” (Jn 19:30). Yet this Petrine new birth into the living-hope dimension of the kingdom
is accomplished through the subsequent act of the resurrection of Christ. Linking this dimension of the new birth
with the living Lord via His resurrection naturally associates it with the believer’s living hope.248 This living hope
is misthological, as Peter makes clear (1Pet 1:4-13). But even if this is the correct syntactical perspective, restricting
this new birth to the misthological arena would be unwise, as Peter will proceed to discuss (1) our redemption
through the blood of Christ and (2) being born again by imperishable seed, namely, the living word of God
(1Pet 1:18-15), which is strictly soteriological. Peter, therefore, appears to be looking at the new birth dualistically.
Foremost in importance for the point Peter is making, the new birth puts believers in a misthological dimension
where they can qualify for the misthological hope, but Peter does not fail to include the soteriological dimension of
the new birth. James, using the logical order, will speak first of the new birth as a gift, and thus soteriological, while
secondarily pointing out that this new birth has the potential of saving our souls, which is misthological (Jam 1:17-
21).
     As to the other syntactical possibility, if Peter is saying that we are born again (A) to a living hope (B) through
the resurrection of Christ, so that the only part of the proposition accomplished through part B is the resulting living
hope, which seems more likely, then the new birth is still seen as the entry point into the misthological possibility
of acquiring this misthological hope. We would not refer to this as a misthological new birth, per se, but as a new
birth into the misthological potentiality. Regardless as to which construction is grammatically correct, this latter
articulation is probably best. The new birth is neither a work that we perform to acquire rewards nor is it a reward.
The new birth itself is a gift; it is not misthological. However, it is the soteriological means that God uses to place
us in the misthological sphere where we can qualify for rewards, and in this sense the new birth may be placed in
the misthological dimension of the graph. Our soteriological new birth has tremendous misthological potential.
                                 Illustration 92. The Duality of Kingdom Entrance




    New Birth by the Sovereign Wind (pp. 51-54) entertains the plausible soteric-mistholic duality for seeing the
kingdom in Jn 3:3. Even though that discussion did not allow this dual possibility for entering the kingdom in
Jn 3:5, the duality proposed herein for enter is, nevertheless, supported by that earlier treatment to some extent.
Both treatments approach the dual notion in this passage with extreme caution for good reason. To avoid the
misthological curse imposed by Darkness for entertaining a dualistic entrance in Jn 3:5, several safeguards are being
implemented herein.
    First, Jn 3:5 is emphatically affirmed as being a go-to-heaven-when-I-die type passage. This is the fundamental
understanding of the passage. Second, this soteriological perspective is the only understanding being considered
within the confines of the GJ context. Third, in these illustrations, the misthological understanding is clearly
portrayed as being over and beyond the basic soteriological foundation. Fourth, the misthological value for
misthological birth and misthological entrance is only given a fuzzy value of 0.6 in Illustration 90, which is weak

                                        Lester does (not?) enter the Kingdom
    The truth table for Mt 7:13-29 has more variables to consider, in part because of the larger scope of the passage.
Owing to the increased complexity, color coding the various cells has been implemented. When seen in blocks of
color, the chart is rather easy to decipher.
                                             Illustration 93. Truth Table for Mt 7:13-29

  Writer G                         P              F             T L                K         W D                M            TV

SG = Singular         Pl = Plural          Sot = Soteriological         Mist = Misthological          W= words         WW= words & works

       Chitwood takes the passage as simply misthological. Thus, the entire first row is yellow for all the variables.
Indeed, the neoclassical position concedes that the entire passage is intended to have yellow (i.e., misthological)
applicability, but much of that applicability is secondary and even sometimes remote. Therefore, although a degree
of truth must be acknowledged in Chitwood’s interpretation by the neoclassical approach taken herein, overall his
thoroughgoing yellow misthology must be rated as failing: falling not only within the false range with a value below
0.5 but within the lower spectrum of that range because his misthology rules out the soteriological interpretation,
which is the primary application. Thus, the Chitwoodian view only rates a 0.2 because it only affirms the truth value
of the secondary application.
       Dillow’s approach takes Mt 7:15-20 section as soteriological, but the rest of the passage is taken as
misthological, as denoted by the yellow versus blue cells. His view is a mediating view between Chitwood’s view
and my classical view, which was my predominant view in Darkness. My original approach expressed in Darkness
was primarily to take an exclusively soteriological understanding of the entire passage—other than the last pericope,
in which the parable of the wise man and foolish man, building their houses on the rock and sand respectively, was
applied to both soteriological and misthological issues. Thus, my original view (the classical view) is soteric blue
throughout the entire passage until one reaches the last pericope, which is neoclassical green, applying both to
believers and unbelievers.
       In the present work, however, which adopts a neoclassical view, a thoroughgoing duality of soteriology and
misthology is allowed throughout the passage’s entirety. Thus, the entire neoclassical row is green.250 Dillow’s
position thus serves not only as a mediating position between Chitwood’s view and my classical view, but also as
a stepping stone to the neoclassical synthesis. The passage has dual applicability. On the one hand, Lester will enter
the kingdom; on the other hand, he will not enter the kingship.
       One advantage of the neoclassical position is that it adopts the competing strengths of the alternate views.
For example, Dillow has switched from taking the fruit in the context from referring exclusively to words (so Reign)
to include works (in Destiny), though he is doing so in a soteriological context. Note that part of the material is still
in blue in his row. But determining if someone is a false teacher soteriologically based on his or her works is
counterintuitive to a soteriology that teaches that works are not a contributing factor in the soteriological arena! For
such reasons, I maintain my classic insistence that when the passage is viewed soteriologically, the plural fruits
should be limited to words and the singular fruit refers to saving faith. At the same time, though, neoclassically I

249
    Misthological birth can be confirmed via other means. The coronation of a king is described as being begotten
as God’s firstborn (Ps 2:7-8; 89:27; Heb 1:5; 5:5). We will be begotten misthologically the day we become rulers.

    Nevertheless, a shortcoming of this truth chart is that it fails to distinguish as to whether the soteric or mistholic
vantage point is primary in each cell. Equal soteric-mistholic applicability should not be assumed in each pericope.

can now accommodate a words-works interpretation for fruits when looking at the passage through the
misthological lens. Naturally, when judging someone misthologically, one is going to include his or her works!
After all, judgment is according to works. Works, logically and naturally, therefore, may be included or excluded
in or from the frame of reference, respectively, according to the dictates of the vantage point being taken when
examining the fruits. Neoclassically, one can have one’s cake and eat it too, hermeneutically.
       Another advantage of the neoclassical position over the classical position is that a green conclusion would
be more readily explained by green cells in its row leading up to that conclusion. In other words, it is a little odd
that the classical position ends with a conclusion that is applicable to unbelievers and believers alike when it
interprets the material leading up to that conclusion as applying exclusively to unbelievers. The joint applicability
of the conclusion points to a joint application of the preceding material and thus to a duality.251
       In summary, the first row takes the passage as being exclusively misthological. Therefore, the details are
used exclusively in a misthological manner. The gate (G) is misthological, so entrance via the narrow versus broad
gate is limited to misthological themes. The false prophets (P) represent genuine believers. The fruit (F) is plural
(Pl) and refers to both their words and works (WW). The tree (T) good verses bad fruit refers to good versus bad
believers. The Lord (L) is dealing exclusively with misthological issues. Entrance into the kingship (K) is
misthological. The will (W) of the Father is a kingdom way of living. They are told to depart (D) from the Lord
misthologically into the outer darkness. The wise versus foolish man (M) refers to believers living wisely or
foolishly in regard to laying up treasures in Heaven. The second row departs from the exclusively misthological
view by taking the false prophets (P) and fruit (F) as soteriological. Thus, only these three cells are highlighted in
blue. The third row expands the soteriological perspective to the entire row, only allowing a misthological overlap
in the last cell. The fourth row allows a soteric-mistholic overlap forever cell.
                                                      Conclusion
       FG writers differ among themselves as to how much of this section from the Sermon on the Mount is
soteriological versus misthological. Summarizing in terms of the rows in the chart, (1) some say that the passage is
exclusively misthological; (2) others say that it is almost exclusively soteriological; (3) others say that some parts
are soteriological while other parts are misthological; (4) others say that the entire passage may be seen dually from
both vantage points.
       In TOD, I preferred the third option, but now my preference has migrated to the fourth option. The migration
to duality steers clear of the LS mentality. The FG distinction between a gift and a reward is still maintained. The
passage is not making a gift out of the reward or a reward out of the gift. Instead, it implements imagery that can
be used to illustrate both the gift aspect and reward aspect of the kingdom message. Thresholds can be pictured in
binary terms: You are either in the kingdom, or you are not. You either rule in the kingdom, or you do not. The
range between the thresholds can be pictured in terms of fuzzy logic via a spectrum running from least to greatest.
       Four possibilities pertaining to four types of people are discernable: (1) Unbelievers will not enter the
kingdom; all believers will enter the kingdom. (2) Unfaithful believers will be least in the kingdom and have no
position of rulership. (3) Halfhearted faithful believers will have the lowest positions of rulership. (4) Wholehearted
faithful believers will have greater positions of rulership.252
       In a very good article dealing similarly with these four types of people, Wilkin appropriately comments
regarding the third type: “I am encouraged greatly by the fact that even if the Lord finds that I was not wholehearted
in my service, I can still reign with Christ. Now I long for his ‘Well done, good servant.’ But it’s good to know that
ruling with Christ is not all or nothing. Actually, there is a huge allowance for failure.”253


251
    An alternative method to derive a green result from preceding blue and yellow cells in that row would have been
to take Dillow’s approach and change the last cell, the conclusion, to green. If I had added such a row, I would have
ranked it with a TV of 0.8 and dropped Dillow’s unmodified approach to a 0.7.

    This summary is referring to the future kingdom. Allowance has been made herein that unbelievers may enter
the mystery form of the kingdom and will be removed from the multiverse form of the kingdom.
